RussmAN FEDERATION
AGRicuLTuRAL REFOpm ImPLEmENTATiON SUPPORT (ARIS) PRojECT
LOAN AND PROJECT SUMMARY
Borrower:          The Russian Federation
Beneficiares:      Ministry of Agriculture and Food (MOAF) and participating private seed and
marketing enterprises.
Amownt:             US$240 million equivalent
Terms:             17 years, including 5 years grace, at the Bank's standard variable interest rate.
On-lding Terms:   The proceeds of the loan would be channelled in two ways: First, funds to the
government agencies involved in the project would be channelled through the
budgetary process. Second, funds to participating private seed and marketing
enterprises would be on-lent in US dollars by the Government through
commercial banks on an agency basis. The sub-loans would have a repayment
period of 10 years, including 2 years grace. The on-lending interest rate would
be equal to the Bank's standard variable interest rate (the base rate) plus a margin
of 2.5 percent. The Government would bear the default risk and the cross-
currency risk; and the sub-borrowers would bear the foreign exchange risk
between the US dollar and the ruble.
Project Objectives:   The main objectives of the project are: (a) to create and strengthen the most
critical agricultural support services to accelerate the implementation of ongoing
and future agricultural reforms; lb) to build analytical and institutional capacity
to identify, appraise and implement agricultural and agro-business projects by the
private sector on the basis of economic criteria; and (c) to demonstrate the role
that the private sector can play in promoting efficiency in the agro-industrial
sector. The support services would focus on: (i) the creation of an enabling
policy environment for private sector participation in seed and market
development; (ii) establishment of a nationwide market information system; and
(iii) establishment of fanrmer information and advisory services. The role of the
private sector would be demonstrated by selective "pilot" investments in hybrid
corn and sunflower seed enterprises, fruit and vegetable marketing bases and
vegetable storage facilities. The provision of critical support services, the
development of analytical capacity and demonstration of the commercial viability
of participating private enterprises are expected to lay the foundation for an
efficient agricultural sector based on private ownership and market principles.



viii
Projed Deseiption:  The I -oject would support the following components:
(a)    Market infrastructure development consists of the creation of a market
infonnation system 'MIS), pilot investments in market infrastructure and
institutional development. The MIS will be a nation-wide system to collect,
process and disseminate market information on about 50 agricultural commodities
and inputs. The pilot investments will consist of establishing, upgrading and
modernizing marketing enterprises (fruit and vegetable bases, mini-wholesale
markets and vegetable storage facilities). These facilities will be owned and
operated by participating private enterprises. The main focus of this component
is to support infrastructure and institutions to demonstrate the positive role of
private sector in agricultural marketing.
(b)    Farmer information and advisory services (FIAS) consist of information
development, information dissemination and institutional development. It is
designed to meet the increasing demand for critically needed information by
newly emerging private farmners, restructured state and collective farms and agro-
business enterprises. The underlying concept is an approach oriented to user
needs which is multi-source, multi-user, multi-disciplinary and multi-media.
FIAS will provide timely and relevant agronomic, technical, legal, business,
management and en"ironmental information in agriculturally important areas.
(c)    Seed industry development consists of the establishrment of commercially
viable hybrid corn and sunflower seed production and processing units owned
and operated by private enterprises located in selected southern oblasts, krays and
republics and having a comparative advantage in seed production for these crops.
These investments are intended to demonstrate how a commercial seed industry
could be organized in a market environrmient.  It will also support the
establishment of a national policy and institutional framework whic3 is consistent
with accepted international norms.
(d)    In addition, the Project Development Unit (PDU, has been established
within the MOAF to coordinate the overall implementation of the ARIS project
and to develop analytical capacity to prepare future agricultural projects for
financing by international financial institutions.
Benefis:            The project is expected to have substantial direct and indirect financial and
economic benefits for individual enterprises, farmers, consumers and the nation.
The project's potential benefits include enhanced economic incentives, increased
agricultural production (particularly corn and sunflower seeds), farm income,
rural employment, and foreign exchange savings. The project would support
accelerating the pace of reforms which are needed to make the agricultural sector
more efficient based on private ownership and market principles.  The
development of a market information system and alternative agricultural
marketing channels will increase ' ompetition, reduce physical losses, provide
higher quality food at competitive prices and have favorable impact on prices and
market services received by farmers and hence farm income. The development
and dissemination of appropriate and timely information to private farmers,
newly restructured farms and agro-business enterprises will enable them to make
informed business decisions, improve agricultural productivity and actively



ix
participate in the process of agricultural reform. The establishment of a modem
seed industry would increase the availability of quality seed which, in turn,
would result in higher crop yields and foreign exchange savings from reduction
in seed imports and would lay the foundation for a modem seed industry in the
private sector. The project would be an important step towards reforning the
agricultural sector and would train a large number of Russian experts to advise
farmers and develop analytical capacity to prepare and implement future
agricultural projects.
Risks:              The main risk is that the project may face delays in implementation. Factors that
could cause problerns in project implementation include: the current unstable
macroeconomic environment and its possible further deterioration, lack of
political consensus on agricultural reforms, delay in government decision-making
and lack of counterpart funds and trained personnel with experience in
implementing World Bank projects. The maintenance of free markets for the sale
of seeds is critical for the commercial viability of participating private seed
enterprises. As long as these prices are not controlled, domestic seed prices
should adjust with changes in international prices and the exchange rate. which
should enable private investors to obtain an attractive return on their investment.
The review and appraisal of at least the first three sub-projects for each of the
seed and marketing enterprises are expected to reduce potential implementation
risks. The project has introduced necessary measures in the form of legal
covenants, training and technical assistance, and has allocated necessary
resources to minimize these risks. The establishment of a strong PDU, with
appropriate support for procurement, disbursement, accounting, project
management and technical aspects, is also expected to mitigate some of the
potential project risks. The project would be intensively supervised (including
a project launch workshop, annual reviews and a comprehensive mid-term
review) so that appropriate and timely actions are taken to avoid and overcome
implementation problems.
Environment:        The project is expected to have a positive impact on the environmert. The move
toward private farning promoted by this project is expected to foster more
prudent use of land and other natural resources. In addition, public awareness
of agricultural environmental issues will be created through appropriately
designed environmental information collection and dissemination, technical
assistance and training programs. Any en  -onmental concerns related to new
investment sub-projects for the developmt;..L of 'pilot" marketing and seed
enterprises will be addressed during implementation of sub-projects by following
the Bank's and the country's environmental requirements and guidelines. The
feasibility studies for marketing and seed enterprises will include an obligatory
environmental review by concerned government agencies, the adequacy of which
will be verified by the PDU. The Bank review of typical fe2sibility studies will
ensure the adequacy of environmental analyses and suggest improvements, as
necessary. The project has a "B" environmental assessment category.

RUSSIAN FEDERATION
AGRICuLTURAL REFORm IMPLEMNTATION SUPPORT (ARIS) PROJECT
STAFF APPRAISAL REPORT
I
THE MACROECONOmIC SETTNG
A. ECONOMIC PERFORMANCE
1.     The collapse of the former Soviet Union (FSU), ,ollowing the historic political events in 1991,
resulted in massive disruptions in all spheres of economic activity. The inmediate impact was a sharp
decline in real output with both industrial output and agricultural output falling by 8 percent each. Real
investment fell in excess of 25 percent. In January 1992, Russia launched an anbitious and
unprecedented economic reform program, which consisted of liberalization of prices, exchange rate and
the trade regime 1. However, high fiscal deficits caused an explosion of inflation, with retail prices
increasing six-fold and severely changing the agricultural input-output price relationships. The fiscal
program, though limited to the first quarter of 1992, imposed new taxes and slashed government spending
with the Central Bank pursuing an austere credit policy. The resultng spectacular rise in inter-enterprise
arrears and fears of a collapse in industrial output led the Central Bank to reverse course and loosen
credit reins.
2.      Some respite from the difficult macroeconomic situation in 1992 was obtained during the first
half of 1993, due to the stabilization measurer enforced by the pro-reform Government to avoid hyper-
inflation. The inflation rate declined from 24 percent per month in the first quarter to 19 percent in May
and 16 percent in June 1993. Decline in industrial production and real wages slowed. Economic
indicators generally worsened during the second half of the year, with the monthly rate of inflation
exceeding 20 percent during the tird quarter of 1993 as compared to the Govermnent's target of 11
percent. Output resumed its fall after a period of relative stability in late 1992 and early 1993, with a
drop of 12 percent in real GDP in 1993, representing an accumulated fall of nearly 40 percent in GDP
since 1990. While exports remained roughly unchanged in 1993, as compared to the previous year,
recorded imports for the economy fell by more than 25 percent. Imports of food and agricultural
commodities fell to about one-half of their level in the previous year. Decline in imports, therefore, was
considerably more than the decline in output during the same period. The decline in output is likely to
continue in 1994, although at a slower rate, with a rebound of growth not anticipated until in late 1995
or 1996. Declining output has not yet led to mass open unemployment, and this remains a potential
challenge in the near future.
3.      Significant progress has been made in the privatization of state enterplises in the last two years.
In late 1992, the Government launched a voucher-based mass privatization program under which vouchers
were issued to the population to be used independently to buy shares in enterprises or through
approximately 300 investment funds participating in the privatization program. By the end of 1993, more
than 7000 medium and large enterprises had been privatized this way, representing about 30 percent of
1.    More information about the macroeconomic situation during the early period of reform is available in the
Country Economic Memorandum (CEM) published ty the World Bank and entitled Russian Economic
Reform: Crossing the Threshold of Structural Change, September 1992.



2
the industrial work force. In addition, approximately 60-70 thousand small-scale enterprises were
privatized under arrangements predating the voucher program.
B. CouNTRY AssISTANCE STRATEGY
4.      The objective of the Bank's strategy is to effectively participate in the overall structural
transformation of the Russian economy into a market economy based largely on the private sector. The
Bank's lending s.rategy for the agricultural sector thus focuses on financing key investments in the private
sector as demonstrations for wider replication, on developing the critical infrastructure and institutions
needed to support farm restructuring, land privatization, development of markets, enhancement of
knowledge through creation of market and farmer information systems, reduction in distortions and
reform of agro-industrial enterprises. The Bank has prepared a comprehensive study that outlines a
broad strazegy for transforming the food and agricultural sector in Russia.2
5.     The Bank's involvement in Russia so far consists of a mix of rek.abilitation, technical assistance
and investment operations. The Board approved four loans for Russia in FY93 and, so far, two loans
in FY94: (a) the First Rehabilitation Loan of US$600 million in August 1992 (Ln. 3513), which included
US$150 million for agriculture to finance imports of critical agricultural inputs; (b) the Employment
Assistance and Social Protection Loan of US$70 miilion in October 1992 (Ln. 3532); (c) the Privatization
Implementation Assistance Project of US$90 million in November 1992 (Ln. 3546); (d) the Oil
Rehabilitation Project of US$610 million in June 1993 (Ln. 3623); and (e) the Highway Rehabilitation
and Maintenance Project of US$300 million in February 1994 (Ln. 3706). The Financial Institution
Development Project of US$200 million was approved by the Executive Directors on May 19, 1994
(Ln 3734).
6.    The Bank's Country Assistance Strategy (CAS) for Russia was discussed with the Executive
Directors on May 19, 1994 in the context of the Financial Institutions Development Project. At that time,
the Bank recommended an Intermediate Case lending program on the ore r of $1.0-1.5 billion anmnally
(not including quick-disbursing adjustment lending), which would include a set of core projects that would
not require major sectoral policy conditionality. The ARIS project and the companion Land Reform
Implementation Support (LARIS) Project are included in the set of core projects. The objectives of the
CAS include: (i) support for the development of a market-oriented economy based on private sector
initiatives; (ii) encouragement of most of the redirection of public sector involvement in the economy
toward the establishment of open and competitive markets ald the provision of physical, social, legal and
institutional infrastructure not normally provided through the private sector; and (iii) establishment of the
Bank as a trusted and reliable development partner. 'The proposed ARIS Project meets all three
objectives. The private sector would be involved in :e operation of key agricultural marketing
infrastructure and the development of a modem seed in,ubLry. The public sector would focus on the
development of market information system and the provision of farmer information and advisory services
to support the evolution of markets and growth of private fanning. The Bank's support for programs
aimed at the development of private farmiing has encouraged the MOAF in promoting sound policies in
this sector. The proposed project is therefore fully consistent with the Bank's CAS for Russia.
2.    This strategy is contained in the Bank publication entided Food and Agricultural Policy Reforms in the
Former USSR: An Agenda for the Transition, Studies of Economies in Transition, Number 1, World Bank,
September 1992; and in the Background Working Papers, which focus on different aspects of the
agricural sector.

1I
THE AGRICULTURAL SECTIOR
A. AGRICULTURE IN THE RUSSIAN ECONOMY
7.      The agricultural sector plays a vital role in Russia's economy. First, the agro-industrial
complex is large and accounts for about 25 percent of the net material product, employment and
investment in Russia. Second, Russia is endowed with an extensive natural resource base with large
agricultural potential, but at present these resources are not being used efficiently. Third, agriculture
absorbs a large share of budgetary and hard currency resources, and any improvements in sector
performance could have a large impact on containing the budget deficit and improving the balance of
payments. Fourth, consumers allocate as much as 50 percent of their annual income to meet food
requirements. Finally, agriculture is an important sector to facilitate transition to a market economy and
development of private enterprises.
8.      Improved performance of the agricultural sector is one of the most important elements in
stabilizing the Russian economy and accelerating the ongoing structural transformation. Despite the
sector's potential for higher efficiency and growth, overall agricultuial production and distribution remain
inefficient. Agricultural production is declining, yields are low, physical losses are high, output and input
distribution systems are inefficient, prices are distorted, profitability is low and declining (particularly
for the livestock sector), and consumer and producer subsidies still remain high. Besides the prevailing
macroeconomic imbalances, deteriorating terms-of-trade, inefficient farm structure and lack of adequate
incentives, competitive markets, technical and market information and a well functioning credit system
have offset the expected benefits of reform in the short-run. The problems of access to inputs, markets,
information and credits are more serious for the newly emerging private farmers as compared to existing
or restructured collective or state farms.
B. STATUS OF AGRICULTURAL PRoDuCTIoN
Production Performance
9.     Despite a rich natural resource base, Russia's agricultural production perfomance has been far
below its potential. Production and consumption of agricultural commodities are dominated by the
livestock sector. Agricultural production is generally inefficient in comparison with Westem countries
and labor productivity is lower, despite relatively high shares of total capital investment allocated to
agriculture in recent decades. Crop yields are roughly 10-30 percent lower while livestock yields are 40-
50 percent lower than in similar agro-ecological regions in the West. Weaknesses in the production
system were compounded by inappropriate and wasteful policies toward consumers. Retail prices in the
state trading system were maintained at about the same level from the mid-1960's to the late 1980's. This
policy, combined with steadily rising costs of production and increasing inflationary pressures throughout
the economy during the 1980's, led to unsustainable levels of subsidies and severe disequilibrium in
agricultural markets by the beginning of the 1990's.
10.    The agricultural growth rate has been steadily declining since the 1970's and turned negative
beginning in 1990, with few hopeful signs of immediate reversal. During the 1980s, agricultural
production in Russia grew by less than two percent per year, allowing for modest increases in per capita
food availability. From its peak level in 1989, gross agricultural production fell by 4 percent in 1990,
a similar amount in 1991, 8 percent in 1992, and 4 percent in 1993, for a cumulative decline of about
20 percent from 1989 to 1993. The livestock sector accounts for about 60 percent of the gross value of
agricultural production. Livestock production fell by 23 percent between 1989 and 1993 while crop



4
production fell by 12 percent. Within the crop sector, over three-quarter of the arable area is allocated
to produce foodgrains, feedgrains and fodder crops (Figure 2.1). Grain production was at record level
in 1990, declined substantially in 1991, was at near record level in 1992 and was slightly lower in 1993.

11.    The main reasons for relatively poor agricultural (particularly livestock) production performance
are summarized below:
e     Overall deterioration in macroeconomic envronment: The break up of the former
USSR, the fragmented political structure, declining export revenues, uneven progress on
economic reforms and rapid depreciation of the ruble (between 1990 to 1993) led to
severe curtailment of imports of critical agricultural inputs and hence decline in
production. On the other hand, hign inflation rates and reduction in subsidies greatly
eroded the real purchasing power of consumers (which was exacerbated by the uneven
liberalization of input-output prices) and led to a decline in effective demand for
agricultural products;
o     Lack of appropriate incentives: Distorted input-output price structure, deteriorating
terms of trade and inappropriate farm structure failed to provide appropriate production
incentives, as did continued limitations on property rights. For January-December 1993,
the index of input prices paid by farms increased 11-12 times compared with the same
period in 1992 while the index of output prices received by farms increased 9 times,
thereby worsening the significant decline in terms of trade for agriculture recorded in
1992 over 1991 (Figure 2.2);
o     Disruptions in trade and marketing: The breakdown of traditional marketing linkages
among countries of the former USSR and Eastern Europe led to a decline in availbility
of critical agricultural inputs and disruption in markets and the payment system;
o     Dependence on state monopolies: Continued dependence on state monopolies for the
production, distribution and marketing of agricultural inputs and outputs led to
inefficiencies, wastage and reduced incentives;

9     Reduced flnancial resources: Reduction in the availability of credit for production
activities and curtailed flow of subsidies to agricultural enterprises led to a reduction in
capacity utilization and hence production of agro-industrial enterprises; and
O     Weak linkages betveen research and applicadon: Agricultural research priorities do
not address the most pressing problems faced by farmers in terms of improved farm
management and agricultural technology.
12.     The poor production performance and low levels of agricultural production led to a heavy
reliance on imports of basic agricultural commodities and a net drain on foreign exchange resources, with
imports greatly exceeding exports. This resulted in large foreign exchange costs to the economy and has
been associated with large budgetary expenditure in the form of large import subsidies (about 40% of
total food and agriculture subsidies) and disincentives to local producers. For example, in 1992, wheat
imports were about US$'.9 billion and feedgrain imports (primarily corn and barley) were US$1.1
billion. The break up of the former Soviet Union led to disruption in the supply of essential agricultural
inputs, such as hybrid corn seed (which was produced mostly in Ukraine and Moldova), necessitating
Russia to import high quality hybrid corn seed worth about US$60 million in 1992/93. Furthermore,
during 1992, Russia imported vegetable oil worth US$275 million and oilseed meal (an animal feed)
worti approximately US$500 million. However, agricultural imports in 1993 declined by about 50
percent over 1992.
Production Prospects
13.    The prospects for agricultural production are largely dependent on the Government's ability to
enhance economic incentives, promote competition, reduce its intervention through directives and
implement agricultural reforms. So far, large state and collective fanns have continued to rely on large
subsidies and a high level of Government intervention. The government decrees and agricultural policies
pursued since January 1992 indicate significant reduction in Federal Government intervention at all stages
of the agricultural production, distribution, marketing and pricing. However, there exists a wide variation
in policies and reform initiatives being pursued by different oblast governments. Building an appropriate
institutional foundation for the implementation of agricultural reforms is, therefore, very critical for its
transformation.



6
C. FOOD AN!D AGRICULTURAL REFORMS
Reform Status
14.     Agricultural reforms were launched in earnest in 1990 with the passing of legislation on land
reform and private ownership, which reestablished the concept of individual or corporate ownership of
property, including: (a) the right to farm land by private farmers; (b) the freedom to enjoy the profits
therefrom; and (c) the right to acquire and dispose of property by sale, lease or inheritance. A list and
a brief description of major laws, decrees and resolutions pertaining to agricultural reform are provided
in Annex 1.
15.     A number of significant Presidential decrees were issued in late 1991 and early 1992 which
liberalized prices for a large nunber of commnodities, reduced agricultural subsidies, laid the basis for
privatization of state enterprises, established a framework for the reorganization of state and collective
farms and developed mechanisms of support for the creation of private family farms. During 1992, 1993
and early 1994, further steps were taken to support the agricultural reform effort. The system of
administrative allocation of inputs was abandoned and the size of centralized state orders for the sale of
agricultural commodities was significantly reduced but, in many cases, was replaced by sirmilar market
interve-ntions by the local governments. In addition, a program for privatizing input distribution,
agricultural service and food processing enterprises was introduced. The Land Decree, which was signed
by the President on October 27, 1993, strengthened private land ownership and removed restrictions on
buying, selling and mortgaging of land, thus creating an enabling environment to develop the land
market.
16.     The prospects for advancing agricultural reforms are uncertain because of a lack of clarity on
institutional responsibility for policy formulation, existence of monopolistic structures in agricultural
marketing, a continuing lack of an adequate legal framework for reform and a lack of consensus about
the strategy for sector development. In order to ensure sustained momentum, institutions responsible for
implementing ongoing reforms need to be supported. Agricultural reforms broadly focus on the following
five areas:
(a)    Land Refonn: The initial steps towards the establishment of land market are in place.
These consist of a package of laws and decrees, particularly the October 1993
Presidential Decree that: (i) allows citizens to own land, individually or jointly; (ii)
allows the owners to buy, sell and mortgage land; (iii) provides the right to exist as
private farms with equal access to credit and agricultural inputs similar to those granted
to state and collective farms; (iv) provides a basis for restructuring state and collective
farms; and (v) simplifies procedures for land registration.
(b)    Market Reform: The initial steps towards the achievement of competitive agricultural
marketing are in place. These consist of the adoption of several new policies which: (i)
have reduced or eliminated formal procurement quotas by the federal government; (ii)
have allowed non-government agencies to buy and sell agricultural produce in the open
markets; and (iii) have initiated programs to break up existing marketing monopolies,
including Roskhleboprodukt.



7
(c)    Enterprise Reform: Steps have been taken for privatization and enterprise reform in the
food processing industry (a portion of shares has been reserved for farms traditionally
doing business with these enterprises), input manufacturing industry and distribution of
inputs and processed commodities. Steps are also being taken towards promoting greater
competition through (i) corporatization of large conglomerates; and (ii) reorganization of
enterprises to eliminate cartelization for certain industrial commodities. In addition, steps
have been taken to (i) abolish the centralized system of administrative allocation of
agricultural inputs to farms, and (ii) introduce a speciali-ed program for privatizing input
distribution and agricultural services.
(d)    Price Reform: Significant steps have been taken to liberalize prices. These consist of:
(i) removal of administrative controls on prices for a large majority of commodities,
including agricultural inputs and outptlts; and (ii) deregulation of farmgate prices for most
agricultural commodities at the federal level (indirect price controls still remain at the
oblast level). Grain prices are now close to world prices. Fruit and vegetable prices are
substantially liberalized.
(e)    Subsidy Policies: The size of agricultural subsidies, which were explicit prior to the
reform, has been significantly reduced; and previously implicit price or non-price
subsidies for credit, irlaports, and energy have been made explicit. The structure of
subsidies has also shifted, with a smalher share tied to specific commodities, and
responsibility for subsidies is increasingly shifting from the federal budget to oblast
budgets. On balance, the size of subsidies going to producers and consumers of
agricultural commodities has declined substantially but still remains high.
17.     In conclusion, while the Government has introduced many reforms in the last three years, the
mnain bottlenecks for their implementation are the lack of necessary infrastructure, critical support services
and appropriate analytical and institutional capacity to design and impljment ongoing reform initiatives.
Reform Impact
18.     The chaotic economic transition contributed to serious short-term disruptions in input supply
(particularly high quality seed), agricultural marketing, deterioration of agricultural terms of trade and
decline in profitability and farm investment. However, reforms have triggered restructuring of
consumption and production patterns at all levels which is essential to improve the efficiency and
productivity of the food and agricultural sector in the long-term.
19.     Gradual Shift from Livestock to Crop Production. The agricultural strategy during most of
the pre-reform period in Russia had a singular objective of maximizing livestock production to increase
per capita dairy and meat consumption, without regard to economic efficiency and response to market
signals. All the policies, production structures, infrastructure and institutions were designed and built
to support this objective. Up to 1990, livestock accounted for about 64 percent of the value of gross
agricultural production and the remaining 36 percent was crop production. Over half the grain (including
wheat) was used as livestock feed.
20.      The dramatic increase in input costs and reduction of subsidies on meat and other livestock
products have made livestock production unprofitable. The removal of state quotas on livestock sales,
combined with greater autonomy for farm producers, has led to an overall decline in livestock production
(Figure 2.3). On the other hand, emerging incentives for grain production, particularly an increase in
grain prices, has led to an increase in the share of crop output in the total agricultural output.



8

21.    Emergence of New Farm Organizations. In 1991, about three-quarters of Russian agricultural
production came from the large state and collective farms and the remaining one-quarter from individual
household plots. The liberalization in land laws has created new farm organizations. Two broad types
of farm organizations, that have evolved since 1991, are: (i) private individual farmers; and (ii)
corporatized state and collective fanms, which are in various stages of restructuring.
(a)    Private farms: By April 1, 1994, 277,000 private farms, averaging roughly 43 hectares
each, had been established (Figure 2.4). These farms account for about 5 percent of
arable land. The land reform has also provided entitlement to about 35 million users of
small land plots (household plots of state and collective farm workers, and garden,
orchard and dacha plots of city dwellers) which account for about 4 percent of arable
land. The plot size varies between 0.2 and 0.5 hectares. All of these users have now
become owners. The number of private farmers continues to increase.
(b)    Corporatization of state and collective farms: Most state and collective farms have
opted to assume property rights to their land from the state, but only a small percentage
has thus far opted for restructuring based on physical delineation of land and other asset
shares of each farm member. However, as the process of restructuring continues,
physical delineation is likely to expand. In addition to an appropriate legal framework,
investment in rural infrastructure (particularly roads), de-linking the provision of social
services in rural areas from the commercial operations of these farms and the
strengthening of rural social safety net are essential to expedite farm restructuring. IFC's
Land Privatization and Farm Reorganization project in Nizhny Novgorod, which is
supported by the USAID and the British Know-How Fund, has developed an approach
to restructure large state and collective farms into more economically viable units,
including private family farms. The process of farm restructuring received a boost with
the issuance of a Government Resolution entitled "On the Experience of Agrarian
Transformation in Nizhny Novgorod Oblast", highlighting the experience of the IPC
Nizhny Novgorod pilot project.

22.    Prvatizaton and Emergence of New Marketing Channels. The gradual reduction in the role
of the Government in procurement and distribution has led to a rapid emergence of a variety of marketing
channels, mostly in the private sector. The privatization of up- and down-stream enterprises has also
progressed reasonably rapidly. According to a survey on April 1, 1993, 53 percent of enterprises
engaged in primary processing of agricultural commodities had been privatized, as had been 33 percent
of enterprises in agro-services and 44 percent of enterprises dealing with agricultural inputs. The number
of private enterprises in the agro-industrial complex has since increased. The size of federal state orders
has been reduced to 10 million tons, compared with nearly 30 million tons just two years ago. This
leaves oblast administrations with tremendous leverage over agricultural marketing and inter-regional
trade. Oblast governments have introduced their own state orders which by and large compensate for
any declines in federal state orders. Furthermore, oblast government tends to dominate agro-industrial
enterprises, including those that have undergone privatization. The Government is in the process of
breaking up Roskhleboprodukt, a major agricultural marketing monopoly.
23.    In order to make significant progress in market reform it will be necessary to develop alternative
marketing channels, which will be free to respond to economic incentives. This implies establishment
of wholesale markets (both producer and consumer oriented) and improvement of existing storage
facilities and transportation links among regions. Another factor holding up development of agricultural
markets is the lack of market information. Goskomstat (the State Statistical Committee) publishes weekly
data on retail prices for major Russian cities, but data on other prices are spotty at best. Furthermore,
there is almost a total lack of information on shipments, supplies and grades of commodities at major
market centers.
24.    Privatization and Input Sector Reforms. As of April 1, 1993, 44 percent of agricultural input
distribution enterprises had been privatized. While oblast governments interfere less directly in the
marketing and distribution of agricultural inputs (farms no longer receive quotas for the supply of inputs
and are free to deal directly with manufacturers), a number of problems exist. First, access to inputs
depends in large measure on access to subsidized credit, which continues to be allocated administratively.
Second, production and marketing of inputs remains largely monopolistic. Tnird, the breakup of the
former USSR disrupted these traditional patterns of input production and exacerbated farm access to



10
inputs. Fourth, input supply at subsidized prices is available only if farmers agree to sell output to the
government at arbitrarily fixed procurement prices.
25.     Russia's existing agricultural research and advisory networks were developed as part of a
centralized administrative system, where access to information was controlled by the state with the
primary clients being the large state and collective farns. The overall agricultural support services,
including research, extension and education have been isolated and are not equipped to meet the rapidly
changing knowledge requirements for emerging farm organizations. Improvements in the overall
ir.formation systems are vital for the ongoing sectoral reforms to be fully effective. Reorganization of
existing, and establishment of new farms are changing the client base for agricultural research and
extension. With the advent of a market system, new types of information have become relevant for
farms, particularly in the areas of agro-business and farm management. Better dissemination of technical
information is also important. The institutional underpinnings of the research and information systems
are in disarray; a functional extension system does not exist and research services are unable to meet
current needs. Furthermore, state support has been declining in recent years, with the result that the
physical infrastructure and the quality of staffing of these systems have deteriorated.
1). STATUS OF AGRICULTuRAL INSrTIuTioNs
26.     The institutional structure of the agricultural sector in the Russian Federation is extremely
complex and is characterized by several layers of decision-making, policy formulation and program
implementation. This complex system, which has evolved over a period of seventy years, is facing new
challenges. The enforcement of the Federal Treaty has led to decentralization of power between the
federal and oblast authorities and the growing independence of large state institutions to maintain control
on production, distribution and marketing. The division of responsibilities and the interaction among
various organizations remain ill-defined and uncertain in practice. The complexities at the federal level
are compounded by uncertainties among organizations at the federal level and those at the oblast level
about their respective roles.
The Federal Assembly
27.     Recent political events have brought about major changes in the structure of government
institutions and decision-making. At the highest level of decision-making is the newly elected Federal
Assembly which consists of the State Duma (the lower house) and the Federation Council (the upper
house). An Agrarian Committee is expected to be responsible for preparing drafts of legislation on
specific areas dealing with food and agriculture for consideration by the Federal Assembly. The Agrarian
Committee is also expected to review drafts of legislatinn originating in the executive branch of the
government. During recent elections, the role of the Agrarian Party and other political parties interested
in addressing agricultural issues has strengthened in the Federal Assembly. However, the degree of their
influence in the overall decision-making process for agricultural reform is not clear at this time.
Federal Executive Apparatus
28.     The head of agricultural policy within the executive branch is the Deputy Prime Minister who
oversees all executive agencies involved in activities related to agriculture at the federal level. The
Minister of Agriculture and Food and the Chairman of the Russian Committee for Land Resources and
Land Management report to the Deputy Prime Minister. Closest to him is the Agro-industrial Division
of the Federal Executive Apparatus, which provides the Council of Ministers with necessaty analysis and
information related to the agro-industrial complex.



11
Ministry of Agriculture and Food and State Committees
29.    The day to day functions of the agricultural sector are carried out by the Ministry of Agriculture
and Food. The organizational chart of the MOAF is presented in Annex 2. The Mi;iistry has the lead
responsibility for farm restructuring and the development of specific subsidy channels to support farms.
In addition, there are state committees for land, fisheries, and a federal service for forestry.
Responsibilities for land administration and land privatization are handled by the Russian Committee for
Land Resources and Land Management. Privatization of state enterprises involved in production and
delivery of agricultural inputs, provision of agricultural services, and procurement, processing and
marketing of agricultural commodities is the responsibility of the State Committee for the Management
of State Property (GKI).
Other Ministries and Agendies
30.    Several other Ministries are also responsible for formulating and implementing policies related
to different aspects of the food and agricultural sector. These are Ministries of Economy; Finance; Fuel
and Energy; Science, University Education and Technology Policy; Transport; Trade and Material
Resources; and Industry. A number of corporatized state organizations continue to play an important role
in the functioning of the agricultural sector. Agroprombank is by far the largest source of credit for
agriculture and also an agent for channeling government subsidy payments. Roskhleboprodukt continues
to remain the near-monopolistic domestic trader of grain and most oilseeds. Rosagrosnab provides most
agricultural inputs to farms, with the exception of fertilizers and chemicals, which are provided by
Rosselkhozkhimiya.
E. BANK'S LENDING STRATEGY FOR AGRICULTURE
31.    The Bank, through its Food and Agricultural Sector work since November 1991, has played a
key role in building the foundation for the design of structural reforms and in putting together a complex
package of macroeconomic stabilization measures needed for supporting the reforms in the agricultural
sector. A broad based policy framework has already been developed, which provides a basis for policy
dialogue with the government. The most important contribution of the Bank has been in increasing the
exposure of key policy makers to market principles and working with them in the design of appropriate
agricultural policy reform program to accelerate transition to a market economy.
32.     The Russian Government has identified agriculture as one of the important sectors requiring
assistance from the World Bank. Such assistance is important to achieve short-term supply response,
improve the efficiency of resource use, support the adjustment process, demonstrate tangible benefits from
the reform program and lay the foundation to achieve a sustained long-term supply response. The Bank's
planned lending program for agriculture consists of six types of lending operations, including:
o     The agriculture components of Rehabilitation Loans to provide funds for financing
imports of critical agricultural inputs. The First Rehabilitation Loan, which allocated
US$150 million to agriculture, has almost been disbursed. The Second Rehabilitation
Loan, which is expected to address structural and macroeconomic policy issues, has been
negotiated and is expected to be presented to the Executive Directors in 1994.
e     The proposed Agricultural Reform Implementation Support (ARIS) Project will
support the creation and strengthening of institutional and analytical capacity to
implement ongoing and future agricultural reform and finance selected critcal
investments as "pilot" projects.



12
*     The proposed Land Reform Implementation Support (LARIS) Project, which is also
expected to be presented to the Executive Directors in 1994, would support the creation
and strengthening of public infrastructure and institutional capacity to implement the
ongoing and future land reforms.
°     Agricultural Sector Adjustment Loan (ASAL), the p;ocessing of which was initiated
earlier, will support a comprehensive national program of agricultural policy reforms
and, in this respect, it will complement the ARIS and LARIS projects. Further
processing of this loan, however, is contingent on the progress in policy reforms and an
agreement on an agricultural policy reform program.
°     Regional Agricultural Development Project (RADP) is designed to focus on two
selected regions to finance critical investments and implementation of specific policy
reforms, with a particular focus on farm restructuring.
*     The Sector or Sub-Sector Investment Loans (SILs) are expected to focus on vital
agricultural sub-sectors. The first in a series of such operations is expected to focus on
agricultural research, extension and education. Other possibilities include forestry,
fertilizer, agro-industry and livestock sub-sectors.
33.    The Bank's strategy is to help put in place the fundamental building blocks, which are essential
to the development of a market oriented agricultural economy. While this transition will necessarily take
place over a number of years, there is an immediate need to develop the human resources and institutional
capacity in agriculture which are vital for the efficient functioning of a market economy and the
implementation of agricultural reforms. The Bank's lending strategy thus focuses on financing key
investments in the private sector as demonstrations for wider replication, on developing the critical
infrastructure and institutions needed to support farm restructuring, land privatization, development of
markets, enhancement of knowledge through creation of market and farmer information systems,
reduction in distortions and reform of agro-industrial enterprises.
34.     In support of these goals, the Bank expects to provide substantial training and technical
assistance through direct lending and co-financing arrangements supplemented by the ongoing and planned
economic and sector work (ESW). In addition to the major Bank report entitled Food and Agricultural
Policy Reforms in the Former USSR: An Agenda for the Transition (Food Sector Study), financed by the
Bank's Technical Cooperation Program (TCP), the ESW includes strategic studies on land reform and
farm restructuring (Yellow Cover completed), agricultural research, extension and education (White
Cover completed) and possibly other sector studies such as forestry, fertilizers, food processing and
livestock.
F. RATIONALE FOR BANK PARTICIPATION
35.    Increasing productivity, farm restructuring and privatization of the agricultural sector are major
goals of Russia's ongoing transition to a market economy. Since 1990, the federal government has
introduced unprecedented land, price, trade and enterprise reforms designed to accelerate this transition
and has made considerable progress. Nevertheless, much remains to be done to develop an overall policy
framework to reform the agricultural sector. There are critical institutional constraints to the sector's
reform process, among the most of which are: an inadequate land title registration system (which is being
addressed through the proposed LARIS project); lack of a market information and competitive marketing
channels; lack of information to serve private farmers and newly restructured farms; and lack of modern
industry for production and distribution of quality seed for two critical crops, corn and sunflower.



13
Furthermore, Russia lacks appropriate analytical and institutional capacity for project preparation. As
a result, it is difficult to prepare projects based on sound economic principles and effectively implement
the ongoing and future agricultural reforms.
36.    The Bank's involvement in the agricultural sector is essential to continue the dialogue with the
Government which was initiated through the Food Sector Study work. The Bank's involvement in the
ARIS project would: (a) help develop key institutions necessary to implement agricultural reform; (b)
accelerate the development of private farming; (c) finance critical investments to develop market
infrastructure and modem seed industry; and (d) maintain the policy dialogue and contribute to the
prospects for continuing policy reform.
I1[I
BANK EXPERIENCE AND LESSONS
37.     The Bank has very limited experience in implementing agricultural projects in Russia or other
FSU countries. The experience in the socialist East European countries in transition is also limited and
it is too early to draw specific lessons. However, the Bank has extensive experience in implementing
diverse agricultural projects (similar to the proposed ARIS project) in many other parts of the world.
Relevant experience is briefly summarized below to provide appropriate lessons for designing the
proposed agricultural project in Russia.
A. MARKET INFRAsTucTuRE DEVEoPMENT
Bank Experience in Agricultural Marketing
38.     Bank-assisted projects in agricultural marketing have, by and large, emphasized the role of
prices in efficient resource allocation, and supported the development of marketing organizations and
infrastructure. A few projects focussed specifically on development of export markets. An Operations
Evaluation Department (OED) report, based on four hundred agricultural projects audited prior to 1986
(almost half of which included marketing components), noted that about 55 percent of the marketing
components met the established criteria for success.3 However, these components did not address the
measures needed for efficient market operations such as conmiercial code and privatization. In most
cases, institutional capacity was overlooked or inadequately appraised. No assistance was provided to
improve trading systems. Studies related to pricing, marketing policy, and organizational issues were
either done unsatisfactorily or not at all. There was no evidence that these studies resulted in any follow-
up action.
39.    The marketing parastatals' dominant role as project beneficiaries was striking. During the 1960s
and 1970s, parastatals were accepted less critically. Parastatal projects were perhaps easy to design and
implement. The parasatals often had a legal monopoly of trade, so that assistance was provided to the
only available and government-supported organization. Projects in Africa and Asia relied heavily on
parastatals, while projects in MENA (Middle East and North Africa) and LAC (Latin America and the
Caribbean) emphasized cooperatives and the private sector, respectively. Inadequate marketing margiins
(due to price controls) were the primary cause of poor services from both public and private narketing
channels. Problems of poor management in large parastatals and cooperatives seemed to outweigh
3.    The World Bank, 'Agricultural Marketing: World Bank's Experience,' Report No. 7353, June 28,
1988. Out of 402 projects, only 12 were classified as primarily marketing projects.



14
potential economies of scale. Private marketing channels, where available, were often preferred by
farmers and were formidable competitors to successful parastatals in the provision of marketing services.
40.     Projects dealing with horticC 'tural exports in the Mediterranean helped to moderniz "he sector
by promoting new agricultural techniques, improving post-harvest facilities and strengthening of
marketing systems. However, the implementation was affected by tensions associated with the use of 1
smallholder approach -- out of concern for poverty -- in projects that were intended to modernize ano
expand exports to a complex, fast-changing market.
41.    The Agricultural Wholesale Marketing Project in the Republic of Korea (Ln. 21 11) was the first
project of its kind by the Bank. Although the Bank-financed wholesale market in Seoul had a beneficial
impact, the preparation was long and costly resulting mainly from the Bank's inexperience in this type
of project and failure to engage adequate and relevant expertise; changes of objectives and components;
and language problems between the Bank and Korean experts.
42.     The Agricultural Marketing Project in Mexico (Ln. 2262) financed private investments to
improve infrastructure and equipment for agricultural marketing. The OED review classified this project
as satisfactory, particularly in institution building. In retrospect, however, one of the main lessons
learned was that the project preparation failed to identify important trends in marketing that were already
underway. Furthermore, the spatial aspects of marketing -- location of new wholesale and retail centers
-- should have received more attention.
43.     The Marketing Project in Brazil (Ln. 2727) introduced reform in trade and pricing policies for
key agricultural commodities. The project also supported a market information system to improve the
quality of the government's agricultural production forecasts. The project was rated satisfactory with
notable success in the areas of price stabilization based on a system of market safeguards (price bands);
trade simplification in terms of reducing unnecessary agricultural inspections and red tape in customs
clearance and establishing better information systems to monitor trade transactions.
Proposed Project Design
44.     The design and implementation of the market infrastructure development component of the
proposed project incorporates the lessons of Bank experience. Training and technical assistance will
support a framework for efficient market operations, develop strategies to restructure existing monopolies
and promote new institutions in agricultural marketing.
45.      The project-assisted pilot investments would be made in the private sector without the
involvement of government parastatals. The pilot investments will reform the operations of a few, large
fruit and vegetable bases which operated as part of massive storage and distribution system that served
the needs of the command economy. The pilot investments will demonstrate the critical role of
infrastructure and institutional reforms for reorienting the operations of existing marketing institutions
and for improving incentives to producers, efficiency in marketing services, and benefits to consumers.
46.     Bank involvement in the development of market information systems (MIS) has been limited.
The MIS design under the proposed project has benefitted from the experience gained from a pilot project
in Kaluga Oblast which is supported by the USDA. Several publications provided useful guidelines for
designing the market information system in Russia. This includes a handbook on Market Information
Services prepared by the German Agency for Technical Assistance (GTZ), based on the Indonesian
Market Information System and the FAO's Agricultural Technical Services Bulletins on Market
Information System and the Use of Microcomputers for Market Information Systems.



15
B. FARMER INFORMATION AND ADviSORY SERVICES
Bank Expenence in Agriculture Extension
47.     The World Bank has been the main multilateral institution to finance agriculture extension
projects, designed to provide information and advisory services, in the developing world. With a modest
investment of US$2.8 million IDA credit to Kenya in 1964, the Bank's commitment to extension either
as free-standing projects or as components have amounted to over US$2.5 billion in nominal terms by
FY92. Agriculture extension continues to be an important element in the overall Bank's strategy to
promote agricultural development. The main objective of Bank financed extension projects have been
to increase production and productivity through: (a) strengthening message delivery capacity and linkages;
(b) improving management; (c) increasing environmental awareness; and (d) providing staff training.
48.     A recent review of past and current agricultural extension projects, supported by the Bank,
reveals three features. First, .the intended client group has been the small-holder subsistence agriculture,
as this group was considered most in need of extension services. However, the emergence of large
commercial farm organizations has created the need for enlarging the client base and information
requirements. Second, almost all the Bank financed projects have relied on the public sector as the main
provider of extension services. With many developing countries facing budget crisis, continued provision
of extension activities solely through the public sector, is unsustainable in the long run. Third, the
underlying Bank approach was the Training and Visit System (T&V) which emphasized face to face
interaction and, at times, was modified to take into account country-specific situations. It was a
hierarchically organized and time-bound method of managing extension that was designed to deliver
selected and timely technology to farmers with strict regularity. It required an extensive organization
with a singular devotion to extension activities.
Lessons from Experience with Extension Projects
49.    The major implementation problems encountered in extension projects were (a) lack of diagnosis
and feedback; (b) insufficient management support for extension staff; (c) unclear procurement
arrangements; and (d) underdeveloped links with research. Furthermore, the expected benefits have been
below expectations.4 However, given their nature, it is difficult to quantify the benefits from extension
services. Despite the importance of extension, a clear cut strategy for its provision and corresponding
policies do not yet exist in many countries.
50.    Lessons from the Bank's experience in agricultural extension are many. First, future extension
projects should focus on strengthening the generation, use and exchange of information. The top-down
approach needs to be changed to a more participatory approach, where the end-users are actively involved
in the design of the extension system. Second, attention should be paid to training of the staff engaged
in extension. Third, increased emphasis should be placed on a multi-media approach to disseminate
information since it would be cost effective and can reach the intended population quickly. Finally, while
the public sector would continue to be involved in extension activities, its role needs to be re-defined in
the light of rapid changes in information technology and increasing role of the private sector.
4.     See, for example, the project performance audit reports (PPARs) on Extension and Research Project in
India, Report No. 8808, June 29, 1990; National Agricultural Extension II Project in Thailand, Report
No. 10101, November 22, 1991; Agriculture Extension and Adaptive Research Project in Sri Lanka,
Report No. 10632, May 13, 1992; and Agricultural Extension and Research Report in Nepal, Report
No. 10661, May 20, 1992.



16
Proposed Project Design
51.     The design of the Farmer Information and Advisory Services (FIAS) component of the ARIS
project incorporates the lessons learned from Bank experience. The design is based on a multi-source,
multi-user, multi-disciplinary and multi-media approach. It uses the existing agricultural information base
available in agricultural research institutes, land data banks, academies and universities and a highly
skilled and literate work force. The FIAS component is designed to provide services initially as a public
good since the Russian economy is undergoing a structural transformation and the private sector is still
in its infancy. However, the project has built-in cost recovery mechanisms and has sufficient flexibility
for private sector participation.
C. SEED INDUSgRY DEVELOPMENT
Bank Experience in Seed Projects
52.     The World Bank has supported about 18 free-standing seed projects and over 80 projects
involving seed as a component for a total conmnitment of US$456 million from 1969 to 1993. Asia has
accounted for about 60 percent of projects and 70 percent of financing for seed projects. The broad
objectives of Bank financed seed projects have been: (a) enhancing the availability of high quality seeds
of major crops; (b) improving the quantity and quality of production, processing and marketing capability
of certified seeds; (c) enhancing the capacity of public sector research and development of pre-basic and
basic (super elite arid elite) seeds: (d) improvement in national seed policies, including seed law; (e)
establishment of quality control laboratories and seed certification agencies; and  (f) investments in
institutional development through technical assistance and training.
53.      Most of the investment has been in the public sector seed corporations for production,
processing, storage and marketing. This was due to considerations of food security and lack of interested
private sector to undertake investments in seed production. Many Bank projects operated well below their
capacity, were often a financial liability for governments and produced sub-standard quality seeds. Only
recently the Bank has initiated the integration of public and piivate sectors in seed projects. This includes
the India National Seed Project-III (1989), Nigeria Seed and Quarantine Project (1990), and Ethiopia Seed
Development project (1993).
54.     There has been a lack of national policy environment that would support market-oriented seed
industry in terms of varietal improvement, quality control, and certification standards. In the absence
of a national seed policy, seed law and attendant regulations were generally not enforced, despite Bank-
supported improvements in seed quality control aspects, seed testing laboratories, and the seed
certification agencies. Rationalization of seed pricing was often ignored since prices were often regulated
and subsidized. Enforcement of law on breeder's intellectual property rights and their consequential
impacts on the varietal development were given little attention.
55.     The establishment of government model seed farms has not been successful due to lack of
appropriate incentives and trained personnel. Many of the seed projects for self-pollinated crops (wheat
and rice), did not achieve their stated objectives. The quantity and the quality of seeds produced by the
government seed farms were not satisfactory and, therefore, were unable to gain farmers' confidence.



17
Lessons from Experience with Seed Projects
56.     The key implementation problems encountered in the Bank-financed seed projects are: (a) delay
in the construction of seed plants; (b) problem of counterpart funding; (c) delays in procurement; (d) lack
of institutional commitment; (e) delays in staff recruitment; and (f) weak implementation of seed policy.
There is also a need for the development of institutional franework, laws and enforcement mechanism
for seeds and intellectual property rights of plant breeders, privatization of seed enterprises, and
technology transfer to ensure establishment of commercial seed industry. Therefore, any future seed
projects should integrate public and private sector roles in developing the commercial seed industry.
Proposed Project Design
57.     The seed component of the proposed project incorporates the lessons learnt from the Bank-
financed projects by focussing both on policy and institutional aspects and investment in the private sector
for the development of pilot seed enterprises. The project will support the establishment of a seed
advisory committee and a non-governmental seed association to represent the interests of the seed
industry. The project will also strengthen the capacity of the agency responsible to register new varieties
and hybrids for conmmercial production and the seed inspection agency to provide a range of services to
the existing seed agencies and emerging seed enterprises.
58.     This component will demonstrate how a commercial seed industry can be organized to increase
the availability of high quality seeds. The proJect will provide institutional support in the form of training
to policy makers and technical personnel of the government seed department and seed industry. Technical
assistance will be provided to help implement the plant breeders' rights legislation, to help administer the
certification and seed testing programs, to help define the roles of the seed advisory committee and the
seed association and to prepare feasibility studies for potential new seed enterprises.
IV
THE PROJECT
A. PRoJECr OBJECTIVES
59.     The main objectives of the project are: (a) to create and strengthen the most critical support
services to accelerate the implementation of ongoing and future agricultural reforms; (b) to build
analytical and institutional capacity to identify, appraise and implement agricultural and agro-business
projects by the private sector on the basis of economic criteria; and (c) to demonstrate the role that the
private sector can play in promoting efficiency in the agro-industrial sector. The support services would
focus on: (i) the creation of an enabling policy environment for private sector participation in seed and
market development; (ii) establishment of a nationwide market information system; and (iii) establishment
of fanner information and advisory services. The role of the private sector would be demonstrated by
selective "pilot" investments in hybrid corn seed enterprises, sunflower seed enterprises, fruit and
vegetable marketing bases and vegetable storage facilities. The provision of critical support services and
demonstration of the commercial viability of participating enterprises would lay the foundation for an
efficient agricultural sector based on private ownership and market principles.  Project support for
participating marketing and seed enterprises is critical since (i) it will facilitate privatization of similar
enterprises; (ii) it will help introduce policy reforms; (iii) it will demonstrate that these and similar
investments are commercially viable and can be replicated; and (iv) it will build analytical capacity to
evaluate and implement sub-projects by the private sector.



18

Infrastructure Development; (b) Farmer Information and Advisory Services; and (c) Seed Industry
Development. These are summarized in Table 4.1. The sub-sector background and additional component
details are summarized in Annex 3 for Market Infrastructure Development; in Annex 4 for Farm
Information and Advisory Services; and in Annex 5 for Seed Industry Development.
