C. MARKErI  FIRsArRucrURE DEVELOPMENT
Objectives
61.      The main objectives of market infrastructure development component are: (a) to establish an
agricultural market information system (MIS) to collect, process and disseminate relevant, timely and
reliable market and price information for the benefit of farmers, traders and policy makers in 86 oblasts
and 500 rayons; (b) to demonstrate, through "pilot" private investments in marketing enterprises in
Moscow City, Moscow and Belgorod Oblasts, the role of commercially viable marketing enterprises; and
(c) to define the evolving catalytic role of MOAF and regional authorities in market development during
tuansition.
Concept
62.      Access to market information is crucial for the successful functioning of the emerging market
economy since it increases market transparency; transmits incentives and opportunities for agricultural
producers; improves producers' bargaining position; stimulates competition among traders; expands
processors' and consumers' choices in product selection; and facilitates rational decision-making by
producers, traders and policy makers. In the past, the government used Goskomstat and MOAF's
statistical systems to generate information necessary for managing a centrally planned economy. The
ongoing privatization, deregulation and decentralization of the economy has led to the emergence of
private farmers, restructured farms and agro-business enterprises. This has generated a need for new
statistical and information services to improve marketing decisions; identify opportunites and adjust
production and marketing activities in response to changes in prices, demand and supply of agricultural
commodities and inputs.



19
63.      There is a need to re-define the traditional role of MOAF and local governments from
performing market functions to playing a catalytic role in encouraging private sector investments in such
activities. This would be demonstrated through selective investments in "pilot" marketing enterprises.
The ARIS project would also establish a legal, institutional and operational framework for emerging
primary and wholesale markets and commodity exchanges for agricultural products.
Detailed Features
64.     The Market Infrastructure Development Component consists of three sub-components: (a)
market information system: (b) pilot investments in market infrastructure; and (c) institutional
development.
65.     Market Information System. The project will support the establishment of a nationwide
agricultural market information system. A pilot project in market information, prepared by MOAF and
the U.S. Department of Agriculture (USDA), is currently being implemented in Kaluga Oblast with
technical assistance from the USDA. Similar pilot projects are being prepared by MOAF and USDA in
Belgorod Oblast and the Republic of Mari. The MIS to be developed by the ARIS project will benefit
from this experience. Market information will flow from rayon offices to oblast offices. Oblast offices
will also collect and process local information and manage the database. A central Market Information
Unit in the MOAF in Moscow will draw information from databases at the oblast level, commodity
exchanges, Goskomstat and international sources. The proposed MIS is designed to perform this new
role and will consist of: (a) Price Information Service for farmers, traders, processors and consumers;
and (b) Price Monitoring Service for government agencies and policy makers. The project will provide
equipment, supplies, training and technical assistance to MOAF in Moscow, and its departments at oblast
and rayon levels. MOAF will release the market information, initially as a public good, through
television, radio, electronic and print media, with a phased introduction of cost recovery through
provision of commercial information services.
(a)    Commodity coverage. Commodities to be covered by the MIS include agricultural
inputs, outputs and processed products as follows: (i) agricultural inputs: seeds, feed and
feed additives, fertilizers, pesticides, fuel and oil, and machinery; (ii) agricultural
outputs: grains, flax, sugar beets, sunflower seeds, soybeans, potatoes, cabbage and
other vegetables and fruits, livestock products, tea, forage products, and (iii) processed
products: sugar, bread, vermicelli, macaroni, flour, rice, alcohol, sunflower and
vegetable oil, frozen and canned meat, and dairy and poultry products.
(b)    Information coverage. The MIS will collect and report on: (i) farmgate prices: prices
received by agricultural producers; (ii) buying prices: prices of raw materials paid by
processing enterprises (representing farmgate price plus transport cost and trade margin);
(iii) selling prices: prices received by processing en erprises ex-factory (representing
purchase price plus value added and trade margin); and (iv) retail prices: prices paid by
consumers (representing selling prices of processing enterprises plus transport costs and
retail trade margins). These prices will be reported with reference to specific grades and
standards. Existing grading and standardization systems are poor and need improvement
to make them consistent with international standards. The MOAF, in association with
the State Grading and Standardization Bureau, will introduce appropriate grades for
collecting and reporting of price data. A formal understanding in this regard has been
reached between MOAF and the State Grading and Standardization Bureau. Information
will also include market arrivals and volumes traded, though in the initial years, the MIS



20
may not provide these data in all oblasts. Price information will be collected once a
week, but at greater frequency during harvest seasons.
(c)    Sources of information. Agriculture departments at the oblast and rayon levels will
identify representative producers and markets to be included in the sample survey, using
guidelines developed by MOAF, with assistance from the World Bank. These guidelines
will be reviewed and rationalized with technical assistance provided by the proposed
project. Information on farmgate prices will be collected at selected numbers of state,
collective and private farms and household and garden plots. As for the processors'
buying and selling prices, price information will be collected directly from sample
enterprises. For collection of data on retail prices, MOAF has established arrangements
with Goskomstat which has extensive experience in this activity. It will work with
Goskomstat to improve the design of information collection on retail prices rather than
establishing a parallel new organization for this activity. The curreat frequency of
reporting retail prices once a week is adequate. Institutional and financial arrangements
for accessing international databases will be negotiated during the first year of pro:ect
impiementation by inviting bids from national and international news agencies dealing
with market information.
(d)    Linkage with FIAS. The MOAF Computer Center will coordinate all MIS and the
Farmer Information and Advisory Services (FIAS) related data processing activities in
Moscow and oblast headquarters, including the installation of computer equipment at the
rayon level, where practical, to minimize costs and increase operational. efficiency. The
project will assist the MOAF Computer Center (MOAFCC) with a central computer
system, which is based on the Open Software Foundation (OSF) Distributee Computing
Environment (DCE) technology and uses the X.25 packet switching network. This
enables users to have access to information within the constraints of their authorization.
The system design includes three tiers of computer processing, viz., rayon, oblast, and
the federal center.
66.      Pilot Investments in Market Infrastucture.  The project will finance selected "pilot"
investments in typical consumption and production areas to demonstrate institutional reforms to promote
development of agricultural marketing by the private sector and the commercial viability of participating
private enterprises. The criteria for selection of private market enterprises and their eligibility for Bank
financing are indicated in Annex 6.
67.     The fruit and vegetable bases (large storage and distribution facilities which are a link between
the producers and retailers) represent substantial past investments in buildings, cold storage and handling
equipment. For example, the 25 specialized fruiit and vegetable bases in Moscow City have storage
capacity for 1.2 million tons of produce, including 737,000 tons of cold storage. Under the old system,
all fresh fruit and vegetables were channelled through one or the other of these 25 "bases" in Moscow
City. They stored and packed the produce to send it to retail shops or institutions in designated areas.
Prices were partially subsidized by the Moscow City Government and retailers had no choice of supplier
or quality. The bases, until late 1991, were under the centralized control of the Moscow Committee on
Fruit and Vegetable Sector. Since 1991, it became possible for the management and workers of
individual bases to organize into Fruit and Vegetable Associations (FVAs), lease their premises from the
Moscow City and operate the bases independently.



21
68.     Since July 1993, all the bases have become or are in the process of becoming joint stock
companies. About half of them are privatized as "closed" joint stock companies, while the other half
retain the Moscow Property Committee as a minority shareholder. The personnel working in the bases,
from the managers to the floor workers, have become joint owners of the bases and depend on the FVA's
for their livelihood and many will remain employees of the bases. These bases are in a period of rapid
market transformation and their traditional activities of providing urban storage for five basic crops, viz.,
potatoes, cabbages, onions, carrots and red beets, are not going to sustain them financially in a free
market situation. Hence, there is a need for them to take on new activities, respond to consumer demand
and improve their operational efficiency.
69.    In order to do this, pilot investments will be undertaken in Moscow City; Moscow Oblast; and
Belgorod Oblast. The sub-borrowers would be recently privatized enterprises (own at least 51 percent
of the shares). The project would finance the rehabilitation and construction of market infrastructure,
including civil works, equipment, cold storage facilities, incremental working capital, training and
technical assistance. Some of the sub-projects have already been appraised by MOAF and the World
Bank and appraisal of the remaining sub-projects will be supervised by the Project Development Unit
(PDU) and the Bank during project implementation.
(a)    Pilot Investments in Moscow  City.  These will include technical upgrading,
rehabilitation and conversion of up to three fruit and vegetable bases and their gradual
transformation from centers for seasonal procurement and long-term storage into
permanent multi-commodity distribution centers, mini-wholesale and "cash and carry
mnarkets and custom storage facilities. This will also focus on reducing post-harvest
losses, and improving storage facilities for fresh fruits, vegetables and potatoes to
safeguard their quality and hygienic conditions. Two bases which have already been
identified for inclusion in this project are: Perovskaya and Sverdlovskaya.
(b)    Pilot Investments in Moscow Oblast. This will include expansion and upgrading cf
storage facilities (up to a maximum of six private enterprises) for fruits and vegetables,
including sorting and grading facilities in support of the proposed transfer of the seasonal
and long-term storage of fruits and vegetables from Moscow City to production areas in
Moscow Oblast.
(c)    Pilot Investments in Belgorod Oblast. These will include investments which would
have an impact on market development in predorninantly production and rural areas of
Belgorod Oblast, consisting of technical upgrading of two fruit and vegetable bases to
reduce their seasonal and long-term storage function and conversion into distribution
centers or cash and carry markets. Pre-feasibility studies for these sub-projects have
already been completed.
70.     Institutional Development. Marketing, as opposed to central allocation, is a relatively new
concept in Russia. Extensive training and technical assistance is needed to provide the necessary exposure
and experience to those who will be involved in agricultural marketing, both in the government and
industry, at the federal, oblast and rayon levels.
(a)    Training.  The project will provide overseas and domestic training to marketing
specialists, regional organizations and other eligible institutions in marketing systems,
stock management and storage operations; financial management; product pricing and
profitability analysis; and financial and cost accounting. Further details on training are
provided in Annex 7.



22
c  (b)    Technical Assistance. Technical assistance would be provided to MOAF and selected
local governments to define their catalytic role in market development. This will
contribute to improved policy and operational framework for development of agricultural
marketing nationwide. Technical assistance would also be provided to the participating
private enterprises in the establishment and efficient operations of sub-projects. Further
details on technical assistance are provided in Annex 8.
Geographical Coverage
71.    The scope of the proposed MIS sub-component will be nation-wide and will focus on important
commodities for respective regions. During project i^nplementation, MOAF may include additional
commodities in specific regions. MIS will have small offices in 86 oblasts, krays and autonomous
republics. The number of rayons, in which market information will be collected, will be limnited to a
maximum of representative S00 rayons. This corresponds to about 5 to 6 rayons in each oblast. Market
information will be disseminated to all rayons nationwide. For the pilot investment program, the
geographical coverage will be Moscow City, Moscow Oblast and Belgorod Oblast. The implied
geographical coverage for the training and technical assistance program will be national. Moscow City,
Moscow Oblast and Belgorod Oblast and the participating private enterprises will also benefit from the
training and technical assistance activities.  The regions must agree to meet the conditions for
participation in the project. The geographical coverage of market infrastructure development component
is summarized in Map No. IBRD 25I49.
D. FARMER INFORMATION AND ADVisORY SERVICES
Objectives
72.    The objective of the Farmer Information and Advisory Services (FIAS) component is to respond
to rapidly increasing demand for information by the newly emerging private farmers, restructured farms
and agro-business enterprises. It is designed to provide timely, reliable, user friendly, easily accessible
and pratical (agronomic, technical, management, business, legal and environmental) information to
enable them to make informed decisions in a market-oriented environment. The project would support
the establishment of FIAS centers in 30 oblasts and 125 rayons covering important agricultural areas.
Concept
73.    In the past, information services for the agricultural producers were centralized. This system
resulted in the development of a large number of narrowly focussed specialists to advise the managers
of large state and collective farms. The knowledge was supplemented by research and field applications
undertaken in a wide network of research institutes and experimental stations. Most of the research
results were introduced by directives. There were little incentives to promote new innovations. While
this system worked reasonably well for a centralized economy with a limited number of clients (about
25000 state and collective farms), it is not appropriate or adequate to meet the needs of large number of
private farmers (277,000) and restructured farm enterprises (50,Ot).
74.     The emergence of new farm organizations and the absence of an appropriate extension system
has created a need for information for the development of business and technical skills. The FIAS
component has been designed to meet this need. The underlying concept of the farmer information and
advisory service component is the need based multi-source, multi-user, multi-disciplinary and multi-media
approach (4-M Modular approach). This approach is the most appropriate for Russia since it is based
upon the existing agricultural information available in an extensive network of agricultural research



23
institutes, agro-chemical testing stations, land data banks, academies and universities and the presence
of a highly skilled and literate work force. The vast logistic problems associated with a widely dispersed
farm population needs innovative approaches for reaching large numbers of the farm community. This
is made possible by the use of a variety of mass media delivery mechanisms and the use of a
teleconmmunication network appropriate for the establishment of a nationwide electronic network. A
regular feedback mechanism is incorporated for continuous updating of information needs. The concept
is flexible; uses methods that could be implemented quickly, easily and at least cost; and are accessible
to a large number of emerging farmers and farm organizations.
Detailed Features
75.     The Farmer Information and Advisory Services component consists of three sub-components:
(a) Farmer Information Development; (b) Farner Information Dissemination; and (c) Institutional
Development.
76.     Farmer Information Development. The project would strengthen the MOAF's capacity for
information development and packaging at the federal, oblast and rayon levels. FIAS would finance the
necessary infrastructure, supplies, equipment and vehicles to carry out these activities by the' MOAF.
Information needs assessment survey of various clients in 12 oblasts and 36 rayons was carried out in
1993. As a result of this survey, MOAF, through ARSRIIMA (All Russian Scientific and Research
Institute of Innovation and Marketing in Agriculture), is providing information and advisory services to
three oblasts (Vladimir, Voronezh and Pskov) and three rayons in each of these oblasts. The project will
benefit from this experience. Furthermore, findings of this survey have been incorporated in establishing
priorities for farmer information development.  The primary sources of appropriate agricultural
information would be national and regional agricultural research institutes, progressive farmers and
appropriate packages of agricultural practices, inputs and technology from foreign countries.
77.     The FIAS centers located at the oblast and rayon level agricultural administrations will be the
main contact points for various end-users. These centers would: (i) provide agricultural advisory
services; and (ii) serve as the primary source of agricultural information. Project trained specialists
would have access to a variety of information, agricultural packages and databases made available through
the electronic network. These centers would also have a variety of locally developed and international
materials related to various aspects of agriculture. The FIAS centers will co-ordinate relevant activities
and information with the MIS centers. Adequate training for PIAS personnel and a large number of end-
users and appropriate technical assistance will be provided to ensure the development and dissemination
of relevant information and advisory services.
78.    Two broad types of information would be developed and disseminated: (a) general information:
examples include laws concerning land reform, privatization and credit; "how to" start new business, keep
simple accounts, reduce post harvest losses; and (b) information specific to regions and clients -
examples include market conditions of farm inputs and outputs in neighboring rayons and oblasts,
weather, recommnended region specific agronomic practices and environmental conditions.  All
information generated by this project would initially be made available by the MOAF, as a public good.
However, cost recovery methods would be introduced in phases during project implementation.
79.      Fanner Information Dissemination.  The information from the FIAS centers will be
disseminated through a multi-media system. The project will finance the necessary supplies, equipment
and vehicles connected with the production and dissemination of farmer information through the following
media:



24
(a)    Video Productions and Dissemination: The project would modernize the MOAF and
AKKOR video centers with new equipment and materials for: (i) the establishment of
national and international agricultural video library in Moscow; (ii) the annual production
of 12 new video productions by the MOAF and 15 new productions by AKKOR
(Association of Peasant Farms and Cooperatives of Russia) which would focus on various
aspects of agricultural reforms; (iii) copying and distributing these videos to about 1200
rayons by the MOAF to existing video equipped farm community centers and to about
1200 rayon level AKKOR organizations; and (iv) airing 60 minutes weekly programs of
selected MOAF/AKKOR video productions dedicated to private farmers on national
television.
(b)   Electronic Infonmation: The development and dissemination of electronic information
will be integrated with the Market Information System. The FIAS component at the
federal, oblast and rayon levels will use personal computers, which have the ability to
interconnect with other computers in different locations. The FIAS specialist at the rayon
level would access specially designed databases and information bulletin boards for
serving the information needs of the various agricultural clients. Larger databases and
more complex farmer information packages would be developed at the oblast and the
federal levels, which would also be easily accessible at the rayon level and by the final
end-users. Given the lack of familiarity with the use of computers at the farm level, the
FIAS component would limit the dissemination of farmer information through electronic
media to the FIAS centers in 30 oblasts and 125 rayons.
(c)    Agricultural Fairs and Exhibitions: The project would provide support, in the form of
equipmeAt, vehicles and operating costs for holding exhibitions, as a pilot program, at
St. Petersburg to be sponsored jointly by MOAF and AKKOR. This program will focus
on promoting available farm technologies and also provide excellent opportunities for
foreign fms to display their products and technologies, which may lead to joint ventures
with local entrepreneurs.
(d)   Television, Radio and Print: The information developed by the FIAS component would
also be disseminated through national and regional television and radio stations and by
the existing printing facilities of the MOAF and those in the private sector. In this
regard, the MOAF would coordinate with the participating regional authorities and make
appropriate arrangements to disseminate information through television, radio and print
media in order to accomplish the objectives of the project.
(e)    Farmer Training Centers: The project will strengthen and modernize about five existing
farmer training centers to function as centers of excellence for training the trainers,
training agricultural extension workers, training far ners and for the development and
dissemination of multi-disciplinary farmer information. These training centers will be
located in the regions, and possibly one in Moscow, to train specialists essential for the
effective execution of the FIAS component. They would serve as focal points for training
the trainers, executing farmer training programs and conducting training programs for
rayon, oblast and federal FIAS and MIS staff. It is expected that these centers would
eventually become models for the development of new methodologies for farmer and
market information development and dissemination, skills development and would act as
a clearing house for the exchange of ideas and experience.



25
(f)    Pilot Programs: The project will test on a pilot basis: (i) development of proven Inter-
Active Video Disc Technology (IVDT) as an effective instruction tool for analyzing
various agricultural business options and this is already being done by the Institute of
Cybernetics (IC) at Pskov Oblast; (ii) the development and dissemination of location
specific environmental information; and (iii) bring experts (both Russian and foreign)
to the field through an electronic network to exchange information and advise on various
agricultural topics.
80.    Institutional Development.
(a)    Training. The project would support training of about 450 agricultural staff for the FIAS
centers at the federal, oblast and rayon levels; and about 6500 end-users representing a
wide range of emerging farm organizations and agro-business enterprises. Training for
the FIAS staff would consist of information needs assessment, information sourcing,
message development, packaging for multi-media, data collection techniques, data
analysis and presentation, computer software development, database design and
communicatimn systems and communication skills. Training for the end-users would
cover a wide range of topics, such as, how to start and operate a business, assessment
of business risks, keeping simple accounts, finance and management, project evaluation,
marketing, agricultural practices, legal and environmental aspects. The domestic training
will involve the local institutions supplemented by foreign experts, who will train the
trainers on topics related to farmer information development and dissemination, including
farm and enterprise management. The foreign training will consist of study tours to
selected foreign countries and short training programs in information development and
dissemination (see Annex 7 for further details).
(b)    Technical Assistance. The project would provide necessary technical assistance for
(i) the development of appropriate computer software for the FIAS component; (ii)
development of a transparent framework for a variety of international contractual
arrangements connected with the use of international copyright information used in the
FIAS component; and (iii) development of appropriate farmer information packages and
dissemination (see Annex 8 for futher details).
Geographical Coverage
81.     The general information developed by the FIAS component will be disseminated nationwide
using mass media. Client oriented location specific information and advisory services will be provided
through the FIAS centers located in 30 oblasts and in about 125 rayons in the major agricultural regions
of the country. The geographical coverage of the FIAS component is provided in Map No. IBRD 25150.
E. SEED INDusrRy DEVELOPMENT
Objectives
82.    The objectives of the seed industry development component are twofold: (a) to establish "pilot"
seed production, processing and marketing enterprises for hybrid corn and sunflower seeds in order to
demonstrate the commercial viability of modern seed industry; and (b) to establish an enabling policy and
institutional framework for the development of a competitive seed industry in the private sector.



26
Concept
83.    Crop yields in Russia are generally lower than those in ecologically similar areas of Europe and
North America. One of the main reasons for low yields is limited availability of quality inputs, of which
seed is critical. There are major problems with the seed sector in Russia. These are: (i) lack of
appropriate national seed policy, quality controls and laws; (ii) a widening gap between research and on-
farm applications; (iii) inefficient seed production, processing and treatment; (iv) a poor system of seed
storage and distribution; (v) lack of seed marketing system; (vi) poor quality and inefficient usage of
icds at the farn level; and (vii) lack of competition and limited inicentives to establish modem seed
industry in the private sector.
84.     The key features of the seed industry development component are (a) to change the orientation
of MOAF institutions to phase out direct participation in the commercial seed enterprises; (b) to establish
"pilot," seed enterprises to produce and market hybrid corn and sunflower seeds of international quality;
and (c) to strengthen the legal framework of key MOAF institutions to test and register new cultivars
(including those originating abroad), to provide laboratory and certification services to enterprises for
quality control, and to regulate the marketing of quality seeds. The pilot units will be owned by legally
registered, private joint-stock companies (some are recently privatized state and collective seed farms and
seed processing entities, or restructured farms originally attached to crop research institutes). The pilot
enterprises will be established in selected southern administrative regions which have comparative
advantage for the production of hybrid corn and sunflower seeds as well as the necessary experience in
seed production and processing.
Detailed Features
85.    The seed component has two sub-components. The first deals with the development of "pilot"
seed enterprises, and the second deals with institutional development, with primary focus on legal, policy,
institudonal and regulatory issues.
86.     Investment in Seed Enterprises. The development of seed enterprises will focus on the
establishment of commercially viable "pilot" units that would integrate the production and distribution
of seeds into one management structure. Sub-loans will be provided to eligible private enterprises after
they submit detailed investment proposals (which are subject to approval by the government and review
and clearance by the World Bank) that document the technical, organizational and financial feasibility of
the project. Three feasibility studies completed thus far indicate that the domestic cost of producing
quality (international standard) hybrid corn seed with facilities using imported equipment is lower than
the prevailing c.i.f. price for imported seed of comparable quality, and that the operations are profitable
without any subsidies. 'fo ensure that the project enterprises are not undermined by subsidized seed
imports and pricing policies, the Russian Govermnent has agreed to eliminate subsidies on the imported
or domestically produced corn and sunflower seeds and to liberalize seed prices. This commitment of
the government has been conveyed to the Bank through an official letter from the Minister of Finance.
87.     This project will provide sub-loans to participating private seed enterprises to build eight new
hybrid corn and two new hybrid sunflower seed processing facilities. On the average, each hybrid corn
seed processing unit of 5,000-ton capacity will cost approximately US$13 million, and each sunflower
seed plant of 2,000-ton capacity will cost approximately US$7 million. The new processing plants will
be designed to receive raw seed directly from the combine harvesters, and will include appropriate
facilities for receiving, drying, bulk storage, cleaning, grading, treating, packaging and storage of bagged
seed. In addition to the imnported seed processing equipment, new plant designs will be introduced that
will significantly improve seed quality, increase processing efficiency and lower production cost. Some



27
enterprises will purchase specialized field equipment to provide custom services for the contract seed
growers. New management approaches will be instituted at the enterprise level to improve the overall
technical and financial performance of the enterprises. This would include, amnong others, the preparation
of annual business plans, the establishment of marketing departments, activity-based accounting and initial
steps to begin new product development (variety testing and development, possibly in association with
foreign seed companies).
88.     About 27 enterprises have indicated their interest in being included in the project. Out of these,
ten enterprises have initiated feasibility studies for the establishment of new seed facilities. These
enterprises are legally registered as private joint stock companies and are recently privatized state or
collective farms and seed processing entities or privatized seed farms which were formerly attached to
research institutes. This initial group of potential participants will increase during project implementation.
Three seed enterprises have completed their feasibility studies and these sub-projects were considered
viable (during appraisal) for financing by the project. These three enterprises are: (i) Kubangibrid
(BARI) Seed Company in Krasnodar Kray; (ii) Kadgaron Seed Company in North Ossetia; and (iii)
Zolotoi Pochathok Seed Company in Voronezh Oblast.
89.     The ten "p.-ot" enterprises will provide sufficient experience and demonstrative impact in the
primary seed production zones of Russia and will lay the foundation for commercial seed production in
the future. The proposed investments wil! result in an annual additional capacity of 40,000 tons of hybrid
corn seed and 4,000 tons of hybrid sunflower seed by 1997. Financial analysis of the three corn seed
sub-projects indicate a rate of return of 12 percent in real terms (after paying interest charges on short
and long-term loans) at seed prices of approximately US$1100-1200 per ton. These prices are about 75
percent of the current cost of imported hybrid corn seed.
90.     Demand for high quality hybrid corn and sunflower seed is high and unsatisfied. At present,
farmers are willing to pay a premium for high quality seeds. Moreover, crop budgets indicate the
farmers' ability to pay substantially higher prices for high quality seed, especially since domestic grain
prices have increased significantly. Domestic corn grain prices range between between US$90-100 per
ton. At a typical hybrid seed-to-grain price ratio of 14:1 for hybrid corn seed, a hybrid corn seed price
could be between US$1260-1400 per ton. Prices of US$1100-1200 are justified by the feasibility studies
of three corn seed enterprises.
91.     All investments in seed enterprises will be carefully monitored by the PDU. The enterprises
receiving sub-loans will be assisted, as necessary, by the PDU. Any actions necessary to improve the
profitability of the enterprise will be recommended. The project will finance critical civil works and
imported equipment, including shellers, dryers, cleaners, sizing and grading units, gravity separators,
treaters, cyclone dust collectors, automatic weighing machines, precision planters, sprayers, harvesters
and de-tasselers. The criteria for selection of private seed enterprises for participation in the project and
eligibility for Bank financing are outlined in Annex 6.
92.     Institutional Development. A law for the protection of plant breeders' rights was enacted in
August 1993, and a basic seed law covering seed quality and seed marketing issues has been drafted and
is under review. The project will support the legislative process to ensure the compatibility of seed law
with recognized, international standards and conventions. A policy dialogue between the government and
emerging private sectors will be encouraged and institutionalized. Finally, institutions (such as
Gossortkomissia and Gosseminspektsia) charged with the implementation of reforms, as well as providing
key services to the seed enterprises, will be strengthened.



28
93.     The project will support the establishment of a National Seed Advisory Committee (NSAC),
with representatives from both the public and private sectors. The NSAC will advise the Minister of
Agriculture and Food on the development of a national seed policy, including the definition of the future
role of the MOAF in the development of a rnodern seed industry, legislation and accompanying
regulations, positive role of joint ventures, and other matters critical to the development of a modem seed
industry in Russia. In addition, the project will support the establishment of a non-governmental National
Seed Association (NSA). The NSA will provide a number of services to its members, including
information services, representation in different forums (domestic and international), and membership in
international seed trade associations such as the Federation of International Trade (also known as the
Federation Internationale du Commerce des Semences, or FICS).
94.       The project will enhance the capacity of the Russian Variety Registration Agency
(Gossortkomissia) to register crop varieties and hybrids for commercial seed production based on reliable
and timely trials and test results. Clients of Gossortkomissia include research institutes and seed
enterprises, both domestic and international. The project will upgrade equipment required for registration
and testing; provide specialized technical training to selected Gossortkomissia staff; and support
interaction with the international bodies involved with variety registrations (such as International Union
for Protection of Plant Varieties, UPOV).
95.     The project will strengthen the Russia Seed Inspection Agency (Gosseminspektsia) to provide
a range of services to the existing seed agencies and emerging seed enterprises. Gosseminspektsia will
help to assure that seed produced and sold within Russia conform to recogpized international standards
through field inspections and laboratory testing. Specific project interventions include international
training in seed certification programs and related technical skills. The project will provide laboratory
and field equipment to enhance the capacity and quality of results, and support the active membership
of Gosseminspektsia in the Organization for Economic Cooperation and Development (OECD) and the
International Seed Testing Association (ISTA).
(a)    Training.  The training program for the investment sub-component will have two
principal audiences, the key management staff of participating seed enterprises and
investment analysts that assist in the preparation of feasibility studies. Eleven training
activities are planned, including visits for ongoing seed enterprises to study the
management and marketing aspects of the seed business, two courses in accounting, four
training programs in contract seed grower management and other technical topics, and
three programs to cover the subjects of strategic planning, project identification, project
prioritization and the preparation of feasibility studies. Training will be also provided
to policy makers and technical personnel of the MOAF Seed Division, Gossortkomissia
and Gosseminspektsia. Members of the NSAC will study current models of seed industry
organization and government policy in other countries in order to assist in the
development of a national seed policy in Russia. The technical personnel will be trained
(both in Russia and foreign countries) to upgrade their skills in their respective fields of
specialization (see Annex 7 for fiurther details).
(b)    Technical Assistance. The seed indLstry model to be implemented through this project
is new and the requisite expertise to manage this pilot activity is not available within
Russia. This is due to the fact that the investments entail imports of modern technology
(seed processing equipment) and the establishment of vertically integrated seed enterprises
that comprise activities from seed research and development to seed marketing. Both the
equipment and the management structure represent innovative reforms to the seed sector
for which local expertise is not available. The pilot nature of this activity necessitates the



29
provision of technical services to seed enterprises. The technical assistance will support
specialists to advise private entrepreneurs, evaluate the technical and financial risks and
screen potential investments. Technical assistance will also be provided to help
implement the plant breeders' rights legislation, to advise Gosseminspektsia on the
administration of the certification and seed testing programs, to help define the function
and role of the NSAC, and to help organize NSA by adopting successful organizational
models from other countries (see Annex 8 for further details).
Geographical Coverage
96.    Policy aspects of the project would cover the entire Russian Federation. The crop variety testing
and registration work will be supported in all the principal production areas where seeds will be
marketed. The strengthening of seed inspection would be limited to the areas where seed enterprises will
be established. The project area for the investment activities will be limited to the southern administrative
regions, including Krasnodar and Stavropol krays, Rostov, Belgorod, the southern part of Volgograd and
Voronezh oblasts, and such other regions as mnay be agreed between the Government and the Bank during
implementation. (see Map No. IBRD 25151).
F. PRoJECr DEVELOPMENT UNIT
Objective
97.    The objectives of the Project Development Unit (PDU) are two-fold: (i) to provide the Ministry
of Agriculture and Food with critically needed institutional support to implement the ARIS project; and
(ii) to develop much needed analytical capacity to prepare investment projects in. a fast changing
agricultural environment for financing by international financial institutions. App.opriate equipment,
training and technical assistance would be provided to the MOAF for undertaking this effort.
Concept
98.     Detailed review of World Bank projects in FSU countries indicates that implementation issues
are of paramount importance as structural changes bring about the need for new tpes of skills and
institutional capacity. In order for the ARIS project to be successfully implemented, it is crucial that
adequate and appropriate implementation capacity be developed in the MOAF. Adequate training and
technical assistance will be provided in those areas which are relatively new to the MOAF staff. The
ARIS project would support the establishment of a PDU to help accomplish the project's main goals.
The proposed Unit is expected to facilitate project implementation and project preparation.
Components
99.    The PDU has already been established within MOAF and will consist of two departments: (a)
the Project inplementation Department (PID) which will be responsible for coordinating project
implementation; and (b) Project Preparation Department (PPD) which will be responsible for developing
analytical capcity for preparing future agricultural projects. The PID will coordinate implementation with
the Component Implementation Units (CIUs) in MOAF, Moscow City, Moscow Oblast, Belgorod Oblast,
other participating regions and the participating private marketing and seed enterprises. These CIUs will
implement individual project components under the supervision of the PDU.



30
V
COST ESTIMATES AND FINANCIAL ASPECTS
A. COST ESTIMATES
Total Project Cost
100.   The estimated total project cost, including contingencies and applicable import duties, is US$325
million, of which 74 percent is foreign exchange cost. The total project cost, net of duties and taxes, is
about US$315 million, of which 76 percent is foreign exchange cost. Payable VAT is not included in
the project cost since it is refundable within about one year. Duties on the applicable imported equipment
and machinery are included as part of the local project cost. The share of local costs in total project costs
is low primarily due to the present undervalued exchange rate and hence low implied real resource cost
of local contributions. The respective shares of the project components in total project cost are 33 percent
for market infrastructure development, 20 percent for farm information and advisory services, 45 percent
for seed industry development and 2 percent for the PDU. The estimated cost of individual project
components and cost categories is reported in Table 5. 1.
Basis of Project Cost Estimates
101.   The base cost estimates were derived from mission analysis (in consultation with local
counterparts), surveys, feasibility studies and preparation reports. Price contingencies of 2.8 percent per
annum (compounded) were applied to all costs expressed in US dollars according to the average annual
price increase projected by the World Bank between 1992 and 2002. The physical contingencies are
assumed to be ten percent of the base costs for civil works and equipment in line with established Bank
practice and experience with similar projects worldwide. The detailed costs and approach for deriving
project cost estimates for individual project and cost components are provided in Annex 9.
B. FINANCING PLAN
Financing Sources
102.    The proposed Bank Loan of US$240 million would finance 100 percent of foreign exchange
costs of the project. The Bank Loan to the Russian Federation would have a maturity of 17 years,
including 5 years grace, at the Bank's standard variable interest rate. The Federal C-overnment, through
the MOAF, would finance all local costs including payment of applicable import duties and taxes, such
as VAT for the public sector components and sub-conponents. Similarly, the participating private
enterprises would finance local costs, including applicable duties and taxes, for private sector components
and sub-components. The proposed financing plan has been presented in Table 5.2. It was agreed during
negotiations, at the request of the Russian Delegation, to include a provision for retroactive financing in
the amount of up to US$1.0 million to cover expenditures under eligible categories beginning on June
1, 1994. The eligibility of such expenditures for reimbursement will be subject to the same conditions
as for other project expenditures, including procurement.

Counterp-.it Funds
103.   Sustainability of the project depends, to a large extent, on the availability of budgetary resources
to finance local costs. The counterpart funds would be provided by the Federal Government through the
MOAF, and by participating private enterprises to finance local costs associated with the project. The
Bank has obtained written assurances from the Government that MOAF was given approval by the
Ministry of Finance and other concerned ministries (and by participating private enterprises) on
satisfactory arrangements for securing funds, including budgetary allocations for programs to be
implemented during the first year of project implementation and a commitment for budgetary support in
subsequent years. Expenditures at the oblast, rayon and city level would be supported by the MOAF
which would allocate the necessary funds during the annual budget process. The World Bank has also
obtained assurances during negotiations that the Ministry of Finance intends to introduce, beginning in
FY95, a line item in the Federal Budget for the ARIS project; all special budgetary allocations would be
restricted for the sole use of the ARIS project; and that they would be carried forward to a subsequent
year when not completely used within the year of allocation. Furthermore, the Project Account to receive
counterpart funds, exclusively to finance local expenditures for the ARIS project, will be established (with
an initial deposit of at least US$1.0 million equivalent) in a commercial bank on terms and conditions
acceptable to the Bank prior to loan effectiveness. The participating private enterprises would cover all
of the local expenditure which could be between 15-35 percent of the total cost for individual sub-
projects.
Cofinancing
104.    Training and technical assistance components are about 8 percent of the total project cost. If
the Government of the Russian Federation is successful in obtaining other bilateral grant funds for this
purpose, then the use of Bank funds for this purpose would either be reduced correspondingly or
reallocated to other components following appropriate review and approval by the World Bank.
C. SPECLAL AccouNT
105.     The Special Account (SA) would be maintained in a commercial bank selected by the
Government and acceptable to the World Bank. This bank would be selected, from a number of
acceptable banks, on the basis of competitive bids. Selection criteria for participation in the bids are: (i)
the bank should be financially sound as demonstrated by their latest accounts, as audited by an
internationally recognized accounting firm; (ii) the bank should have a significant foreign correspondence
network covering all major currencies; (iii) the bank should have a reasonable capacity and experience
for issuing letters of credit, for making direct foreign payments and other international transactions, with
appropriate arrangements for the training and development of their staff; (iv) the bank should be capable



33
of performing a wide range of local banking services, including cash payments, transfers to other
domestic banks, issuance of debit notes, application of conversion rates from foreign currencies, and
maintenance of adequate accounts for the SA; (v) the bank should preferably be a member of SWIFT
(Society for Worldwide Interbank Financial Telecommunications) and should routinely use or plan to use
this facility to expedite payment transfers; (vi) the bank should be willing to issue a Comfort Letter to
assure that amounts deposited in a Special Account will not be set off or otherwise seized or attached to
satisfy amounts due to the commercial bank by the borrower; (vii) the bank should be willing to maintain
adequate accounts as required by the World Bank, provide monthly bank statements to the World Bank
and the PDU, and any other information considered necessary (copies of all transactions would be
routinely submitted to the PDU); and (viii) the bank should be willing to charge reasonable rates for their
services and provide reasonable interest on balances held from time to time. Broadly, the same criteria
would apply to select a comnmercial bank which would serve as an agency bank to manage the sub-loan
accounts. It would be preferable to have one bank manage both the Special Account and the sub-loan
accounts. Agreement on the selection procedures for a commercial bank to manage the Special Account
was reached during negotiations.
106.   The maximum authorized allocation of the SA will be US$1.0 million. The overall authorized
allocation will, however, depend on the short-term need of the project. The account will be opened in
the name of the Borrower (MOAF will provide specific names of personnel authorized to withdraw funds
from the Special Account) and would be administered by the PDU which will send requests to the World
Bank for replenishment of the Special Account. These requests would be accompanied by detailed
monthly bank statements of the Special Account.
D. FLOW OF FUNDS
107.    The proceeds of the loan would be channelled in two ways. First, funds to the federal
government agencies would be channelled as special line items through the budgetary process. Second,
due to lack of established financial intermediation mechanisms and the "pilot" nature of sub-projects, the
funds for financing investments by the participating private seed and marketing enterprises will be
channelled by the Government, under agency agreements acceptable to the World Bank.
108.   Under this second arrangement, the commercial banks would serve as agency banks primarily
to maintain accounts and recover these sub-loans on behalf of the Government and will be paid a
commission to perform these functions. These banks would be selected on the basis of competitive bids.
The Federal Government would bear the default risk. The PDU would be responsible for the appraisal
of all sub-projects which are subject to review and clearance by the World Bank. However, at least the
first three sub-projects for each of the seed and marketing enterprises will be appraised by the Bank. The
Project Coordination Committee (PCC) would approve all sub-projects for Bank financing upon the
recommendation of PDU and concurrence by the Bank. These on-lending arrangements are different
from those under the proposed Enterprise Support Project because of the special needs of the agricultural
sector and the "pilot" character of the investments involved. The Federal government and enterprises
will enter into a sub-loan agreement outlining the terms and conditions of sub-loans and cost recovery
arrangements. These arrangements were agreed during negotiations.
109.  The majority of the World Bank's financing would be used for payments to foreign suppliers of
goods and services, either through the Special Account or direct from the World Bank. Where the World
Bank fliances locally incurred expenditures, this would be on reimbursable basis. The participating
private enterprise and/or the government agency would first incur and make payment of the expenditure,
submit withdrawal applications to the PDU, which would initiate reimbursement from the Special Account
for the portion of the expenditure to be financed by the World Bank under the project.



34
110.  The PDU would include, in its accounting arrangements, procedures for recording all financing
provided by the World Bank whether as sub-loans to individual participating private enterprises and/or
to government agencies. The amounts to be recovered under the project would form sub-loans which are
discussed in the following section on "on-lending". The PDU, on behalf of the Government, would enter
into agency agreements with selected commercial banks and participating private enterprises, acceptable
to the World Bank, to monitor and recover repayment (principal plus interest) of these sub-loans for a
commnission to be determined during implementation.
111.   The Government's share of financing would be limited to the expenditures incurred by its own
agencies in the MOAF and agricultural departments of the participating oblast and rayons. This would
be financed directly from their budgets and in accordance with their normal procedures, with appropriate
records maintained for the Project and reported in their regular quarterly accounts/reports. The
expenditure to be financed by the participating private enterprises would be financed directly by them,
some of which would be reimbursed by the World Bank, as per disbursement guidelines. These
enterprises would also be required to maintain appropriate records subject to audit and included in their
regular quarterly accounts/reports.
E. ON-LENDiNG TERMS
112.    The prevailing macroeconomic instability makes it very difficult to devise appropriate rubkr-
based on-lending terms. Therefore, the Bank and the Ministry of Finance have agreed that the sub-o'ains
to participating private enterprises will be in US dollars. The interest rate to sub-borrowers would be
the Bank's standard variable interest rate (the base rate) plus a margin of 2.5 percent. This rate will cover
administrative costs, including commission to commercial banks for processing recovery of subloans, and
the cross-currency exchange and the default risks. These sub-loans to the participating private enterprises
would be for a maturity period of 10 years, including 2 years grace. Since the amount outstanding and
the repayment would be in US dollars, the foreign exchange risk (between the US dollar and the ruble)
would be borne by the sub-borrowers. However, the Federal Government would bear the cross-currency
risk between the basket of currencies in Bank loan and the US dollar.  In the event that there is
insufficient demand for project funds allocated for investment sub-projects, the portion of the loan for
the investment components would be utilized either for similar investments that are consistent with project
concepts and covenants or, if this is not feasible, would be cancelled and would not be reallocated to
other comnponents. Investment sub-projects would be subject to sensitivity analysis to test the viability
under various assumptions about the foreign exchange rate, interest rate and outpu.t price.
113.   A number of "pilot" commercial enterprises would be sub-borrowers under the marketing and
seed components. These enterprises must meet the agreed selection criteria and must be commercially
viable based on feasibility studies (see Annex 6). The financing arrangements for the participating private
enterprises would be as follows:
e   The feasibility studies for potential sub-projects will be prepared as per World Bank
guidelines which have already been discussed with potential sub-borrowers. These feasibility
studies will be appraised by the PDU and will be reviewed by independent experts, including
physical inspections and other field work. However, at least the first three sub-projects for
each of the seed and marketing enterprises would be appraised by the Bank. The selected
proposals will be recommended by the PDU for approval by the PCC (which will include
representatives, inter alia, from MOAF, the Ministry of Finance and the Ministry of
Economy) and concurrence by the World Bank. These arrangements were agreed during
negotiations.



35
0   Sub-loan agreements would be concluded between the Govermnent (the MOAF and the
Ministry of Finance) and each of the participating private enterprises. The (PDU),
representing the MOAF as a participant in the sub-project, would countersign the agreement,
ensuring appropriate arrangements for carrying out its responsibilities for monitoring
compliance with contractual provisions on the private enterprises' use of sub-loan and
repayment. These arrangements were agreed during negotiations.
O   The principal amount of financing for a given sub-loan would be calculated in US dollars on
the basis of the current exchange rate, on the date of payment of each eligible expenditure.
Repayment of principal and interest would also be made in U.S. dollars or in ruble
equivalent amount based on the foreign exchange rate prevailing on the repayment date.
*   Repayment schedule for sub-loans would be established to reflect the private enterprises'
ability to repay the sub-loans, but would not exceed 10 years, including a two-year grace
period. Interest would accrue from date of disbursement and repayments would be required
on a quarterly basis.
*   Repayment of principal and interest would be made by the enterprises and would be
deposited for credit through an agreed account in a local bank to be designated by the MOF.
*   Any surplus reflows (the difference between borrower's repayment obligations to the Bank
and sub-borrowers' repayments to the borrower) will be used on a revolving basis, and on
similar terms and conditions, to finance similar private sub-projects acceptable to the Bank.
*   Collateral (in the form of assets) would be required from the participating private enterprises
in favor of the government as a preferred creditor and would be included in the sub-loan
agreement.
o   During negotiations, the Russian Delegation assured the Bank that legal and administrative
decisions to make sub-loans to private enterprises could be taken on the basis of adequate
existing legal capacity possessed by the Government.
114.   The participating private enterprises are expected to be able to bear the foreign exchange risk
even though their output (seed, for example) will be sold in local currency. This is because the project
seed enterprises are expected to be commercially viable at liberalized seed prices and seed would remain
in short supply even after the project-financed seed enterprises are fully operational.
F. Cosr REcovERY
115.  The proposed ARIS project wi d finance activities in the public and private sectors. The
participating private enterprises as "pilo sub-projects would account for about 60 percent of the loan
proceeds. These funds would be on-lent by the government to these enterprises. These sub-loans would
be repaid with interest to the government within the agreed term of the sub-loan which generally will not
exceed 10 years.
116.  The remaining 40 percent of loan proceeds would finance much needed public sector activities
to develop and disseminate information to private farmers, restructured farms and agro-business
enterprises and institutional support to private seed industry. The project would introduce the concept
of "fee for service' for selected services. The immediate goal of the fees will not be full cost recovery,
but rather establishment of the "fee for service" principle. Fee will be set at a level so as not to



36
discourage farmers from seeking information and advisory services and no fees will be charged initially.
Ultimately, the goal is to be able to recover 100 percent of incremental operating cost for selected
activities.
117.  Agreement was reached with the Government during negotiations on the following three cost
recovery principles for services provided by the public sector. These are: (i) the establishment of fee
schedule for selected services in 1995; (ii) introduction of fee for service and other appropriate cost
recovery measures in 1996; and (iii) the establishment, operation and use of a "fund" in the MOAF from
resources collected through fees or other cost recovery measures in 1996. The details would be worked
out during project implementation. Progress and plans for cost recovery would be part of mid-term
review of the project. The Bank will work with the Government to design a cost recovery strategy in
order to generate adequate resources to sustain the project work.
G. PROCUREMNT
118.  The procurement of all goods funded from the loan proceeds would be in accordance with the
Bank's "Guidelines for Procurement Under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits (May 1992)". Local goods
would be eligible for a preference as provided in the Procurement Guidelines. The Bank's standard
bidding documents for the Procurement of Goods and the Construction of (Smaller) Works will be used
for International Competitive Bidding (ICB), Limited International Bidding (LIB) and Local Competitive
Bidding (LCB). Consultants for training and technical assistance would be employed in accordance with
the Bank's Guidelines "Use of Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and the World Bank as Executing
Agency (August 1981)". Details of the, procurement arrangements and procurement agencies are provided
in Annex 10. The methods of procurement, proposed for the ARIS project, are summarized in Table 5.3.
119.  Civil Works. Construction would consist of a large number of market facilities. US$6.6 million
would be bid via ICB. US$18.0 million is for construction of many scattered works with contract values
between US$100,000 and US$1 million by various enterprises which would be let through local
competitive procedures satisfactory to the Bank, and US$0.3 million would be contracted through local
shopping with at least three quotations. All civil works and turnkey contracts for seed processing plants,
consisting of US$7.2 million in works and US$83.8 million in equipment, would be bid by ICB and
prequalification would be used. LCB would also be subject to prequalification. Renovation works
(US$4.4 million) consist of repair to numerous government offices which would be carried out by local
public works agencies with US$1.2 million by force account financed by the agencies and imported
inputs, US$3.2 million (fixtures, soundproofing, security devices), acquired through International
Shopping.
120.  Goods. Computer and communication equipment costing US$47.3 million would be acquired
through ICB. Turnkey equipment, valued at US$83.8 equipment, would be included with the turnkey
civil works and obtained via ICB. Equipment and machinery would include a variety of items for the
MIS, FIAS, and Seed components and would total US$61.9 million, of which US$44.2 million would
be procured under ICB, US$10.2 million would be procured by LIB, US$4.4 million by IS, US$0.1
million could be direct purchase, and US$3.0 million by the enterprises with their own funds. These
items would be bulked to the extent feasible but as they would be distributed among various enterprises
and required in different years of the implementation period, these purchases will be below the limit for
ICB. Materials inputs for US$7.3 million would be purchased through IS by the several market
enterprises throughout the implementation period.
121.  Procurement Thresholds. Civil works contracts expected to exceed US$1.0 million would be
let through ICB. Civil works contracts between US$100,000 and US$1 million would be let through



37
local competitive procedures which would be satisfactory to the Bank and Local Shopping would be used
below US$100,000. Equipment would be bulked to the maximum practical extent and packages greater
than US$0.5 million would be bid by ICB. Equipment packages between US$300,000 and US$500,000
would be contracted with LIB; below US$300,000 International Shopping would be used with three
quotations from three eligible countries. Direct equipment procurement for the FIAS component up to
US$100,000 would be permitted.

38
122.  Training. Training would consist of assistance from foreign and national institutions, with visits
abroad, as well as local training assisted by foreign trainers. The terms of reference and the conditions
of employment would be satisfactory to the Bank. Further details are contained in Annex 7.
123.  Technical Assistance. Technical assistance would include short-term consulting assignments, and
longer-term assignments as well as staff for the PDU and ClUs which would be selected in accordance
with procedures agreed with the Bank. Long-term assignments would be obtained via competitive
proposals from consultant firms. Short assignments of a month or two would be met by consultants
recruited individually or from the consultant firms as proved most suitable to requirements. Selection
would be made according to the Bank Consultant Guidelines. The terms of reference and the conditions
of employment would be satisfactory to the Bank. Further details are contained in Annex 8.
124.  Prior Review. Procurement documentation for all contracts such as ICB, LIB and Direct
Contract (DC) contracts as well as first two LCB packages under each component and all contracts in
excess of US$500,000 for works, would be subject to prior review. This is estimated to cover 90 percent
of the total procurement value and could entail 150 contract awards, of which half may be for computer
and communications equipment. The other contracts financed from the loan funds would be subject to
selected post review during the supervision of the project. The bidding documents would use the Bank's
standard bidding documents for all contracts financed by the loan proceeds. All consultant services will
be procured with Bank's prior review of complete documentation and Bank's standard docum.entation will
be used.
125. Procurement Arrangements. The PDU would be responsible for the overall supervision of
procurement under the project. The PDU would have procurement personnel and technical experts to
advise and assist in project procurement and project implementation Government departments and public
agencies would use a selected procurement agent for procurement. Each of the private enterprises would
also be required to use a qualified procurement agent. During  e mid-term review of the project, the
continued use of the procurement agents would be assessed i. the light of experience gained and the
procurement capabilities developed by the component implementation units and the PDU.   Further
details on project procurement arrangements and monitoring are provided in Annex 10.
126.  The procurement agent for the public agencies would be selected from a short list prepared by
the PDU in accordance with the Bank's Guidelines. The PDU would prepare a longer list of eight to
twelve qualified procurement firms for selecting procurement agents by the private enterprises. From
this list, selection will be made in accordance with criteria of qualifications and price in the invitation for
proposals. The selection would be based upon experience and administrative capacity, in combination
with fee levels. The fees of the procurement agents would be eligible for disbursement from the loan
proceeds.
H. DISBURSEMENT
127.    The project is expected to be completed within a five-year period, with the completion date
being December 31, 1999. This disbursement profile is based on Bank experience in financing similar
projects in other countries. The World Bank funds would be disbursed either directly or through the
Special Account on the basis of "Withdrawal Requests" signed by authorized signatories under the control
of the Project Development Unit.
128. The World Bank's financing would follow the normal World Bank disbursement procedures as
summarized in the Disbursement Handbook. Loan proceeds would be disbursed against eligible project
expenditures, either (a) directly to suppliers; (b) by means of special commitments to support letters of



39
credit issued by the borrower's bank in favor of foreign suppliers of goods; or (c) by way of
reimbursement to participating private enterprises/agencies where expenditures have already been
incurred.
129.   Disbursements would be made against eligible project expenditures for: (a) 100 percent of the
contract amount for the turnkey projects (which will cover equipment, installation and related civil work;
(b) 30 percent of expenditure on civil works; (c) 100 percent of foreign expenditures on goods, 100
percent of local expenditure on goods (ex-factory) and 75 percent of other items procured locally; and
(d) 100 percent of expenditures on consultant services, training, and technical assistance and procurement
services. Disbursements for contracts of or above US$100,000 equivalent would be made against full
documentation, and for those less than this amount will be made against Statements of Expenditures
(SOEs). Eligible goods, works and services procured after June 1, 1994 would qualify for retroactive
financing in an amount up to US$1.0 million. Full documentation in support of SOEs would be retained
by the implementing agencies for at least two years after disbursement. This information would be
available for review by the World Bank staff during supervision and the auditors. The estimated
disbursements of IBRD loan are presented in Table 5.4.
