I. ACCOUNTING
130.    The Project would have an accounting and control system capable of reliably recording and
reporting all financial transactions. An appropriate accounting and control system would be put in place
by the PDU. This system must be operational when project expenditures begin. For the use of
Statements of Expenditure, the PDU would process and maintain SOE documentation to be satisfactory
to the World Bank. For the Special Account mechanism, the PDU and the depository commercial bank
would introduce arrangements for proper accounting of receipts, payments and submission of
disbursement documentation to the World Bank for replenishment of the account.



40
J. REPORTING
131.   The World Bank requires the financial performance and status of a project and borrower to be
monitored regularly during implementation. As a result, the Project Development Unit would provide
quarterly financial reports to the World Bank throughout the monitoring period. These financial reports
would be classified into two categories: project specific and participating agencies.
132.    The quarterly and annual accounts for the project will report on the cash receipts and
expenditures related to each component and the overall financial performance of the project. The detailed
accounts for the project would not only include expenditures directly financed by the World Bank, but
also the expenditures financed by the participating private agencies and the government agencies. These
reports would reflect actual activity versus planned expenditure for the most recent fiscal quarter, fiscal
year-to-date and project inception-to-date. The format and individual classification of receipts and
expenditures has been designed and was agreed upon between the Russian Government and the World
Bank (see Annex 11) during negotiations and will be distributed to the accounting officer in the PDU and
to the accountants in the individual component implementation units.
133.   As SOEs and a SA will be used in this project, the PDU would be requested to submit, together
with accounts for the projects, on a quarterly basis: (i) a schedule of SOE expenditures and amounts, and
(ii) a summary of the SA account activity and reconciliation withi the SA balance in the commercial bank
account and the project account. All uwiudited quarterly reports are to be submitted to reach the World
Bank no later than 30 days after the end of the quarter.
134.    The annual financial statements that will be required of participating private enterprises will
comprise a balance sheet, a statement of income and expenditures and a statement of sources and
applications of funds. The financial statements would be prepared by the participating pr; 'ate enterprises
on an annual basis, following the generally accepted accounting principles as prescribed oy Russian law,
together with any additional information that may be required by the World Bank.
135.   In addition, the participating private enterprises would provide to the PDU, on a quarterly basis,
unaudited accounts for the sub-projects summarizing the cash receipts and expenditures, by components
financed (e.g., civil works, equipment). The account will include not only the expenditures directly
financed by the World Bank, but also the expenditures financed by the participating private enterprises
themselves. The account for the sub-project would reflect activity for the most recent fiscal quarter,
fiscal year-to-date and project inception-to-date. The format and individual classification of this report
has also been designed and agreed upon between the Russian Government and the World Bank during
negotiations and will be distributed to the accounting officer of the individual participating private
enterprises and the accounting officer in the PDU. All unaudited quarterly reports are to be submitted
to reach the World Bank no later than 30 days after the end of the quarter.
136.   Quarterly project reports on the physical performance of the project showing actual and planned
performance for the quarter and accumulated to date would also be prepared and submitted to the Bank.
These reports, also submitted to reach the Bank no later than 30 days after the end of each quarter, would
include comments highlighting project progress and significant bottlenecks, showing an action plan for
remedial actions where necessary. Detailed reporting requirements would be finalized during the project
launch workshop and would include key physical and financial indicators. In addition, a comprehensive
joint mid-term review (MTR), would be carried out by the PDU and the Bank, within three months from
the end of 30 months from project effectiveness. Agreement on reporting procedures, accounts for the
project, financial statements and monitoring targets was reached during negotiations.



41
K. AuDrTNG
137.   Annual accounts for the project, including SOEs and SA for each fiscal year, would be audited
in accordance with generally accepted auditing standards by an independent auditor, acceptable to the
World Bank. The sub-borrowers would also provide, to the PDU, annual financial statements and
accounts for the sub-project which are audited in accordance with generally accepted auditing standards
by an independent auditor, acceptable to the World Bank.
138.   The auditors would be appointed by the borrower and sub-borrowers close to the beginning of
the fiscal year, so that the auditors may commence reviews sufficiently early in the fiscal year to complete
the audit on a timely basis. Terms of Reference for the auditors, acceptable to the World Bank, would
need to be prepared. The financial statements and the auditor's opinion and reports should be submitted
to the World Bank no later than six months after completion of the fiscal year. Agreement on auditing
requirements was reached during negotiations.
VI
PROJECT ORGANIZATION AND MANAGEMENT
A. PROJECT MANAGEMENT
Ministry of Agriculture and Food (MOAF)
139.   The MOAF is in charge of supervising the preparation and implementation of the ARIS project.
The MOAF, through its departments and agencies, will implement different components of the project
at the federal, oblast and rayon levels through appropriately organized and staffed Component
implementation Units (CIUs). It will work with the departments of agriculture at the oblast and rayon
levels. These departments are part of the oblast administrations. At the federal level, the project comes
under the jurisdiction of three Deputy Ministers. The market infrastructure development component,
except the MIS sub-component, will be implemented by the Deputy Minister responsible for agrarian
policy. However, the MIS component, which would be implemented by the Department of Prices and
Price Policy, will be directly under the supervision of the Minister of Agriculture and Food.  The
Farmer Information and Advisory Services component will be implemented by the Deputy Minister
responsible for science and education. Finally, the seed development component will be implemented
by the Deputy Minister responsible for agrarian production and technological policy.
Establishment of PDU
140.    Rationale. The ARIS project, involving multiple agencies at the federal, oblast and rayon
levels, requires a strong and independent project management unit for successful implementation. Given
the limited experience of the MOAF in the implementation of externally funded projects, coupled with
the need to provide on-the-job training to Russian experts, the Government has already established the
PDU to coordinate the management and implementation of the ARIS project.
141.   An interagency Project Coordination Committee (PCC) will be established to assist and advise
the Minister of Agriculture and Food (who will chair this Committee) to implement the ARIS project,
to approve applications for sub-loans to participating private enterprises and to establish priorities for
preparing agricultural projects. The PCC will comprise a membership representing, inter alia, MOAF,
the Ministry of Finance and the Ministry of Economy. This PCC would meet periodically to address
implementation problems that would require inter-ministerial coordination and intervention.



42
142.    Structure and Functions of the PDU. The Minister of Agriculture and Food, will be
responsible, through the PDU, for the management of the proposed ARIS Project. The PDU director
will report directly to the Minister of Agriculture and Food and will be empowered through its charter
to: (a) recruit highly qualified staff; (b) coordinate the implementation of the ARIS project; (c) open and
operate accounts in foreign currency; and (d) select and supervise professional consulting services. The
PDU has already been established, and the PDU director has been appointed, by an ordinance of the
Minister of Agriculture and Food.
143.    The PDU will consist of two departments: (i) Project Implementation Department (PID); and
(ii) Project Preparation Department (PPD). Each of these departments will be headed by a deputy
director whose selection and appointment would be the responsibility of the Minister, with the
concurrenee of the World Bank. The detailed organizational structure, functions, staffing plans,
recruitment of staff, operational procedures and authority of the PDU were agreed during negotiations
(see Annex 12 for details).
144.   The PID would coordinate the implementation of the ARIS project. It will have three sections
(Market, Information and Seed) which will deal with implementation of each of the three main ARIS
components. The PID staff, with sufficient technical experience and qualification, would coordinate and
monitor the implementation of various components and sub-components of the ARIS Project. Foreign
consultants would be engaged to provide necessary support for procurement, accounting and project
management. The procurement and accounting specialists would work with the Russian staff preparing
them to take full responsibility in these areas. The project management specialists would work with PID
Deputy Director and report to the PDU Director.
145.   The PPD would develop much needed analytical capacity in Russia for identifying and preparing
sub-projects under the ARIS Project and new agricultural projects for financing by the international
financial institutions. This department would consist of economists, financial analysts, technical
specialists, and experts in agricultural project management. The PPD would also engage staff/consultants
with specific technical expertise to meet specific needs. The foreign experts, with skills in these broad
areas, would work with the Russian staff to prepare priority projects dealing with different aspects of
agricultural development. The Bank will review the TORs and selection of consultants. The reports will
be jointly reviewed by the SLC and the Bank.
146.   PDU Staffing. The PDU would be headed by a competent director who has been selected and
appointed by the Minister of Agriculture and Food. The PDU will be staffed by both local and foreign
experts. The PDU would have a competent (i) Finance Officer with appropriate qualification and
experience in international accounting practices to be responsible for the introduction and management
of all financial and accounting matters required for project implementation and preparation; (ii)
Procurement Officer trained in the procurement procedures of the World Bank with the ability to train,
direct and guide procurement activities of all participating agencies associated with the implementation
of the ARIS Project; and (iii) Project Management Specialist with appropriate qualifications to manage
externally funded projects. These three appointments will require concurrence of the World Bank. The
PDU director has been appointed and the appointment of other key Russian PDU staff would be
completed prior to loan effectiveness.
147.   All the Russian PDU staff will be recruited locally. Deputation from the civil service will be
allowed but only on a temporary basis. Most of the staff appointments would be fixed-term and will be
designed to complete a specific assigmnent. This is essential to give the PDU the flexibility to change
its staff mix to reflect the agricultural project portfolio under preparation and implementation. All the
critical PDU staff will be expected to have a working knowledge of spoken and written English. The



43
local PDU staff would be supported by foreign experts who would also train Russian experts in project
implementation and preparation.
148.    The Minister of Agriculture and Food, in consultation with the PCC, will outline the
responsibilities and the decision-making process for project implementation. In other words, the
responsibilities of the PDU, the CIUs, the PCC and the Minister (who will also be the Chairman of the
PCC) will be clearly defined for effective implementation of the project.
B. PROJECT LMLEMENTATION
149.   The MOAF will be responsible for implementing the ARIS project. The PDU will coordinate
all the work related to budgeting, procurement, disbursement audit, reporting, monitoring/evaluation and
appraisal of all the sub-projects. Actual implementation of project components and sub-components will
be the responsibility of individual CIUs located within departments and agencies of MOAF and in local
government agencies. The CIUs will be established and their heads will be appointed prior to loan
effectiveness. The success of project implementation, therefore, depends on the working relations between
the PDU, the CIUs and the regional authorities or agencies for each of the three components. Details
on the organizational structure and staffing of agencies or units responsible for project imnplementation
are presented in Annex 12. MOAF shall take necessary steps to secure appropriate assurances for project
support from participating regional authorities. A brief description of various government agencies
involved in the implementation of individual components and sub-components is provided in Anmex 13.
Market Infrastructure Development Component
150.   Market Information System. The Department of Prices and Price Policy (DPP) in the MOAF
will have the primary responsibility for itnplementation of the MIS. DPP will report directly to the
Minister of Agriculture and Food and PDU. It will establish "Agricultural Marketing Information Unit,"
which would be responsible for day-to-day management of MIS. At the oblast and rayon levels, the local
departments of agriculture will also establish "Agricultural Marketing Information Units" to perform
similar functions.
151.   The MOAF Computer Center (MOAFCC) will implement all aspects related to the establiishment
of the computer network. MOAFCC will provide technical assistance on system configuration,
procurement of equipment, integration of systems, and development and installation of software, as
needed for all agencies under MOAF. They will provide maintenance and repair services to support
oblast and rayon centers. MOAFCC will develop a training program for computer operators at all levels.
Furthermore, at the oblast and rayon levels, the use of computers would be combined, as much as
possible, by MIS and FIAS, thereby reducing initial project and operational cost.
152.    Pilot Market Investments.  The market investment component comprises of three sub-
components. The Moscow City and Moscow and Belgorod Oblast sub-components would be implemented
by the three Component Implementation Units to be established by local governments. Implementation
Units in two participating regions would be established prior to loan effectiveness. Detailed feasibility
studies are being prepared by participating private enterprises with assistance from local design institutes
and independent local and foreign experts. The Bank preparation and appraisal missions have developed
indicative designs and layouts for major sub-projects in collaboration with concerned enterprises. The
PDU will: (a) review the sub-project feasibility studies; (b) arrange preparation of technical specifications
for each sub-project, coordinate with the PDU Procurement Officer for the procurement of imported
equipment, goods and services, and coordinate with the PDU Financial Officer for the payments, as
necessary; (c) coordinate the activities of the CIUs at Moscow City, Moscow Oblast and Belgorod Oblast



44
to ensure that procurement of local equipment and services, those funded by the Project, follow the World
Bank procurement guidelines; (d) coordinate with the "agency" bank activities related to accounting,
reporting and recovery of sub-loans; and (e) ensure that adequate accounting and reporting is maintained
to monitor the expenditures. The sub-projects approved by the PCC, in each of the three investment sub-
components, will be subject to prior review and concurrence by the Bank. The World Bank would
appraise at least the first three sub-projects dealing with private marketing enterprises.
Farmer Information and Advisory Services Component
153.   The primary responsibility for the implementation of the FIAS component is the MOAF. It has
designated the All-Russian Scientific Research Institute of Innovation and Marketing in Agriculture
(ASRIIMA) and its affiliates at the oblast and rayon levels as the responsible agencies for implementing
the FIAS component. The implementation of the FIAS component also involves the active participation
of various autonomous agencies outside the administrative jurisdiction of the MOAF, viz., the AKKOR
and several research and scientific institutes who would be the primary sources of information. In order
to ensure coordination, a FIAS advisory committee would be formed consisting of representatives from
the MOAF's Information and Video Unit, AKKOR, IIC, RAAS and end-users to advise the head of the
FIAS Component Implementation Unit.
Seed Industry Development Component
154.    The project officer dealing with the seed component in the PDU will coordinate the seed
components and will oversee the execution by different agencies (MOAF, NSAC, Gosseminspektsia,
Gossortkomissia, and NSA); provide technical and management advice to mobilize financial resources
for project implementation; and organize training to strengthen human resources and institutions to
promote a modem seed industry in Russia.
155.   Development of Seed Enterprises. The coordination of the on-lending program and investment
monitoring to seed enterprises will be implemented by the PDU. The proejct officer (seed development)
and an independent foreign expert on seed enterprise investment will review bankable feasibility studies
submitted by the seed enterprises. The expert will assure that all studies meet Bank requirements, and
provide recommendations to the Government and the World Bank concerning the acceptability of the sub-
loan. ONce the sub-loan is approved and the subsidiary loan agreement is signed, the release of funds
and their utilization will be monitored, and periodic assessments of the enterprise will be undertaken to
provide technical advice and help ensure profitability. The PDU will coordinate with the "agency" bank
activities related to accounting, reporting and recovery of sub-loans.
156.   Institutional Development. The monitoring of the institutional development aspects will be
undertaken by the project officer (seed development) located in the PDU. The project officer will work
with designated officers located in the agencies receiving project support: (i) MOAF/NSAC; (ii)
Gossortkomissia; (iii) Gosseminspektsia; and (iv) the NSA. The project officer in the PDU shall ensure
that the agreed programs are carried out in a timely and acceptable manner by these agencies, r-Jluding
all procurement and training activities.



45
C. IMPLEMENTATION SCHEDULE
Market infrastructure Development Component
157.    Market Infonnation System. The implementation of the nationwide MIS project will be
carried out in three phases (see Annex 14). The USDA-supported "pilot" project in Kaluga oblast has
already been established and key staff has been trained. This process will be further strengthened by the
two new pilot projects in Belgorod Oblast and the Republic of Mari. The MIS will be a nationwide
system and will be established in 86 oblasts, krays and autonomous republics and 500 rayons over a
period of four years after the project becomes effective.
158.   MOAF will conclude contracts with at least 50% of the oblasts covered by MIS during first year
of the project. It will also select rayons where MIS should be implemented; prepare a detailed list of
commodities covered in each of the oblasts during the first phase of the project; establish samples of
farms, processing enterprises and traders to be interviewed; and develop software packages for processing
of information. The software should be ready before equipment arrives on site. MOAF will also ensure
that oblast and rayon level departments of agriculture upgrade and renovate MIS offices before the
scheduled installation of computer equipment and communications. MOAF will also contact mass media
to arrange for dissemination of market information via radio, television and newspapers in oblasts and
rayons to be included in the project. MOAF has begun the preparation of instruction manuals on data
collection and processing and material for foreign and local training.
159.   MOAF Computer Center will appoint appropriate personnel to carry out the computer systems
development, with clearly assigned functions and responsibilities. It should prepare suitable training
programs for the assigned personnel to start the project as soon as the new hardware and software arrive.
Simultaneously, MOAF will clarify contractual arrangements with various users of information in the
following aspects: (i) ownership of data; (ii) accessibility of data; (iii) maintenance of computer systems;
and (iv) phasing of computer equipment required for MIS and FIAS. MOAF and MOAFCC will prepare
a detailed computer system design document, including a detailed design of infrastructure matching the
expansion of MIS/FIAS with X.25 net expansion. MOAF, in consultation with the Ministry of
Telecommunications and other providers of telecommunication services (especially X.25 packet
networks), will make sure that appropriate facilities are available in all the participating oblasts and
rayons for MIS and FIAS services required in 1994.
160.   Pilot Investments in Market Development. As a condition of loan effectiveness, governments
in two participating regioins will set up local CIUs. The pilot investment sub-component will be
implemented (Annex 14) on a case-by-case basis, consistent with the following approach: First, the
MOAF has been asked to require participating private enterprises to complete sub-project feasibility
studies. MOAF will evaluate the feasibility studies as soon as these are received. Meanwhile, the Bank
and MOAF will work out appropriate arrangements to assist MOAF in the evaluation of these feasibility
studies. Second, all fruit and vegetable farms participating in the project must complete production
restructuring plans to make use of new technologies and management techniques to increase their
production and productivity which is fundamental to make investments in storage facilities viable. The
production restructuring plans will be reviewed by technical teams to be established by MOAF and World
Bank.



46
Farmer Information and Advisory Services Component
161.    The implementation of the FIAS component will be carried out in phases (see Annex 14).
During the first year, ARSRIIMA will establish the federal FIAS center, three oblast centers and five
rayon centers. New methods and the application of technologies for identifying, sourcing and packaging
information products will be developed in these areas. These will be tested, evaluated and refined before
being generalized to other areas. The FIAS will be extended to the major agricultural regions, covering
about 30 oblasts and 125 rayons, over a period of five years. Information through videos, print media
and on-line computers is expected to be accessible to users in selected areas within a year of project
implementation.
Seed Industry Development Component
162.   Implementation of the institutional and enterprise investment sub-components will take place
over the entire project period (see Annex 14). Disbursements for the institutional development sub-
component will be made for equipment and machinery procurement during the first two years of the
project. Technical assistance and training for this sub-component will be provided during the first three
years. The enterprise development program will finance the construction of three seed processing facilities
starting in project year 1, followed by four new facilities in project year 2, and three more facilities will
start construction in project year 3. All new processing facilities would be commissioned by the end of
project year 4. Technical assistance and training for the enterprise investment sub-component will be
provided in project years 1-4, with the majority of the expenditures during project years 2 and 3.
D. SUPERVisION PLAN
163.   The supervision plan and schedule for supervision missions is developed in Annex 15. During
the first year of the project, four Bank supervision missions would be required, followed by three
supervision missions during each of the ensuing years. Each mission would be staffed by appropriate
experts and will be for two to three weeks duration. The mission would review the physical targets,
monitor the impact and assess the institutional development and implementation procedures agreed for
the project; identify critical implementation problems and agree on remedial actions. Supervision costs
of the project are expected to be high because of MOAF's lack of experience in implementing World
Bank projects and other operational difficulties in Russia. The supervision missions will be assisted by
staff from the Resident Mission who will also be available to advise the government between the
missions. This would be further supplemented by hiring qualified local consultants on a continuing basis.
164.    The Bank supervision mission in the last quarter of each implementation year will assess
progress during the previous year and plan the work program and budgetary allocations for the following
year. The MOAF, with assistance from the PDU, will prepare brief reports for discussion during the
mission and will provide evidence of necessary budgetary allocations to carry out the planned work in
the following year. A detailed mid-term review (MTR) will be carried out 30 months after the project
becomes effective and will involve the Bank and relevant Borrower agencies.
165. Benchmarks for measurement of progress in project implementation will include the following:
(a) the operational status of all the component implementation units; (b) the initiation and implementation
of in-country and foreign training; (c) the start-up and implementation of the technical assistance
packages; (d) the implementation and operation of "pilot" marketing and seed enterprises in the private
sector; (e) the establishment of oblast and rayon market information systemn; (f) the establishment of
farmer information and advisory services (FIAS) centers; (g) dissemination of farmer information through
multi-media system; and (h) the operations and scheduled reporting of the PDU.



47
V1II
PROJECT BENEFITS AND RISKS
A. PROJEcr BENEFIs
Overall ARIS Project
166.    The project is expected to have substantial economnic and financial benefits. The project's
potential benefits include increased production of corn and sunflower seeds, farm income, rural
employment and foreign exchange savings. More importantly, this project would lay the foundation for
future policy reforms in the agricultural sector. The project will accelerate the implementation of ongoing
reforms through the provision of critical support services. The project will train a large number of
Russian experts to advise farmers and develop analytical capacity to prepare and implement future
agricultural projects to be financed by the international financial institutions. The training and technical
assistance will impart important skills to the staff of agencies involved in project implementation at the
federal and regional levels.
167.    The creation of viable institutional capacity will lay the foundation for a more efficient
agricultural sector based on private ownership and market principles. For example, the development of
a marketing system and alternative agricultural marketing channels will increase competition, reduce
physical losses, provide higher quality food at competitive prices and have favorable impact on prices and
marketing services received by farmers and hence improved farmer income. The development and
dissemination of appropriate information to private farmers, newly restructured farms and agro-business
enterprises will enable them to make informed business decisions, improve agricultural productivity and
participate in the process of agricultural reform. The establishment of a modern seed industry would
increase the availability of quality hybrid corn and sunflower seed which, in turn, would result in higher
crop yields and foreign exchange savings from reduction in seed imports and will lay the foundation for
developing a modern seed industry in the private sector.
168.   The successful implementation of the ARIS project would have positive benefits in at least three
key areas. First, the seed component would increase corn and sunflower seed production, both of which
are important sources of foreign exchange savings since they are both imported. Domestically produced
corn (feed) and silage (fodder) would assist in making the livestock sector more efficient. Second, market
information system and farmer information and advisory services components would assist the emerging
farmers to respond to changing economic conditions and increase their understanding of a competitive
markets. Third, with the emergence of new marketing channels, investments in private marketing
infrastructure would demonstrate how competitive markets function and could be replicated in other parts
of the country.
Market Infrastructure Development Component.
169.  The MIS will result in several positive benefits: it will signal possibilities for profitable sales of
agricultural products and thereby create producer incentives; improve bargaining position of emerging
producers; stimulate competition among traders thereby contributing to efficient allocation of resources;
help producers and traders to adjust to changing demand; reduce interregional price disparities; promote
comparative advantage; and expand international trade. Investments in pilot investment sub-projects will
provide a number of important institutional and economic benefits. It would demonstrate the commercial
viability of these enterprises and the critical role of appropriate policies for the establishment of marketing
systems in which producers, traders, processors and consumers could operate in a competitive
environment. Producer prices would reflect the market conditions, marketing margins will provide



48
reasonable returns on services rendered by market intermediaries and consumer prices should be
competitive. The market prices would result in demand and supply responses that will promote increased
production and productivity and reduced waste. Prices to farmers would probably show an overall
increase while consumers will benefit from reduced prices--contributed by reduced post-harvest losses
of agricultural products and reduced marketing margins--and improved grading and standards, product
quality, and market intelligence. The projert will be an important step in the ongoing transition from the
command to competitive agricultural marketing system.
Farmer Information and Advisory Services Component.
170.  The provision of timely and relevant agricultural information (agronomic practices, technology
choices, business regulations, market conditions and agricultural reforms) is vital to increase awareness
of farmers, processors, and traders of the possible new options on different aspects of the agricultural
sector. Access to free information would motivate agricultural producers to seriously confront the new
challenges of emerging farm problems that are blocking their economic progress and would increase their
exposure to a variety of alternative choices and opportunities. This flow of information will expose
agricultural producers to new opportunities for those willing to take normal business risks. The
successful implementation of the FIAS component would have a positive impact on accelerating the pace
of agricultural reforms and in providing the needed stimulus for grass roots development in the
agricultural sector. The use of the mass media, viz., television, video and radio, in the dissemination
of agricultural information has the potential to reach a very large audience across the Russian Federation.
The establishment of the FIAS centers at the federal level and in 30 oblasts and 125 rayons in important
agricultural areas over the project life would also benefit a large number of emerging farm organizations
and agro-business enterprises. The strengthening of farner training centers would create institutional
capacity to train the trainers and FIAS advisors.
Seed Industry Development Component.
171. The establishment of modern seed enterprises will lay the foundation for developing a dynamic,
market-oriented seed industry in the private sector. The new enterprises financed under the project would
increase the quantity and quality of corn and sunflower seeds sold to Russian farmers, which will result
in increased yields for these principal food, feed and fodder crops. The model demonstrated by these
enterprises is expected to be replicated in other new seed enterprises in the principal seed production
areas.
172.     The project will help define a comprehensive national seed policy that delineates the
complementary roles of government regulatory and support agencies and private sector seed enterprises.
The Government will institutionalize a seed policy dialogue with representatives of the emerging private
seed industry. The project will support the enactment and implementation of the necessary legal
framework including a law for registration of crop varieties and protection of plant breeders' rights, and
a basic seed law that regulates the quality of the seeds traded in the marketplace. The new legislation will
promote the development of the private sector, including the involvement of foreign seed companies as
joint ventures. In addition, seed testing laboratories and field inspectors would be technically upgraded
and assisted to provide quality services to the participating private enterprises.
173.    The seed enterprises established under the project would produce approximately 40,000 tons
of hybrid corn seed, and 4,000 tons of hybrid sunflower seeds annually. The corn seed produced is
expected to be planted on 200,000 ha for corn grain production, and approximately 1.24 million ha of
corn silage production. The sunflower seed is sufficient to plant approximately 650,000 ha. Estimates of
quantifiable benefits from the use of this seed indicate an incremental annual production of 300,000 tons



49
of corn grain, 8.64 million tons of corn silage, and 585,000 tons of sunflower seeds. The current value
of this incremental production is estimated at about US$35 million for the increased corn grain
production, US$120 million for corn silage and US$175 million for the increased sunflower production
annually. The project will also have a fiscal impact through the savings in foreign exchange due to the
reduction in the import of corn and sunflower seeds, and the elimination of import and price subsidies
applied to these seeds.
B. ECONOMIC AND FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
174.  Given the unstable macroeconomic environment and distorted input-output price structure, the
estimated economic rates of return on sub-project investments are preliminary. However, the economic
and financial analysis indicates that investments in private "pilot" seed and marketing enterprises are
viable (see Annex 16). A detailed enterprise analysis for representative participating private enterprises
(one each for hybrid corn seed, vegetable storage and fruit and vegetable base) as case studies is provided
in Annex 17.
Pilot Seed Enterprises
175. Competitiveness of Seed Production. Russia has a strong comparative advantage in the
production of hybrid corn and sunflower seeds. The results summarized in Annex 16 show that the cost
of production of processed hybrid corn seed is 50 to 60 percent that of production costs of comparable
seed in North America and Western Europe. The important factors contributing to this diffeirnce are
the lower production costs of parent line seed, low wages and low processing costs in Russia.
176.  Economic Rate of Retun. The underlying basis of economic analysis for the seed enterprises
is that Russia is a net importer of hybrid corn and sunflower seeds. As a result, import parity prices are
used to calculate economic rates of return. The estimated economic rates of return for investmnents in the
hybrid corn and sunflower seed enterprises are 25 percent and 37 percent, respectively.
177. Financial Analysis. The financial analysis for the newly privatized hybrid corn and sunflower
seed enterprises was based on detailed feasibility studies. Of the 10 seed enterprises, three hybrid corn
seed enterprises and one sunflower seed enterprise were appraised. The financial rates of return of 18
percent and 34 percent were obtained for com and sunflower seed enterprises, respectively, which
indicate that these enterprises are commercially viable. Incremental revenues from the proposed
investments in seed enterprises would be adequate to enable sub-borrowers to repay the loan within a
period of 7-10 years with a grace period of 2 years. The risk of a sharp decline in marketing margins
is less likely due to strong effective demand for quality hybrid com and sunflower seeds.
178.  Sensitivity Analysis. Sensitivity analysis for economic rates of return was undertaken to evaluate
the impact of changes in investment costs, operating costs, total costs and seed prices. Results indicate
that in both the hybrid com and sunflower seed enterprises, the project is resilient to an increase in
project costs. For example, an increase in investment, operating and total costs by 20 percent yields
economic rates of return on hybrid corn enterprises of 21 percent, 17 percent and 14 percent,
respectively. In the case of sunflower enterprises, the corresponding rates of return are 31 percent, 30
percent and 26 percent, respectively. The corn seed enterprises are more sensitive to decline in output
prices. A 20 percent decline in corn seed prices yields a rate of return of 11 percent, and a similar
decline in sunflower seed prices yields a rate of return of 24 percent. Furthermore, sensitivity analysis,
expressed in terms of switching values using an opportunity cost of capital of 15 percent in constant 1993
US$ terms, shows that the com seed enterprises could withstand a 15 percent decline of revenues or 16



50
percent increase in total costs. In the case of sunflower seed enterprise, the corresponding results are 31
percent decline in revenues or 45 percent increase in total costs.
Pilot Marketng Enterprises
179. Economic Rate of Return. The economic analysis for investments in fruit and vegetable bases
and storage facilities is based on the assumption that the two major urban markets in the country, Moscow
and St. Petersburg, would remain net importers of quality fruits and vegetables with supplies coming
from FSU and East European countries. The project output must, therefore, sell in competition with
imported fruits and vegetables. As the major emphasis of the proposed investments in market
infrastructure is to demonstrate the role of systemic reforms, their contribution to the economy in terms
of increased supply of better quality fruits and vegetables, relative to the total turnover in the market,
would be marginal. Thus, for the purposes of economic analysis, the value added project outputs (mainly
fruits and vegetables) are considered as traded commodities and valued at projected CIF prices, where
available, and in terms of consumers' willingness to pay, as appropriate. The prices of non-traded inputs,
as far as feasible, have been valued at their approximate opportunity cost. The economic rate of return
for investments in typical fruit and vegetable bases and cold storage facilities have been estimated to be
in the range of 26 to 29 percent.
180.  Finanal Analysis. Financial analysis was carried out for: (i) fruit and vegetable bases in
Moscow City and Belgorod Oblast; and (ii) cold storage facilities for vegetables in Moscow Oblast. About
25 sub-projects were reviewed during the preparation and appraisal missions and financial analysis was
carried out on those where marginal investments could yield quick returns and on those enterprises that
were willing to participate in the project and pursue systemic mnarketing reforms. Financial analysis for
fruit and vegetable bases indicates financial rates of return of 19 percent for Sverdlovskaya base in
Moscow City and 23 percent for Plodoovoshprom base in Belgorod Oblast. The financial rates of return
for selected cold storage facilities at restructured farms located in Bunyatino, Ozeri, Yahromskiya and
Kras in Moscow Oblast were estimated to be 19 percent, 22 percent, 23 percent and 23 percent,
respectively. Incremental revenues from the proposed investments in selected marketing enterprises
would also be adequate to enable the sub-borrowers to repay the loan within 7 to 10 years, with two years
of grace period.
181. Sensitivity Analysis. Sensitivity analysis for economic rates of return indicates that the economic
rates of return are moderately affected by increases in total costs or decline in revenues if these are in
the range of 10-20 percent. For example, in the case of cold storage facility, rates of return of 15 percent
and 10 percent are obtained if the total costs increase by 60 percent or revenues decline by 50 percent.
However, border prices (expressed in US$) for inputs and outputs are unlikely to witness such
fluctuations during the life of proposed investments. The financial rates of return are particularly
sensitive to product prices and marketing margins and, therefore, project will require, as a condition of
subloan approval, that enterprises agree to introduce modern production and storage technologies; pay
attention to bring about efficiencies in vegetable and fruit production; and ensure optimal storage capacity
utilization using energy efficient practices. The project will provide necessary technical assistance to
enterprises.
C. PROJECT BENEFICIARIES
182.    The primary beneficiaries of the project would be private farmers, restructured farms, agro-
business enterprises and participating private enterprises for seed and agricultural marketing. The
comers (both in rural and urban areas) will benefit through increased availability of fruits and
vegetables of higher quality at lower prices. The government decision makers, policy makers and private



51
traders would benefit from increased availability of timely information related to agricultural problems,
market and prices. In the long run, the nation would benefit through increased agricultural production,
foreign exchange savings and environmental protection. Overall, this project would lay the foundation
for a more efficient agricultural sector thereby increasing the prospects for inproved farm income and
employment opportunities in rural areas and improved quality and potentially lower prices for consumers
in both rural and urban areas.
D. ENVIRONMENTAL AspEcTs
183.   The project is expected to have a positive impact on the environment. The move toward private
farming promoted by this project is expected to foster more prudent use of land and other natural
resources. In addition, public awareness on agricultural environmental issues will be created through
appropriately designed environmental information collection and dissemination, technical assistance and
training programs. The project has a "B" environmental assessment category.
184.    Any environmental concerns related to new investments in "pilot" sub-projects for market
infrastructure and seed enterprises will be addressed during implementation of sub-projects by applying
Bank's and country's environmental requirements and guidelines. The feasibility studies for marketing
and seed enterprises will include an obligatory environmental review, the adequacy of which will be
verified by the PDU. The Bank's review of typical feasibility studies will ensure the adequacy of
environmental analyses and suggest improvements as necessary. PDU will be given the responsibility
of ensuring that recommendations made in environmental analyses are implemented.
185.    For the participating marketing enterprises, the project would ensure proper disposal of food
wastes which are generally high in organic content. The cold storage facilities for fruits and vegetables
are designed to ensure proper storage conditions such that the produce does not become contaminated
through inadequate handling and storage. All environmental and health regulations would be required
to avoid any contamination of food. No other environmental risk are involved.
186.   For the participating seed enterprises, dust from the seed cleaning operations will be controlled
by installing appropriate dust control system and cyclone dust collectors. Seeds will be coated with
appropriate treatment chemicals. Only those treatment chemicals will be used for coating of seeds which
are on the approved FAO codex list. The equipment and areas where the treatment will be applied will
conform to international norms for ventilation and worker health. All treated seed will be packaged in
suitable containers with information (labels in Russian) about the treatment and safe storage and handling
of seeds. All enviromnental regulations will be followed to avoid any possible contamination. No other
environmental risks are involved.
E. PRojEcr RISKS
187.   While potential benefits of the proposed ARIS project are substantial, the potential operational
risks are also large. The main risk is that the project may face delays in implementation. Factors that
could cause problems in project implementation include: (i) the current unstable macroeconomic
environment and its possible further deterioration; (ii) lack of political consensus on agricultural reforms;
(iii) lack of counterpart funds; (iv) delaying in government decision making; and (v) lack of trained
personnel with experience in implementing World Bank projects. The review and appraisal of at least
the first three sub-projects for each of the seed and marketing enterprises by the Bank are expected to
reduce potential implementation risks. The project has introduced necessary measures in the form of legal
covenants, training and technical assistance and has allocated necessary resources to minimize these risks.
The establishment of a strong PDU, with appropriate support for procurement, disbursement, accounting,



52
project management and technical aspects, is also expected to mitigate some of the potential project risks.
The project would be intensively supervised (including a project launch workshop, annual reviews and
a comprehensive mid-term review) so that appropriate and timely actions are taken to avoid and overcome
implementation problems.
188.    The major risks facing the Market Infrastructure Component are: (i) the inability of oblast
governments to implement the MIS program as planned; (ii) lack of financial viability of participating
private enterprises due to poor management and/or reintroduction of price controls by the government
for fruits and vegetables; and (iii) MOAF's inability to complete the planned training and technical
assistance programs. The project and covenants have been designed to address these potential risks.
These measures would be supplemented by intensive Bank supervision to facilitate effective project
implementation.
189.    The main risks facing the FIAS component are: (i) problems of institutional co-ordination in
achieving administrative efficiency; (ii) availability of relevant information to prepare packages on time;
(iii) degree to which these information packages are adopted by users, given the lack of support services,
critically needed farm inputs and the financial resources to obtain them; and (iv) degree of commitment
of the participating oblasts and rayons. The degree of acceptance of the information generated and
disseminated by the FIAS component would depend upon the overall production incentives as well as the
general macroeconomic situation. The emphasis of the component to develop easy to understand multi-
disciplinary information packages and the use of multi-media approach for dissemination minimizes the
risk of timely availability of relevant information.
190.   The seed industry development component faces risks in three areas: (i) possibility of reversal
in government policy to seed price liberalization and elimination of seed subsidy; (ii) continued lack of
appropriate incentives for staff engaged in the implementation of seed policy, viz., Gosseminspektsia,
Gossortkomissia and MOAF Seed Division; and (iii) possibility of the Russian Academy of Agricultural
Sciences (RAAS) not providing the required hybrid seeds to the private sector enterprises being financed
under the project. The maintenance of free markets for the sale of seeds is critical for the commercial
viability of participating seed enterprises. As long as these prices are not controlled, domestic seed prices
should adjust with changes in international prices and the exchange rate, which should enable private
investors to obtain an attractive return on their investments. The formation of the National Seed Advisory
Committee being proposed under this project has been designed to ensure that timely supply of
appropriate inputs from RAAS are provided to the participating seed enterprises and that contractual
arrangements between RAAS and the private sector seed enterprises are honored.
VIE[
AGREEMENTs AND RECOMMENDATIONS
A. PRoJECr AGREEMENTS
191.   The Government has informed the Bank of: (a) Government's policy on liberalization of prices
and elimination of subsidies and administrative controls on marketing of seeds (hybrid corn and
sunflower), fruits and vegetables; and (b) government commitment to maintain these policies. These
policies are critical to make project investments in the seed and marketing enterprises commercially viable
and to encourage private investment on a continuing basis.



53
192.    The following agreements were reached during negotiations:
(i)    Agreement on cost recovery principles (para. 117);
(ii)   Agreement on terms and conditions for sub-loans to participating private enterprises (paras.
108, 110, 112, 113, 114);
(iii)  Agreement on the process for preparation, appraisal, approval and financing of sub-projects
(paras. 108, 113);
(iv)   Agreement on the use of any surplus reflows on a revolving basis for financing similar sub-
projects (para. 113);
(v)   Agreement on the composition of the Project Coordination Committee 2nd its role in
approving project sub-loans (para 141);
(vi)   Agreement on the organization, staffing, functions and financing of PDU and the CIUs
(paras. 142-149);
(vii)  Agreement on the provision of periodic reports on project progress, accounts for the
project, audit requirements and financial statements (paras. 130-138);
(viii) Agreement on selection procedures for procurement agents (paras. 125, 126);
(ix)   Agreement on selection procedures for commercial banks to manage the Special Account
and/or to serve as agency banks (paras. 105, 108, 110);
(x)   Agreement on adequate legal and administrative framework for making sub-loans to private
enterprises (para. 113);
(xi)   Agreement on guidelines for oblast participation in the project (para. 71); and
(xii)  Agreement on provision for retroactive financing up to US$1.0 million (paras. 102, 129);
193.  The following are the conditions for loan effectiveness:
(i)    Appointment of key staff of the PDU  (project management specialist, accountant,
procurement specialist and technical specialists) (para. 146);
(ii)   Establishment of CIUs within the departments and agencies of MOAF and in at least two
participating regions for sub-components dealing with private seed and marketing enterprise
(para. 149); and
(iii)  Establishment of the Project Account and an initial deposit of at least US$1.0 million
equivalent in a commercial bank acceptable to the Bank (para 103).
B. RECOMMENDATION
194.    The proposed ARIS Project constitutes a suitable basis for an IBRD loan of US$240 million
equivalent to the Russian Federation for a period of 17 years, including a five-year grace period, at the
Bank's standard variable interest rate.

CONSnTTUTION
The Constitution, Dec. 12, 1993. Approved by referendum, it recognizes private, state,
municipal and other forms of ownership over land, all with equal status under the law.
Previous moratoria on land sales are abolished.
LAWS
1.    On Land Reform, October 23, 1990, with changes December 27, 1990, reintroduced
*the concept of land ownership in Russia.
2.    On Peasant Farms, Nov. 22, 1990 with changes Dec. 27, 1990, established a legal
framework for private family farms.
3.    On Ownership in the RSFSR, Dec.24, 1990. The basic document dealing with
property rights, including rights in land. Elaborated and refined in subsequent
legislation.
4.    The RSFSR Land Code, April 25, 1991. Defines the basis of land ownership, use and
disposition. A revised version was prepared by the Supreme Soviet in 1993, but never
approved by the President. Most of the Land Code was pronounced invalid by a
-Presidential Decree on Dec. 24, 1993. The Federal Assembly is responsible for
preparing a new land code.
5.    On Payment for Land, Oct. 11, 1991. The law provides methodology for determining
sales price, land tax and lease payments (for state land leased to private persons), using
regional coefficients and technical measurements.
6.    On the Ri2ehts of Citizens to Acauire as Private Propertv and to Sell Land Parcels to
Conduct Subsidiarv Farming and Daclha Operations. Horticulture and Private Housing
Construction, Dec. 23, 1992. Eliminated sales moratoria for small land parcels
(generaly less than 0.5 hectares) in the above categories.
7.    The Law on Protection to Plant Breeders' Rights. August 6, 1993. The law protects the
breeders' right on the variety of crop, planting material and animal breeds which are
different in specific single or combination of genotypic characters of the same taxonomy.



56                                   ANNEx 1
Page 2 of 5
These breeds or plant varieties whole or in part may be used for production purposes.
Protection is provided to the following: clones, lines, hybrids of first generations, seeds
or part of plant used for multiplication, breeds or group of animal type, line crosses,
newly evolved male, female or young breed of animal.

1.    On Uraent Measures for Implementation of Land Reform, No. 323, Dec. 27, 1991.
Provides for the destructuring of state and collective farms and the creation of a state
reserve fund of land for reallocation of agricultural land to private users.

State.

farnr  and private farmer cooperation.
7.    On Regulation of Price Settlement between Producers of Agricultural Produce & Food
Stuffs and the Purchasers. No. 1401, September 22, 1993. Sets out the conditions to
protect the interests agriculture produce and food stuff producers from the delayed
payment by the purchasers. The decree imposes fine of 1 % of the contracted price of
supply, per day of default in payment to the producer.
8.    On Regulation of Land Relations and Development of Agrarian Reform in Russia, No.
1767, Oct 27, 1993. Eliminates restrictions on the sale of land and reiterates the right
to mortgage land. Greatly simplifies the procedure for registration of land by eliminating
the need for surveys as part of land tides. Establishes a simplified "Certificate of Title"
as the basic land registration document. Reiterates the mandate of the State Land
Committee to administer the land registration system. Identifies structures as well as land
in the concept of real estate.

18.   On the Experience of Agrarian Transformation in Nizhny Novgorod Oblast, No. 324,
April 15, 1994. Approved the results of the Nizhny Novgorod farm restructuring pilot
project; called for establishment of a regional training center in Nizhny Novgorod to train
specialists to carry out farm restructuring work; and ordered elaboration of regulations
and procedures governing the process of fann restructuring by June 15, 1994.

Page 1 of 5
RUssIAN FEDERATION
AGRICULTURAL REFORM IMPLEMENTATION SUPPORT (AIS) PROJECT
ORGANIZATION OF THE MIsTRY OF AGRICULTURE AND FooD
ORGANIZATION
1.    The Ministry of Agriculture and Food (MOAF), can be characterized as comprehensive, complex
and all pervasive. It was responsible for production and processing of agricultural raw materials and
implementation of policies within the overall guiding principle of the Supreme Soviet. It has the history
of seventy years of command approach for directing the agricultural production units to meet the
economic and political goals of the central government. The MOAF and other bodies connected with
agriculture, under the FSU, were organized and staffed on the concept of centrally controlled order and
compliance system. Agricultural administration, till recently, was through a three-tiered pyramidal
system. The federal government at the apex, oblasts/krays/independent Republics at the next level, and
the rayons at the base. Under the FSU, there were large number of employees to oversee the
implementation of the programs on farms and agro-industrial complexes under the central command.
2.    Following the break up of the FSU, and the consequent disintegration of the central command
system led to the crisis in the supply and demand of agricultural commodikies. Government reorganized
the MOAF such that it can play a greater role in agricultural reforms. At present, the MOAF has nearly
20 departments (see Annex Figure 2.1). The Minister of Agriculture andl Food is the head and is
responsible to the Council of Ministers. The Minister of Agriculture is suppcrted by two First Deputy
Ministers and six Deputy Ministers looking after the following major areas in the Ministry: (i) the
territorial department dealing with Republics, krays, and oblasts, scientific and technical councils; (ii)
mechanization, electrification, technical services and labor protection (safety); (iii) economics and
finance; (iv) agrarian policies; (v) research, technology, and agricultural education; (vi) soil amelioration
and irrigation water-management; (vii) agricultural reform, state sector programs, rural development and
resettlement; and (viii) crop production and animal husbandry. The organization of MOAF departments
dealing with the ARIS Project is shown in Annex Figure 2.2. However, the present reorganized structure
continues to reflect the practice of the command economy but to a lesser degree.
3.    Attached to the MOAF, there are other privatized and semi-public organizations that are
concerned with agriculture. Until recently these were part of the Ministry and were responsible for
administering processing, procurement, distribution and services under the centralized command. After
mid-1991, they were separated and are formed into independent agencies. They are officially independent
of the Ministry of Agriculture at the Federal level. These agencies deal with the processing, production
of agro-iindustrial products, inputs and outputs marketing, distribution and other services. They are
supported by payments received from beneficiaries of their services. They are, however, not yet
financially independent in the true sense.



61                                  ANNEX 2
Page 2 of 5
FUNCInONS
4.    Under the FSU, the MOAF had a mandate to design and implement socio-economic programs
for maximizing agricultural production to meet the demands for agricultural goods for the entire country;
implement programs to improve the efficiencies of the agro-industrial complexes; introduce scientific
systems on the farms for intensive agriculture; improve the land use and fertility status to achieve high
levels of production for agricultural crops and livestock; strengthen fodder base and accomplish stability
in agriculture production. MOAF established targets for the production by the production units
(Kolkhozes, Sovkhozes and Meshkhozes and Agroproms) and implemented this policy by regulating
inputs, machinery and budget. The MOAF also generated basic data to fix prices of the agricultural
commodities and quantities for imports and exports. Pricing of agricultural commodities was determined
by the Ministries of Economy and Finance and was based on the data provided (and in consultation with)
the MOAF and Ministries of Trade and External Affairs.
5.     After the break up of the FSU, the major task of the MOAF is to implement the structural
reforms to facilitate transition to a market economy. The government has to dismantle management of
the agro-industrial complexes, promote private sector enterprises to replace the public sector such as
inputs supply and output marketing. The MOAF has plans to shed its bureaucratic approach to facilitate
independent functioning of the enterprises; get away from the farm input and output marketing function;
and to eliminate procurement quota obligations. It shall assume the standard setting; quality regulating;
policy defining; and implementing organizational development programs. The linkage between research,
teaching and state production system at the functional level is minimum. There is a felt need for
improving the relationship between the agricultural research institutions on the one hand, production and
processing systems on the other. Similarly, the Departments of Agriculture at the rayon and oblast level
have to reorganize to meet the needs of the public institutions, private farmers, restructured farms and
agri-business enterprises in the context of changing agriculture in response to reform.
STAFFING
6.     In the past, there were large number of specialists (economists, agronomists, mechanical
engineers, civil engineers, zoo-technicians, veterinarians, soil conservationists, accountants, lawyers and
administrative staff) working at the Ministry. They formulated development programs as per the
Government directives; established targets for production for each oblast and rayon; allocated
proportionate budget and the farm inputs to match the accepted targets for production; negotiated with
the oblast government to achieve the targets and allocation of inputs and subsidies; and followed up with
periodic review of the progress. The programs were implemented through the three tier system: (i) state
level, (ii) oblast (region) level, and (iii) rayon (district) level. Department of Agriculture and Food of
the oblast (Agricultural Committee) under the local Regional Government (Oblispolkom) is independent
of the MOAF. The staff at the oblast are employees of the oblast government. The oblast staff
implement both central and provincial programs as per the agreed annual plans. Similar mini pyramid
structural arrangements are at the rayon (district) level with local government administration (Raispolkom)
which implements, collects and consolidates information from the production units, Sovkhozes and
Kolkhozes and send it to the rayon administration and the department of agriculture and food at the
oblast.
7.    The Department of Agriculture and Food of the rayon level, under the local rayon administration
(Raiselkhosprod) in-racts with Sovkhozes/Kolkhozes/Meshkhozes for rendering technical service from



62                                   ANNEX 2
Page 3 of 5
the local government. The agricultural staff in each of the rayon work under the policy framework of
the oblast and rayon level government, on the technical matters. They also interact with supply and
service organizations such as Raiagropromtekhniki (for machinery), Raiselkozekhim (agrochemicals),
Raiagrosnab (input supply), Raiagropromzerno (grain processing and procurement), Selkhozstraenia
(constructions), Agrofirmi (processing) which are the rayon level branches of over 20 federal and oblast
level organizations.
8.     During the past two years, the Government has been making a serious effort to rationalize the
manpower needs in the public administration of agriculture. This includes responsibilities to facilitate
and promote production systems through public services; develop capability to analyze and formulate the
national agricultural policies; monitor the policy ramifications under the changing economic
circumstances; and provide farm information service to the emerging private farms/enterprises with legal,
technical, organizational and managerial assistance to facilitate implementation of the land reform and
enterprise restructuring process. This process entailed restructuring the organization of the Ministry of
Agriculture and Food (formerly the Ministry of Agriculture and Production) and reducing the staff by
more than 50%. Private sector is new to Russia. Establishment of - market economy requires the
development of a new legal framework to facilitate for the tran    . There is general lack of
experienced technical personnel who could make decisions based oi, miiarket principles and prepare
technically and commercially viable programs.
