RUSSIAN FEDERATION
AGRICULTURAL REFORM IMPLEMENTATION SUPPORT (ARIS) PROJECT
PROCUREMENT PROCEDURES AND ARRANGEMENTS
INTRODUCJrON
1.    The individual Component Implementation Units (CIU) of each project component would
undertake the procurement through procurement agents (agents), under the general supervision of the
Project Development Unit 'PDU). The private seed and marketing enterprises would also procure
through procurement agents.
2.    As noted in the Appraisal Report and elsewhere in this annex, most of the loan-financed
procurement would follow international competitive bidding as required by the 'Guidelines: Procurement
Under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits (May 1992)" and the Loan Agreement.
3.    The PDU would monitor the selection of procurement procedures proposed by the procurement
agents to ensure that they were in accordance with the Loan Agreement, review the draft bidding
documents and the evaluation reports with award recommendations prepared by the agents, and transmit
the bidding documents and evaluation reports for Bank review as required. For this purpose, the PDU
would have experienced procurement staff which would provide technical ass.stance to the CIU's and the
agents.
4.    Equipment and civil works procurement would be carried out by international competitive
bidding, except as noted below, in accordance with the Bank's Procurement Guidelines The Bank's
standard bidding documents for the Procurement of Goods and the Construction of (Smaller) Works will
be used. Consultants will be employed following the Bank's Guidelines "Uses of Consultants by World
Bank Borrowers and by The World Bank as Executing Agency (August 1981)."
5.    Equipment will be packaged for procurement through periodic bidding exercises for each project
component or sub-component, except for computers and communication equipment for MOAFCC, farmer
information and advisory service and the market information system components, which will be combined
for procurement purposes.
6.    Each procuring unit is expected to obtain the services of a procurement agent. These procurement
agents will be selected on an technical and fee basis from firms in accordance with the Bank Guidelines.
The consideration for the selection and responsibilities of these procurement ageiLs is discussed further
below.
MARKET INFRASRUCrURE DEVELOPMENT
7.    The market infrastructure would include installations in Moscow City, Moscow Oblast and
Belgorod Oblast. The civil works for these facilities would be bid through ICB, estimated at US$6.6



128                                ANNEX 10
Page 2 of 25
million, where the value would be greater than US$1.0 million, Local Competitive Bidding (LCB),
estimated at US$18.0 million, where the contract value would be between US$100,000 and US$1.0
million, and Local Shopping (LS), with at least three competitive quotations, for contracts less that
US$100,000, amounting to US$0.3 million. Pre-qualifications would be required for LCB procurement.
A summary of procurement arrangement for market infrastructure development is provided in Annex
Table 10.1. The local procedures (LCB and LS) would be acceptable to the Bank. The first two
contracts and those above US$500,000 for LCB would be subject to prior review.
8.    The renovation works, US$1.7 million, would be widely scattered in various government
installations. The local civil works component would be carried out by local public works departments
(US$1.2 million) from their own resources by force account. The imported inputs (US$0.5 million) will
be procured through international shopping (IS) in consultation with PDU.
9.    Equipment for the infrastructure facilities would be procured through ICB, US$13.6 million,
Limited International Bidding (LIB) US$4.0 million, and IS US$0.7 million. The equipment procurement
would be packaged in a manner satisfactory to the Bank and acquired through ICB for packages above
US$0.5 million, LIB for packages between US$300,000 and US$0.5 million, and by IS for packages
smaller than US$300,000.
10.   Materials (US$7.3 million) would cover inputs required for the operations of the facilities and
would be procured by the enterprises on the basis of International Shopping.
11.   The computer and communications equipment for the market information system (US$35.6
million) and the farmer information and advisory service (included below in the Summary Table for
Farner Information and Advisory Services), as well as for the Ministry of Agriculture and Food's
Computer Center will be procured through ICB.
12.   Computer and communications equipment supply would include a long-term servicing and
warranty program by the suppliers. Post-sales service is essential to the effective use of the equipment
by providing maintenance and repairs as required to keep the equipment operational. A servicing
arrangement is believed to be necessary in the current local circumstances. The requisite service would
be provided at the oblast level, i.e.. the supplier would be required to ensure that service would be
provided at the oblast city in which the FIS/MIS facilities were located. Rayon equipment would have
to be delivered to this city for service. A performance guarantee from the supplier would be required
to ensure that the service was provided.
13.   The first two construction contracts and those above US$0.5 million per contract procured under
LCB for the market infrastructure facilities would be subject to prior review. Assuming the computer
and communications equipment for the market information system, including the equipment for the
Computer Center and the farmer information and advisory services, is procured as a combined exercise,
there could be as many as 200 separate awards. Prior review of awards for equipment exceeding
US$300,000 would cover 80 per cent of the total value of the procurement.

14.   Technical assistance (US$3.0 million) would be provided by consultants whose selections, terms
of reference and contractual arrangements would be according to the Bank Guidelines. Normally
consulting assignments would be filled through competitive proposals from shortlisted consulting firms;
individual consultants would be used where (a) teams of personnel are not required, (b) no additional
outside professional support is required, and (c) the experience and qualifications of the individual are
the paramount consideration. The terms of reference and approximate times of assignment are contained
in Annex 8. Training (US$0.7 million) is for courses and seminars, and more details are provided in
Annex 7.
FARMER INFORMATION AND ADVISORY SERvICES
15.    To the extent possible the individual items will be bulked and coordinated by the PDU. A
summary of procurement arrangements for the FIAS component are summarized in Annex Table 10.2.
The foreign component of the civil works (US$2.7 million) consists of security systems, furnishings and
fixtures for federal, oblast and rayon level FIAS centers. The exact items still have to be specified and
there would be as many as 30 oblasts, with a larger number of rayons. so the amount for each FIAS
center will be small. Therefore, as the items cannot be bulked because of dispersed location and year
of renovation, the items would be purchased though International Shopping (US$2.7 million).
16.   Renovation work (US$3.8 million equivalent) would be done by force account by local public
works units with local funds.
17.   Equipment purchases above US$0.5 million would be purchased through ICB for US$12.4
million. Some equipment is varied, spread over time and different users and cannot be conveniently
bulked. Equipment of US$1.7 million would be acquired through LIB and US$3.3 million by IS. Some
of the audio-visual equipment may be purchased through direct purchase for reasons of compatibility;
direct purchase would be no greater than US$100,000.
18.   The computer and communications equipment will be purchased jointly with similar items for the
market information system component through ICB (US$11.7 million).
19.   Prior review of awards in excess of US$300,(00 for goods would include 80 percent of the total
value.
20.   Training (US$9.6 million) is for institutional development and includes foreign training courses
for staff at the federal, oblast, and rayon levels, training for multi media specialists, and for individual
short-term advisors.
21.   Technical assistance (US$1.9 million) would be provided by consultants whose selections, terms
of reference and contractual arrangements would be according to the Bank Guidelines. Normally
consulting assignments would be filled through competitive proposals from shortlisted consulting firms;
individual consultants would be used where (a) teams of personnel are not required, (b) no additional
outside professional support is required, and (c) the experience and qualifications of the individual are
the paramount consideration. The terms of reference and approximate times of assigm ent are contained
in Annex 8.

SEED INDuSmY DEVELOPMENT
22.   The projected contracts for the construction and placing in operation of corn and sunflower seed
processing plants total up to US$7.2 million of civil works and US$83.8 million of equipment. These
contracts are estimated to be between US$7.0 million and US$11.0 million equivalent and would be
awarded following international competitive bidding. Bidders would be pre-qualified. The first five
plants are proposed to be commissioned through turnkey contracts. Later units may be divided into
separate civil works and equipment supply and installation contracts, as project implementation takes place
and seed enterprises gain experience with the design and construction of new seed processing plants. The
corntracts for civil works and for equipment would continue to be placed through ICB. A summary of
procurement arrangements for the Seed Industry Development component is provided in Annex Table
10.3. Some preliminary and simple works, estimated at US$6.3 million, such as roads, garages, lab and
office buildings, will be constructed by the enterprises using their own funds.
23.   Equipment procured for institutional development, i.e. for the Russian Seed Registration Agency
and the Russian Seed Inspection Agency would be through ICB for US$18.2 million, LIB for US$4.5
million and IS is for US$0.1 million.
24.   Some local equipment for processing and laboratory items, estimated at US$6.9 muilion, would
be purchased by the enterprises with their own funds, primarily on a negotiated basis. This equipment
is produced locally and is nature, timing, and availability are such that it does not lend itself to formdal
competitive bidding.
25.   The contracts for seed processing plants, whether turnkey or separate construction and equipment,
would be subject to prior review by the Bank. Equipment contracts greater then US$300,000 would be
subject to prior review. These limits would cover essentially all loan-financed procurement.
26.   Training will be arranged with foreign training agencies and consist of study tours and training
to support the investment activities of the seed enterprises. The trainees will be individuals selected by
the public agencies and the enterprises with the concurrence of the PDU and the Bank. The terms of
reference and the conditions of training contract would be acceptable to the Bank.
27.   Technical assistance would assist the seed registration, inspection agencies and enterprises.
Individual advisers would be selectively recruited by the PDU and would be acceptable to the Bank. The
terms of reference and the conditions of contract for technical assistance would be acceptable to the Bank.
Details are provided in Annex 8.

PROJECr DEVELOPMENT UNIT
28.   The equipment for the PDU (US$0.3 million) would be procured by IS as the items are varied
and needed at the beginning. A summary of procurement arrangements for the PDU is provided in
Annex Table 10.4.
29.   Training (US$1.9 million) would consist of study tours and workshops. Details for various
training programs are provided in Annex 7.
30.   Technical assistance (US$5.3 million) also includes US$1.5 million professional and support staff,
as well as a retainer for legal assistance to the PDU and would be selected in accordance with procedures
satisfactory to the Bank. The technical assistance would cover short-term consultants over the project
implementation period. These consultants would be recruited in accordance with the Bank's Consultant
Guidelines and the terns of reference and the conditions of employment would be satisfactory to the
Bank. Details are provided in Annex 8.
PROCUREMENT ARRANGEMENTS
31.   The Component Implementation Units (CIUs) of each project component, and the private seed
and marketing enterprises, would undertake the procurement of goods and works and turnkey contracts
using the services of the procurement agents. Individual consultants, consulting firms and procurement
agents would be procurd following procedures specified in the World Bank's Guidelines Use of
Consultants by World Bank Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing Agency (August 1981).
32.   As noted in the Staff Appraisal Report and elsewhere in this annex, most of the procurement
would follow international competitive bidding as required by the World Bank's Guidelines Procurement
Under IBRD Loans and IDA Credits and the Loan Agreement. The PDU would ensure that procurement
procedures are in accordance with the Bank Guidelines, unless otherwise agreed with the Bank. PDU
would also review the draft bidding documents and the evaluation reports with award recommendations
prepared by the agents, and transmit the bidding documents and evaluation reports for the Bank prior
review as required.
Slection of Procurement Agents
33.   The procurement agent would be selected by assessing capability, experience and fee levels. The
PDU would propose a shortlist of qualified firms capable of satisfactorily handling procurement under
the ARIS project.

Page IO of 215
34.   Procurement Arrangements The PDU would be responsible for the overall supervision of
procurement under the project. The PDU would have procurement personnel and technical experts to
advise and assist in project procurement and project implementation. Government departments and public
agencies would use a selected procurement agent for procurement. Each of the private enterprises would
also be required to use a qualified procurement agent. During the mid-term review of the project, the
continued use of the procurement agents would be assessed in the light of experience gained and the
prc^urement capabilities developed by the component implementation units and the PDU.
35.   The procurement agent for the public agencies would be selected from a short list prepared by
the PDU in accordance with the Bank's Guidelines. The PDU would prepare a longer list of eight to
twelve qualified procurement firms for selecting procurement agents by the private enterprises. From
this list, selection will be made in accordance with criteria of qualifications and price in the invitation for
proposal. The selection would be based upon experience and administrative capacity, in combination with
fee levels. The fees of the procurement agents would be eligible for disbursement from the loan
proceeds.
36.   The organizations' fees and reimbursable costs would be disbursed in the form of direct payments
from the Bank or from the Special Account.
International Competitive Bidding
37.   The following steps would be taken to implement the international bidding requirements of the
Loan. They are intended to be an explanatory guide to the Project Development Unit and the Component
Implementation Units.
38.   General Procurement Notice The Project r jvelopment Unit's procurement specialist would
prepare a General Procurement Notice for publication in "Business Development" of the United Nations.
This will describe in general terns the goods and works to be financed under the project, provide an
address for interested parties to indicate their wish to be advised when bid documents will be available
and will alert interested parties to subsequent announcements. The General Procurement Notice will be
published by the PDU and forwarded to the Bank for review and submission to Business Development.
39.   Follow-up General Procurement Notices would be placed by the PDU in Business Development
when the bidding documents are scheduled. The Notices would also be circulated to diplomatic
commercial representatives in Moscow, and published in one or more national newspapers. These would
provide more detailed information, including the date the bid documents would be available, and an
address to which interested suppliers could refer.
40.   Procurement Scheduling The grouping of the items to be procured into bid packages will be
prepared and the procurement scheduled by the PDU with the assistance of a consultant. The schedule
may be subsequently modified in consultation with the procurement agents when the agent is selected ani
when the PDU is fully staffed.
41.   Bid Document Preparation The World Bank's standard Bidding Documents for Procurement
of Goods will be the principal document used, although the Bidding Documents for Procurement Works



137                                ANNEX 10
Page 11 of 25
(Smaller Contracts) and the Bidding Documents for Procurement of Works, Major Equipment and
Installations, would be used in seed processing and market facilities.
42.   The technical specifications and any technical requirements to be considered in bid evaluation
would be the responsibility of the end-users, in consultation with the PDU and CIUs, with the assistance
from consultants. The procurement agent would be expected to review and comment on the technical
specifications in order to reflect any commercial considerations. On the other hand, the agent would
prepare the commercial portion of the documentation, with comments from the end-users, CIUs and the
procurement specialist in the PDU. The final documents would be reviewed by the PDU and the Bank,
as required.
43.   Effective warranty service, regular maintenance with spare parts, and consumables peculiar to
the equipment are important. Therefore, the specificaticns are expected to require the suppliers to
provide such service for the duration of the project, where such requirements are appropriate to the goods
being purchased. This would apply especially to the computer related equipment, which would also
provide for technical upgrade to equipment and software following .ritial purchase.
44.   Advertising As the procurement packaging and scheduling proceeds, updated notices of the
bidding opportunities would be advised through advertisement in one or more national newspapers of
wide circulation, circulars to local commercial diplomatic representatives, and notices to those who had
responded to General Procurement Notices.
45.   Bid Receipt and Bid Openn  The bidding documents would specify the place and final time
for bid submission. The trading organizations would provide secure receptacles into which bidders could
msert their offers and be assured they would remain secure until bid opening. The organizations would
open the bids at a place pre-notified, ensure a bid guarantee had been included, and read aloud the total
price of the offer. Any protests by the representatives present would be noted and forwarded to the Bank.
The minutes of bid opening would be prepared immediately by the organization. The bidders or their
representatives could attend the bid opening.
46.   Evaluation of Bids The bids would be evaluated by the procurement agent in consultation with
the staff of the PDU and the end-user. Where elements other than price were to be a factor in the award
selection, these would be taken into account as prescribed in the bidding documents. An evaluation report
would be prepared with specific recomnmendations for contract awards by the agent. The report would
be submitted to the PDU for review and forwarding to the World Bank for its review as set out in the
Loan Agreement, or as subsequently agreed with the Bank.
47.   Contract Finalization After the Bank has expressed no objection to the contract award where
this was required, the contract would be finalized. Contract effectveness would require a letter of credit,
as the most probable method of payment, to be opened in favor of the supplier, and the deliyery of the
performance securities from the supplier/contractor as required by the bidding documents.
48.   Monitoring Conract Execution The agent would monitor the delivery of the goods, arrange
for receipt and inspection at the place of delivery stipulated in the purchase contract, arrange customs
clearance, and manage any further local transportation or distribution that might be necessary for the
goods to reach the end-user. If third party inspection were necessary, this would be arranged by the



138                                ANNEx 10
Page 12 of 25
Agent by using the services of an independent agency established for this purpose or, where more
suitable, by an inspection by a representative of the end-user.
Procurement Other Than ICB
49.   Limited International Bidding Where all eligible specialized suppliers are confidently known,
or in certain minor procurement where time or the amount of the purchase did not permit/warrant
international advertising, a limited number of eligible suppliers can be invited to submit bids. The same
bid documentation would be required, and identical bid opening, evaluation and contract finalizing
procedures would be followed as for ICB. The use of such a procurement procedure must be agreed with
the Bank.
50.   International Shopping  For small amounts of purchases or where special circumstances,
generally of an urgent nature arose, bids could be requested informally but in writing from at least three
sources from three different Bank member countries. An evaluation report should be prepared, listing
the offers received and explaining the reason for the choice, with an explanation of the reason for using
international shopping. The use of this procurement procedure must be agreed with the Bank. An
example of the document that might be used is to be found at the end of this Annex.
51.   Sole Source Prom ment In clearly justified instances, and with the concurrence of the PDU
and the World Bank, purchases could be made from a single source, the original equipment supplier.
Tlhe applicability of this procedure in any particular case must be agreed with the Bank..
52.   Consultant Selection The selection of consultant firms for assignments would follow the
competitive proposal selection process outlined in the Guidelines Use of Cnnsultants by World Bank
Borrowers and by the World Bank as Executing Agency (August 1981). 'The TORs as well as the
credentials of the proposed individual consultants or firms should be satisfactory to the Bank.
RESONSIBILTEs OF PROcuREmENT AGEN1s
53.   The procurement agents will be responsible for carrying out procurement for and on the behalf
of end-users in accordance with the procedures outlined in the World Bank Guidelines Procurement under
IBRD Loans and IDA Credits and as specified in the Loan Agreement with the Borrower. The term
procurement agents is usxd to mean an organization with expert and experienced staff which has wide
knowledge of and experience with international commercial practices of goods generally similar to the
items to be procured. The Agents must be capable of providing the various support services required to
effectively conduct procurement activities. For procurement carried out according to International
Competitive Bidding (ICB) procedures the procurement agent, in consultation with the appropriate
technical staff of the MOAF or private enterprises and in consultation with the PDU, will:
(a) Arrange advertising and provide information to interested suppliers;
(b) Prepare the bidding documents in detail, using standard bidding documents issued by the
World Bank, with appropriate adjustments to accommodate the individual procurement
packages. The technical specifications will be prepared by MOAF staff or experts from
private enterprises, with the assistance of consultants, as may be necessary to reflect project



139                                 ANN    10
Page 13 of 25
needs effectiveiy and economically. Submit the bidding documentation to the PDU for
review by the World Bank;
(c) Distribute/sell bidding documents to prospective bidders at a nominal price, any excess
beyond reproduction costs are to be credited to the PDU's account;
(d) Provide clarifications in response to the queries of potential bidders;
(e) Conduct the bid opening process and prepare Minutes of the Bid Opening;
(f) Organize a bid evaluation committee which would include, among others, representatives of
the staff/consultants who prepared the technical specifications. The award recommnendations
would be based solely on the criteria contained in the bid documents. Prepare an evaluation
report with recommendations for awards. This report shouid contain any comments that
members of the evaluation committee wish to make. Submit the evaluation report for review
by the PDU and/or Bank, as may be required.
(g) Finalize the contract, folow-up and monitor deliveries, and, ensure payment in accordance
with the contractal terms;
(h) When required, arrange pre-shipment inspections, either by third party inspectors or
tepresentatives of the end-user; and
(i) Receive goods at the point of delivery, clear customs and arrange delivery, or confirm that
it is arranged by the end-user, to the proper destination.
54.   For non-ICB procurement, the procurement agent will, in consultation with the end-user and the
PDU, foUlow the appropriate procedures as specified in the Loan agreement and in accordance with the
Procurement guidelines.
55.   The procurement agents will be responsible for contract administration and will:
(a) Collect and copy to the PDU docmenton such as inspection certificates, certificates or
origin/manufacturer, manufactuers/suppliers guarantees, bills of lading, beneficiary
insurance certificates, and invoices;
(b) Maintain in coordination with the PDU, accounting and financial management and handle any
warranty and insurance claims;
(c) Assist the PDU in the preparation of withdrawal application from the loan funds with the
required supporting documentadon and assist in ensuring payment to the suppliers; and
(d) Submit periodic progress reports to the PDU and the end-user.



140                                ANNEX 10
Page 14 of 25
EVALUATION PROCEDURE FOR PROCUREMENT AGENTS
56.   The assignment comprises the following main tasks:(i) full range of procurement services for
International competitive Bidding (ICB) and other forms of international procurement; (ii) contract
administration, and (iii) reporting and distribution and monitoring activities.
57.   The proposal from the candidate procurement agents would comprise the following:
(a) description of the organization including:
(i)  its charter and most recent balance sheet,
(ii)  the volume of the firm's international procurement for the past three years, and
(iii) the finn's experience in the purchase of items similar to the ones proposed;
(b) the experience of the staff the firm proposes to use for the assignment, including their
proficiency in English;
(c) a description of how the firm proposes to conduct the assignment, i.e., the organization and
procedures to be followed; and
(d) the proposed fees as discussed below.
58.   The remuneration will be on a commission basis. The commission will be calculated as a
percentage of each contract placed on a slab basis, at the rate of exchange on the date of contract. The
fees should be quoted for the following range of contracts: up to US$100,000; from US$100,001 to
US$500,000; from US$500,001 to US$1,000,000; from US$1,000,001 to US$5,000,000; from
US$5,000,001 to US$10,000,000; and over US$10,000,000. Please note that the financial proposal
should be submitted in a separate sealed envelope. The mix of contracts for the comparison of the fee
schedules should reflect the anticipated mix in the procurement to be undertaken.
59.   Proposals will be evaluated in a two-stage procedure which include (a) an evaluation of the firm's
experience and procurement plan, and, subsequently, (b) an evaluation of the firm's fees. For the
selection of procurement agents only, the experience and procurement plan could be awarded a weight
of 60 percent and the fees 40 percent.

RussIAN FEDERATION
AGRICULTURAL REFORM LMPLEMENTATioN SUPPORT (ARS) PROJECT
ACCOUNTING AND FINANCIAL 1REPORTING PROCEDURES
ACCOUNTING
1.    The accounts for the project include expenditures financed directly by the World Bank and
expenditures financed by the government agencies (project specific) and the participating private
enterprises (PPEs). As the financial reports would be classified into two categories: project specific and
participating agencies, the Project Development Unit (PDU) would consolidate and maintain financial
records from these two categories.
Project Specific
2.    The Component Implementation Unit (CIU) of the individual project component would have an
accounting and control system capable of reliably recording and reporting all financial transactions. This
system must consist of two ledgers; one for US dollar receipts and expenditures and another for local
currency Government appropriations and expenditures and must be operational when project expenditures
begin, capturing all transactions related to the project on a monthly basis.
3.    All disbursements from the IBRD loan must be recorded as receipts in the US dollar ledger, on
the day that the disbursement is made. Any Government Budget Appropriations must be recorded as
receipts for the month that they relate to, in the local currency ledger, and may not exceed the amount
recorded under expenditures included in the Project Component Cost Schedule for Government financed
expenditures.
4.    Project expenditures should be recorded as incurred following the same method used in capturing
receipts.
5.    The two monthly ledgers would be sent to the PDU, no later than ten business days after the end
of the month, to be reviewed and consolidated with the other component ledgers. The PDU will be
responsible for translating the local currency ledger to US dollar equivalents using an exchange rate based
on a formula to be agreed upon between the World Bank and the borrower prior to conmencement of
disbursement.
6.    Where circumstances indicate that documents are excessive, they should be filed by the CIU in
such a way and with PDU approval, that the PDU can access them easily.
Paricpating Private Enterprises
7.    The PPEs would provide to the PDU on a quarterly basis, unaudited accounts for the project
summarizing the cash receipts and expenditures, by component financed (i.e. civil works, equipment,



153                                ANNEX 11
Page 2 of 7
etc.). This account would not only include the expenditures directly financed by the World Bank, but
also the expenditures financed by the PPEs themselves.
8.    Each PPE would have an accounting and control system capable of reliably recording and
reporting all financial transactions. This system must consist of two ledgers; one for US dollar receipts
and expenditures and another for local currency expenditures financed by the PPE and must be
operational when project expenditures begin, capturing all transactions related to the project on a monthly
basis.
9.    All disbursements from the IBRD loan must be recorded as receipts in the US dollar ledger, on
the day that the disbursement is made. Any PPE contributions must be recorded as receipts in the US
dollar ledger, on the day that the disbursement is made. Any PPE contributions must be recorded as
receipts for the-month that they relate to, in the local currency ledger.
10.   Project expenditures should be recorded as incurred following the same method used in capturing
receipts.
11.   The two monthly ledgers would be sent to the PDU, no later than ten business days after the end
of the month, to be reviewed and consolidated with the other PPE ledgers and subsequently with the other
project components. The PDU will be responsible for translating the local currency ledger to US dollar
equivalents using an exchange rate based on a formula to be agreed upon between the World Bank and
thie borrower prior to commencement of disbursement.
12.   Where circumstances indicate that documents are excessive, they should be filed by the PPE in
such a way and with PDU approval, that the PDU can access them easily.
13.   The PDU will receive the monthly ledgers, in US dollar and local currency, from the individual
CIUs and the PPEs no later than the tenth business day of the following month. The local currency
ledgers will be translated to US dollar equivalents using an exchange rate based on a formula to be agreed
upon between the World Bank and the borrower prior to conmmencement of disbursement.
14.   The individual PPE accounts would be consolidated and reported as one, together with the other
sub-components, which in turn would be consolidated by component.
15.   The components would be consolidated in US dollar equivalents and filed to be used for reporting
purposes at the end of the quarter.
16.   As statements of expenditure (SOEs) and a Special Account (SA) will be used in this project, the
PDU would be requested to prepare and maintain, on a monthly basis:
(a) a schedule of SOE expenditures and amounts; and
(b) a summary of the SA account activity and reconciliation with the SA balance in the
commercial bank account and the Project Account.



154                                ANNEx 11
Page 3 of 7
FINANCIAL REPORTING
17.   The World Bank requires the financial performance and status of a project and borrower to be
monitored regularly during implementation, either during the period of loan disbursement or throughout
the life of the loan. As a result, the PDU w%ould provide quarterly financial reports to the World Bank
throughout the monitoring period. These financial reports would include the aforementioned two
categories: project specific and participating private enterprises.
18.   The PDU would provide quarterly and annual accounts for the project, and reporting on the cash
receipts and expenditures related to each sub-component, component and the overall financial performance
of the project. The accounts for the project would not only include expenditures directly financed by the
World Bank, but also the expenditures financed by the PPEs and the government agencies. These reports
would reflect actual sub-component activity versus planned expenditure for the most recent fiscal quarter,
fiscal year-to-date and project inception-to-date. The format and individual classification of receipts and
expenditures was designed and agreed between the Russian government and the World Bank during
negotiations (see Attachments 1 to 4) and will be distributed to the accounting officers in the PDU and
the ClUs and to the responsible accountants in the individual PPEs.
19.   The quarterly reports will be sumnarzed in a Consolidated Statement of Project Receipts and
Expenditures prepared using the US dollar combined amounts for each individual component
consolidation of each sub-component prepared using the US dollar combined amounts (the summation of
the US dollar receipts and expenditures, and the local currency receipts and expenditures translated to
US dollar equivalents).
20.   All quarterly reports are to be submitted to reach the World Bank no later than 30 days after the
end of the quarter. In addition to the unaudited quarterly accounts for the project discussed above, a
summary of the physical performance of the project showing actual and planned performance for the
quarter and accumulated to date. This would be presented in such a manner as to facilitate comparison
with the project expenditures. These reports would also include comments highlighting project progress
and significant bottlenecks, showing an action plan for remedial actions where necessary.

RussIAN FEDERATiON
AGRICULTURAL REFORM 1IMPLEMENTATION SUPPORT (ARIS) PROJECT
PROJECT DEVELOPMENT UNIf AND PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
A. General Implementation Arrangements
Organizations Responsible for Implementing the Project
1.    The executing agency responsible for implementation of the Project is the Ministry of Agriculture
and Food (MOAF). The Project Development Unit (PDU), established in the MOAF, would coordinate
all the work related to budgeting, procurement, disbursement, audit, reporting and monitoring of the
Project, including appraisal of all proposed sub-projects involving on-lending of funds to private
enterprises. Actual implementation of project components and sub-components will be the responsibility
of individual Component Implementation Units (CIUs) located within the departnents and agencies of
the MOAF, with the exception of private seed and market enterprises. The PDU director would report
to the Minister of Agriculture. The Minister would be advised by a Project Coordination Committee
(PCC).
Project Coordination Committee (PCC).
2.    The PCC would be chaired by the Minister of Agriculture. Other members of the PCC will
include a representative each from the Ministry of Finance, the Ministry of Economy, and other relevant
agencies. This Committee will assist and advise the Minister to effectively implement the ARIS project,
to establish priorities for agricultural projects; coordinate preparation and implementation of agricultural
projects; and approve, with clearance from the World Bank, sub-projects involving on-lending of Bank
funds to private enterprises.
B. PDU Organizaton and Management
PDU and Management
3.    The MOAF, with advice from the Project Coordination Committee, is expected to carry out the
project implementation functions thiough a Project Development Unit (Annex Figure 12.1) which has
been established in the MOAF. The PDU would be headed by a director appointed by the Minister of
MOAF who would be empowered to appoint staff and consultants, with concurrence from the Ministry
of Finance and the World Bank. The PDU operations would be divided into two departments: Project
Implementation Department (PID) and Project Preparation Department (PPD). Each of these departments
would be headed by a deputy director whose selection and appointment would be the responsibility of the
Minister, with concurrence from the Ministry of Finance and the World Bank.



160                                 ANNEX 12
Page 2 of 9
4.    The PDU director would be responsible for overall day-to-day decisions regarding PDU functions.
The two department deputy directors would be responsible for day-to-day decisions on matters related
to the functions of their departments, subject to concurrence of the PDU Director on critical issues.
Project Implementation Department (PID)
5.    The PID's initial responsibility would be to implement the ARIS Project. Staff with sufficient
technical experience and qualification would be selected to facilitate, guide, and monitor the
implementation of various components and sub-components of the project (Market, Information, and
Seed). Foreign consultants would be engaged for each component to provide necessary support. In
addition, support staft would be engaged as considered necessary.
Project Preparation Department (PPD)
6.    The PPD would be responsible for developing analytical capacity for identifying and preparing
new agricultural projects for financing by the international fine.  o institutions. This department would
have a regular and fixed-term staff of economists, financial ana  -, technical specialists, and experts in
agricultural project management and policy analysis. The PPD would also engage staff and consultants
with specific technical expertise to meet specific needs.
PDU Staffing
7.    The PDU staffing must include project management specialist, accountant and procurement
officer. The PDU would need a Senior Accountant with appropriate qualification and experience in
international accounting practices to be responsible for the introduction and management of all financial
and accounting matters required for project implementation and preparation. This appointment should
be made as soon as possible to allow participation in the design and development of the internal control,
accounting and reporting arrangements required to implement the Project. The Senior Accountant would
also be responsible for the administrative management of the Special Account and the disbursement
process for all agencies and institutions participating in the Project. The appointment of the Senior
Accountant should have the concurrence of the World Bank. The Senior Accountant should be supported
by at least one assistant accountant, who should also be experienced in international accounting practices.
8.    The PDU would also need a Procurement Officer trained in the procurement procedures of the
World Bank with the ability to train, direct and guide procurement activities of all the participating
agencies and institutions associated with the implementation of the Project. This appointment would have
the concurrence of the World Bank. The activities of the Procurement Officer will be supported by a
procurement assistant who should be fully versed in the World Bank procurement procedures. The PDU
would retain a lawyer (or law firm) with appropriate experience in Russian administrative law and
international commercial transactions to assist as required with legal issues. The selection of the lawyer
(or law firm) should have the concurrence of the World Bank.
9.    All the PDU staff (higher-level and support level) will be recruited locally. Most of the staff
appointments would be fixed-term and will be designed to complete a specific assignment. This is
essential to give the PDU the flexibility to change its staff skill mix to reflect the agricultural project
portfolio under preparation and implementation. All the critical PDU staff will be expected to have a
working knowledge of spoken and written English. The local PDU staff would be supported by foreign



161                                 ANNEX 12
Page 3 of 9
experts who would also train Russian experts in agricultural project managemnent, implementation and
preparation, including procurement and accounting.
Size of PDU Staff
11.   Following is an estimate of the number of staff required. The first column indicates the estimated
total number of staff required when the ARIS Project is under full implementation, including an active
Project Preparation Department. The second column indicates those positions thais need to be filled most
urgently to assure effective implementation of the Project.

Terms of Reference for Selected Rusian Staff of the PDU
12.   All the critical Russian PDU consultant and support staff must have good communication skills,
both oral and written, in the English language. Following is a summary of the most important additional
qualifications and skills required:
PDU Director. The PDU Director will have responsibility to direct, oversee, and coordinate the
management and implementation of the Project. Therefore, the Director must be a specialist in



162                                ANNEx 12
Page 4 of 9
project preparation and project implementation, should have one or more advanced degrees in an
agriculture or management-related field, and must have had at least 10 years relevant experience
in managing complex programs or projects.
Accountant. The Accountant will review for consistency with Project and World Bank
procedures the withdrawal, or disbursement, applications prepared by component project
implementation units before sending them to the Senior Accoumtant for signature. The
Accountant should be experienced in international accounting practices.
Procurement Officer. The Procurement Officer should have been trained in the procurement
procedures of the World Bank and should have the ability to train, direct and guide procurement
activities of all the participating agencies and institutions associated with the implementation of
the Project. The Procurement Officer will be assisted for the first six months by foreign advisers
who are familiar with World Bank procedures.
Procrment Assistant. The Procurement Assistant should be fuHly versed in the World Bank
procurement procedures. A major duty will be to provide practical assistance to procurement
agents.
PED Deputy Director. The Deputy Director for the Project Implementation Department will
oversee and facilitate implementation of the four components of the Project. The Deputy
Director, who will be the point of contact in PDU for the Heads of Implementation Units for the
four components, should meet as often as necessary with the Heads to learn what problems or
issues they face and to work out a plan of action to facilitate implementation. The Deputy
Director will also be responsible for assuring that the Implementation Units submit the periodic
financial and progress reports required by the World Bank, and will recommend disbursements
under the Bank loan. The Deputy Director should have one or more advanced degrees in an
agricultural discipline, and must have had at least five years' relevant experience in agricultural
project implementation.
Project Offlcers. The PID Deputy Director will be assisted by two project officers whose duties
will be to assist the four Component Implementation Units, as required, in the processing of
documentation related to budgeting, procurement, disbursement, and reporting.
PPD Deputy Director. The Deputy Director for the Project Preparation Department will be
responsible for overseeing and directing the identification and preparation of sub-projects and new
agricultural projects for financing by the World Bank and other financial institutions. The ideal
candidate would have had at least five years relevant experience in project identification,
preparation, and management.
Project Spedalsts. Short-term technical specialists will be drawn upon as required in the
preparation of projects. Depending on the nature of the project being developed, the project
preparation team might consist of economists, financial analysts, technical specialists, policy
analysts and experts in agricultural project management.

RUSSlAN FEDERATION
AGRICULTURAL REFORM IMPLEMENTATION SUPPORT (ARPS) PROJECT
AGENCIES INVOLVED IN PROJECT IMPLEMENTATION
MIISTRY OF AGRICULTURE AND FOOD COMPuTER CENTER
1.    The Ministry of Agriculture and Food Computer Center (MOAFCC) was established as an
independent state joint stock enterprise owned by MOAF according to the resolution No. 142 of
November 1990. It is the principal computing center of the MOAF, with its new title of GVC
ROSSELKHOZ reflecting this responsibility. It has the legal privilege to purchase computers and related
supplies.
2.    The governance of GVC is based on the principles of self-management of a work collective on
the basis of the right of an owner to use its assets to satisfy the stated objectives. Its activities are guided
by the Law of the Russian Federation "On Enterprises and Entrepreneurial Activity". According to the
statutes, the main objectives of the MOAFCC are:
(i) To provide information services; establish and maintain centralized data base of the MOAF;
and purchase and operate computer hardware and software;
(ii) To develop, improve and implement appropriate software to process agricultural information;
(iii) To provide computer, communication and other information facilities and their maintenance;
and
(iv) To organize, coordinate and provide methodological advice to computer centers and other
organizations of the agricultural and industrial complex in Russia on the issues of
development, implementation and operation of the unified system of information and
computer support of MOAF.
3.    It has its own facilities in Moscow, with adequate air conditioning and power circuits to support
the planned ARIS servers and related peripherals. The MOAFCC has 8 progranuning specialists and
similar number of operational and support personnel. They have received clearance to expand the staff
to 25 persons. The MOAFCC staff, even though small in number, has the potential to become effective
through appropriate training.



169                                 ANNEX 13
Page 2 of 5
ALL RUSSIAN SCIENTIFIC AND RESEARCH INSrTUTuE OF INNOVATION ANI) MARTING
IN THE AGRO-NDUSRSAL COMPLEX (ARSSIIMA)
4.    The All Russian Scientific and Research Institute of Innovation and Marketing in the Agro-
Industrial Complex (ARSRIIMA, was established in accordance with the resolution of the Council of
Ministers of the Russian Federation (Order No 469 of the Ministry of Agriculture and Food of the
Russian Federation, of June 4, 1991). The Institute reports to the General Directorate of Science and
Technology at the MOAF and is located in Moscow. The Institute is a legal entity which enjoys rights
and performs responsibilities associated with its activities; has its own assets; independent balance sheet
and account. It has been designated to be a head organization and a methodological research center
dealing with issues of innovation and marketing in the system of agro-industrial complex in Russia. It
has affiliates located in various areas of Russia consisting of 38 branches in autonomous republics, 2
krays and 28 oblasts. All branches have legal entity status and have their own balance sheets. It has
about 200 staff members in a variety of disciplines. All the research works, services of the Institute are
made under direct agreements with the Chief of the Department for Science in the MOAF. The institute
is involved in a number of activities, the most important ones are:
(i) Identifying the priority areas and developing an appropriate system of managing the progress
in science and technology; preparation of economic and legal proposals to ensure efficient
state policies in science and technology and enhanced response of the agrarian economy to
innovations;
(ii) Providing agricultural consulting services in selected pilot areas;
(iii) Developing appropriate cost-effective arrangements for research and development works in
fundamental and applied sciences for the agro-industrial complex and development of
methods for evaluating economic efficiency of projects.
(iv) Carrying out theoretical, applied and experimental studies aimed at improving the efficiency
of the agricultural production related to savings in labor, fuel, energy, and physical
resources; efficiency of the production capacity through science and technology and, using
this as a basis for organizational, legal, economic, managerial decisions in collective and
state farms, private farms and other enterprises;
(v) Developing concepts and programs for adjusting the agrarian science to conditions of market
relations inside the country and its adjustment to the agrarian science of other sectors of the
national economy. Development of programs for formation and development of the market
infrastructure for the scientific and agricultural products.
S.    ARSRIIMA will play the most important role in the implementation of the FIAS component of
the ARIS project. Its staff has been actively associated with the preparation of the FIAS component and
were responsible for overall coordination. Given the institute's involvement in the information needs
assessment, the Institute is capable of providing necessary project support, training and technical
assistance for the achievement of project objectives.



170                                 ANNEX 13
Page 3 of 5
AKKOR - RuSSIAN ASSOCIATION OF FARMER ENTERPRISEs
AND AGRICULTURAL COOPERATIVE SOCIETrES
6.    AKKOR is a voluntary cooperative association of private farms, rural cooperatives,
partnerships and other newly emerging farm structures. It was established at the foundation congress
held in Moscow on January 23-25, 1990. The basic objectives of the association are:
(i) protection of public, socio-economic and personal rights and iinterests oi members of the
association in relation with state and other organizations;
(ii) provide its members with credit and material resources, including those allocated by the
G.vernment for the development of farming;
(iii) coordinate the actions in the interests of the farmers and participate in the
implementation of the land reform;
(iv) collective regulation of inter-farm relations;
(v) provide assistance to develop independrnt activities in production, procurement,
processing, marketing of agricultural products and primary materials; and
(vi) provide service and social welfare infrastructure to private farms.
7.    AKKOR has over 80 regional associations and farmer's unions, over a thousand rayon level
associations and more than 5000 small rural cooperatives. It represents the interest of about 300,000
private farmers. The president and the management board of the association are elected by the
congress consisting of representatives of AKKOR associations.
8.    AKKOR has oblast level administration offices and an office in each rayon. Rayon level
offices operate independently with locally elected councils. The chairmen, in addition to one
representative from the Administration and one from the Land Committee of rayon AKKORs, form
the council of for the oblast. The Oblast council sends three delegates to the All-Russia council.
There are about 10 to 15 full time staff who work at each oblast level AKKOR offices. The funding
comes mostly from trading operations through the supply of farm inputs and sale of farm produce.
9.    AKKOR obtains financing from the Federal Govermment, which is then allocated to the rayons
by the oblast councils on the basis of the number of private farners and their size. The rayon level
AKKOR offices in turn distribute the funds to the most profitable farmers. AKKOR has its own
newspaper which is produced in Moscow and sent to its members. In addition, there are regional
magazines and newspapers.
10    AKKOR is actively engaged in providing various types of information to the emerging private
farmer. Through AKKORINTER, it assists the private farmers in obtaining foreign technologies,
credits and investments, delivery of equipment, primary goods, materials and other commodities to
private farmers. It organizes exhibitions and fairs in various agricultural regions.



171                                  ANNEX 13
Page 4 of 5
THE INSTITUTE OF CYBERNETlCS (IC)
II.   The Institute of Cybernetics is one of the specialized institutes of the Russian Academy of
Agricultural Sciences (RAAS). Its main responsibility is to develop and provide the information
services of the RAAS. It was one of the key institutes responsible for the design of the
"Informatization programn for the Agro-Industrial Complex in the Russian Federation - 2000".
12.   The Institute is based in Moscow and consists of 8 research departments and 2 auxiliary
divisions. The institute works closely with a variety of RAAS institutes and its affiliatuons in the areas
of scientific and technical areas. The main functions of the Institute are: (i) carrying out systems
analysis of the agro-industrial complex, analyze transition issues on the basis of economic and
mathematical modelling in a variety of disciplines; (ii) development of systems to computerize
administration and economic management using computer technologies; and (iii) provide technical
support and establish systems to provide information services for the RAAS.
13.   The Institute of Cybernetics, in collaboration with the Institute of Informatic Problems, has
developed a variety of computer based resource materials, which are available in diskettes and on-
line. They include specially designed databases, spreadsheet templates, document clearinghouse and
skill development training materials. Given the initiatives already undertaken by IC, the ARIS project
will support the pilot project in Pskov (and possibly in Krasnodar) is expected to speed up the
implementation of the applications of these computer based information packages and provide an
opportunity to evaluate its cost-effectiveness and likely replicability in other regions.
STATE VARITAL TESTNG COMMISION (GOSSORTKOMSSUA)
14.   State Commission for Variety Testing and Registration (Gossortkomissia) was set up by
Decree No: 322, dated November 2, 1992 by the Council of Ministers of the Russian Federation.
Gossortkomissia (GSK) is a legal entity located in Moscow. It is authorized to use the official seal
bearing the national emblem of the Russian Federation. The property managed by GSK is property of
the federal government. Therefore, all property transactions need prior approval of the MOAF.
GSK is responsible for the registration, based on field verification trials for varietal merits of
germplasm, of all cultivated varieties, planting materials, including mulberry and silk worm strains.
Activities of the GSK are governed by the Constitution and laws of the Russian Federation,
Resolutions of the Congress of People's Deputies, Presidential Decrees, Directives and Orders, as
well as directives issued by the MOAF.
15.   GSK is managed by its Chairman who is appointed by the MOAF. The Chairman appoints
the Deputy Chairman, the chief accountant and all high level executive staff, including the heads of
the regions and oblasts. It has 70 skiff members in 10 divisions (about 7 members in each division)
at the Moscow office. GSK's functional organ is Gossortset (State Variety Field Testing Network)
which consists of Gossortstansia (owned testing stations), Gossort-Uchastok (rented testing plots), and
laboratories. Gossortset employs over 13,270 people in approximately 568 field locations and 11
laboratories.
16.   The mandate of the GSK is to: (i) otganize expert varietal testing to assess economic viability
of sub-projects; (ii) protect the state from dangers of pests and plant diseases originating outside the
country; (iii) maintain the State Register of Varieties; (iv) regulate procedures and terms for inclusion



172                                  ANNEX 13
page 5 of 5
of specific varieties in the State Register; (v) publish State Registered varietal lists for public
knowledge; (vi) issue the varietal quality certificate; (vii) issue the Certificate of Authorship; (viii)
consider proposals for and decisions on names of varieties; (ix) examine, elaborate, determine and
approve crop variety certification methods; (x) enters into agreements with internaLional seed agencies
fcr testing planting materials in Russia; (xi) compile and maintain a data bank on varieties tested and
their quality s:atus; and (xii) conduct training for its technical field staff.
17.   GSK has so far tested 3130 varieties of seeds, (about 2380 domestic and 750 foreign) in 691
locations (265 kolkhozes, 421 sovkhozes, and 5 testing stations). For corn hybrids, 110 domestic,
112 other CIS and 78 foreign varieties (predominantly from Hungary, Serbia, Austria and Germany)
have been tested. They are tested in 64 oblasts, in 179 locations. For sunflower, 45 Russian, 18
other CIS, and 18 foreign varieties (predominantly from France, Serbia,and Hungary) have been
tested.
18.   It is a self-sustaining organization and is supported through fees collected from seed testing
activities, farm production from testing farms and the sale of seeds (1.8 billion rubles in FY 92). In
addition, GSK receives budgetary support from the federal government (3 billion rubles in rY 93
under article 32 of the constitution, together wit:. e 00 million rubles for capital cost on bitildings). It
also ccllects fees for testing seeds from foreign agencies (about US$110,000 in FY 92).
STATE SEE INSPEctiON AGENCY (GOSSEMINSPEKTSIA)
19.   The Gosseminspektsia (GSI) was set up under Resolution No. 723, dated April 7, 1969 by the
FSU council of Ministers and under Order No: 342, dated April 23 1986, of the MOAF of RSFSR.
Currently, it works under the administrative control of the MOAF and Russian Federation.
20.   GSI is the central seed inspection organ of the state, responsible for seed quality control
services; testing of seeds distributed to the kolkhozes and sovkhozes in Russia; regulating the seed
quality a various stages of seeds production such as sowing, cultivation, harvesting, processing,
grading, treating, storing and distribution; setting up seed quality standards and testing
methodologies; issuing certificates after testing seeds on fields for genetic purity and in laboratories
for physical purity; rendering necessary assistance to seed procuring agencies with appropriate seed
quality testing; and for providLOg training to its office staff and field staff of kolkhozes and sovkhozes
to organize quality seed production. GSI is a member of the Inte. national Seed Testing Association
(ISTA) and strives to maintain international standards in seed testing procedures in Russia.
21.   GSI at Moscow has 5 divisions: (1) Standards and methodology; (2) Quality control; (3)
International testing system; (4) Metrology; and (5) Follow-up of New Techniques. There are 78
oblast level divisions and their corresponding 1713 rayon level sead inspection offices. Rayon
governments assume the responsibility for rayon level seed testing laboratories. Approximately
11,200 employees work in the GS1 network ir- Russia. Annually 15,000 to 16,000 tons of seeds are
approved by GSI and used by 23,000 farms over an area of 46 million ha. About 3 million seed
samples are tested annually. About 180,000 ha (including 15,000 ha of elite seeds) are registered for
seed production and inspection annually.
22.   GSI is a governnent agency run by the MOAF out of the federal budget. The oblast and
rayon level offices are budgeted by their respective local governments.

RussIAN FEDERATION
AGRICULTURAL REFORM IMPLEMENTATION SUPPORT (ARIS) PROJECT
IMPLEMENTATION PLAN AND MONITORING TARGETS
MAIN IMPLEMENTATION FEATURES
1.    Project Development Unit: The establishment of the Project Development Unit and Component
Implementation Units for individual project components is the most critical step to begin the ARIS project
implementation (Annex 12).
2.    Training: The implementation arrangements for various training programs envisaged in the
project and described in Annex 7 should commence soon after Board approval of the project.
3.    Technical Assistance: The implementation arrangements for technical assistance to be provided
for the project and described in Annex 8 should conmmence soon after Board approval of the project.
4.    individual Components: The implementation phases, main features of implementation and the
monitoring targets for each component of the ARIS project are described in more detail in the following
sections.
MARKET INFRASRucTURE DEVELOPMENT
A. Market Information System (MIS)
5.    MIS design. Market infonnationi will flow primarily from rayons to oblasts. At the oblast ievel,
the local Market Information Section in the Department of Agriculture will collect and process local
information. The computer center at the oblast level will manage the database. The federal Market
Information Section in MOAF at Moscow will draw information from data-bases at oblast level and
supplement it by commodity exchanges, Goskomstat and international databases. MIS will be
implemented in phases over four years.
6.    Pilot phase (1993-94) The proposed MIS will be preceded by a pilot project in Kaluga Oblast
that is being assisted by the US Department of Agriculture (USDA). The pilot project would test some
of the MIS concepts and arrange training for key staff who will likely be used as trainers.
7.    Phase I (1995) In the first phase, three economic regions comprising about 26 oblasts will be
included. The number of rayons to be included will be 71. MOAF has agreed to conclude contracts with
at least 50 percent of the oblasts covered by MIS during Year 1 of the project prior to negotiations, which
will coJver the following aspects: (i) cost sharing -- office, furniture, transport, computers, and office
equipment; staffing; (ii) the staffing of MIS activities and responsibility for payment of salaries of staff;
and (iii) dissemination - logistical arrangements for dissemination of information through various media.



174                                 ANNEX 14
Page 2 of 12
The oblast and rayon governments will be responsible for efficient operation of MIS in their jurisdictions,
including data collection, processing, transmission and dissemination.While in the Central and Central
Chernozome (black soil) regions, one rayon per oblast will be initially included, in the North Caucasus
region, as far as possible, all rayons will be covered given their importance as major agricultural
producers in the country. Commodity coverage will be expanded to include inputs, all prices with main
grades and qualities, and price information to include their ex-factory and wholesale prices.
8.    Phase Ir (1996) In Phase II, MIS will cover all 11 economic regions with additional 44 oblasts
raising the total number of oblasts to 70, and rayons to 224. The MIS will begin to collect systematic
information on market arrivals.
9.    Phase III (1997) Phase m includes the establishment of a nationwide MIS. For information
collection, all 86 oblasts will be included, while the number of rayons will be restricted to 500.
Dissemination of the market information will cover the entire nation. The key features of the above
phases have been summarized following Annex Table 14.1.
B. Pilot Investments in Marketing Enterprises
10.   Sub-project Feasibility Studies and Imulemenation. All enterprises would complete feasibility
studies for the proposed investments during 1994 and 1995. These studies would be reviewed by the
PDU supported by local and foreign technical experts financed by the project. Most of the sub-projects,
identified for possible inclusion in the project, have been reviewed by the Bank preparation and appraisal
missions. Subject to the preparation of detailed feasibility studies, technical, financial and economic
analysis has been carried out for some of the typical subprojects. The sub-projects are expected to be
completed as per implementation phases reported in Annex Table 14.2.
FARMER INFORMATION ANm ADVISORY SERVICES
11.   The FIAS component has been designed to provide information to emerging farm organizations.
The FIAS centers, established at the federal, oblast and rayon levels, will have the main responsibility.
The establishment of these centers will be carried out according to the phases shown in the following
table. By the end of the project period, a total of 155 FIAS centers would have been established. These
centers would serve two purposes: (i) trained FIAS advisors would provide advisory services; and (ii)
as these centers would be well equipped with a variety of agricultural information, it will serve as the
primary source of agricultural information. In addition to the FIAS centers, appropriate agricultural
information would also be disseminated through multi-media channels. In this regard, the number of FIAS
centers, agricultural video productions, agricultural fairs and exhibitions and farmers training centers to
be established and implemented in different years have been summarized in Annex Table 14.3.

the two sub-components i.e. (i) seed enterprise development through processing of sub-loans; and (ii)

GUIDELNES FOR REGIONAL PARTICIPATION
14.   The participating regions, namely, oblasts and republic governments (on their own behalf and on
behalf of rayons under their jurisdiction), should agree to implement Market Information System (MIS);
Pilot Investments in Market Development; Farmer Information and Advisory Services (FIAS); and Seed
Industry Development components in accordance with the objectives, concepts, procedures, and terms
and conditions agreed between the Bank and the Government (per Loan Agreement).
15.   For MIS and FIAS components specifically, the participating regions (including Moscow City)
should enter into an agreement with MOAF which will specify their mutual responsibilities with regard
to the implementation of the concerned component. These agreements will include oblast and city
governments' responsibility to pay for local contributions for the establishment of project systems and
facilities and their maintenance on a continuing basis.
16.   For Pilot Investment in Market Development and Seed Industry Development, the participating
regions (including Moscow City) should agree to expeditiously provide to private enterprises, necessary
administrative clearances (such as for fire safety, environment and labor safety), technical support and
facilities to prepare, appraise and implement the sub-projects in a timely and efficient manner and, if
required by PDU, any financial obligations and assurances as may be necessary for the completion of sub-
projects; their viable operations and sub-loan repayments.
17.   The participating regions (including Moscow City) will provide administrative support and
facilities to carry out project-assisted technical assistance and training programs. In particular, for MIS
and FIAS, the participating regions will assist in organizing the collection of market information;
development of fanner information; information dissemination arrangements; for security of computer
hardware, software and other materials provided by the project. For the seed component, the
participating governments will provide administrative support and facilities to strengthen seed inspection
laboratories; and varietal testing farms and stations.
18.   The participating regions must also agree to ensure the sustainability of the MIS, FIAS and the
institutional aspects of the seed component: (i) share the cost of establishing necessary offices and
facilities by providing in-kind support; (ii) provide budgetary support to operating these offices and
facilities beyond the life of the project; and (iii) use the revenues generated through cost recovery
measures implemented in the project for the maintenance and operations of these offices and facilities.

RUsSIAN 1FEDERATION
AGRICULTURAL REFORM IMPLEMENTATION SUPPORT (ARS) PROJECT
SUPERVISION PLAN AND SCHEDULE
INTRODUCIION
1.    This project involves three distinct components with participation of different institutions and
enterprises at dispersed locations. Timely implementation and adherence to agreed project objectives and
activities would require an effective supervision plan.
PDU SUPERVISION
2.    The PDU is expected to play an important role in project supervision, since it is piLimarily
responsible for coordinating all the work related to implementation, budgeting, proct)rement,
osbursement, audit, reporting, monitoring and evaluation and appraisal of all sub-projects. It will prepare
bi-monthly supervision reports to be submitted to the World Bank for review. The component specific
project implementation uiiits in MOAF and local governments would be responsible for the day to day
implementation of the project.
BANK SUPERVISION
3.    Supervision Schedule The proposed Bank supervision plan and supervision schedule for the
project are sumnarized in Annex Table 15.1.
4.    Project Launch Workshop The main objective of the project launch workshop is to re-enforce
project implementation strategies of the various components. This would be carried out by holding a
project launch workshop in Moscow in July 1994. It will bring together various representatives from
institutions and enterprises responsible for implementation of each component. The main topics to be
covered would be: (a) detailed description of each component objectives, sub-components and detailed
features; (b) implementation schedule; (c) mechanism to obtain project funds, (d) procurement
procedures; (e) loan conditions and covenants; (f) PDU and Bank supervisory scope and content and (g)
monitoring and evaluation indicators.
5.    Supervision Missions Periodic Bank supervisions will be undertaken by the i.Bnk staff who have
been involve I with the identification, preparation and appraisal of individual components in order to
ensure conunuity. They will be assisted by appropriate technical experts. The supervisions will be
supplemented by follow up activities to be undertaken by the Agricultural Unit at the resident mission.
Local consultants will also ta used to supplement supervision efforts on a continuing basis. Although this
may likely result in higher than Bank average supervision coefficients at least during the first two years
of project implementation, the emphasis would be placed on combining supervision missions with other
Bank related activities to ensure cost-effectiveness and minimize manpower requirements.



186                                ANNEX 1S
Page 2 of 4
6.    The supervision missions will follow established Bank guidelines (Operational Directive No.
13.05). Thus, the Bank supervisions would focus on the following key indices:
(i)   implementation delays, cost overruns and disbursements;
(ii)  special account operations;
(ii)  audits and accounts;
(iv)  compliance with project development objectives, legal  covenants, project
management performance, availability of counterpart funds;
(iv)  compliance with procurement muidelines;
(v)   training and technical assistance;
(vi)  environmental aspects; and
(vii)  compliance with technical standards, sub-project management, financial health
of the private sector seed and marketing enterprises and management of
individual components.
7.    The implementation plan presented in Annex 14 will provide the basis for Bank supervisions,
which will be reviewed during negotiations.
8.    Mid-Team Review  A mid-term assessment of tht project status, design, objectives, detailed
fares, organization and management, expected benefits in relation to costs and implementation will be
caried out during the first quarter of 1997. The purpose of this assessment is to determine what has
worked and what hasn't, constraints to channelling funds, delivery of technical assistance and training,
Implementation bottlenecks, technical, organization and management aspects. Following this mid-term
review, appropriate changes, as required, both in scope and size, will be made to ensure timely
completion of the project and achievement of project objectives.
9.    Project Completion Report A project completion report will be prepared at the close of the
project in accordance with standard Bank guidelines.
