RUssIAN FEDERATION
AGRICULTURAL REFORM hMPLmNENTATI  SUPPORT (AIS) PROJECT
ECONOmiC AND FiNANCIAL ANALYSIS
1.    This annex provides the main assumptions of the economic and financial analysis of sub-project
investments for hybrid corn and sunflower seed enterprises, fruit and vegetable bases; and cold storage
facilities for fruits and vegetables.
ASSUMPTIONS FOR ECONOMIC ANALYSIS
2.    The following are the main assumptions for economic analysis:
General: (i) All calculations have been carried out in constant November 1993 US dollars, using
the G-5 Manufacturing Unit Value Indices for conversion from current price projections to constant 1993
values. (ii) All tradeable inputs, such as fuel, gas, chemicals and imported inputs have been priced at the
import parity ex-fictory gate prices which have been derived from the prevailing and projected world
market prices. Non-tradeable goods, of which labor (skilled and unskilled) is the most important, has
been shadow priced using a factor of 0.5, which represents its opportunity cost in terms of production
foregone in alternate activities. (iii) Analysis covers 15 years of project operation.
Seed enterprises: (i) The price projections of internationally traded commodities, viz., feed
quality corn, U.S. No. 2 Yellow have been used to derive the economic prices for corn seed prices.
Soybean prices have been used as proxy to derive sunflower seed prices. These prices were provided by
the Interational Economics Department of the World Bank. (ii) Seed to grain price ratios for hybrid
corn and sunflower seed have ranged between 14 to 16 in the past. For the purposes of this analysis, the
lower range of seed to grain price ratio of 14 has been used.
Marketing enterprises: (i) The financial models have been used as the basis for the derivation
of economic rates of return on the representative fruit and vegetable bases and cold storage facilities.
(ii) Economic prices for fruits and vegetables used in the analysis are import parity prices, as without the
project, the produce would have to be imported from neighboring FSU countries or East European
countries. (iii) The general assumptions stated above have been applied for valuing tradables. Non-
tradables have been valued using opportunity cost principles.
ASSUMPTIONS FOR FINANCIAL ANALYSIS
3.    The following are the main assumptions for financial analysis:
(i) All input and output prices, costs of equipment and materials are as of November 1993 obtained
during appraisal. (ii) Assumptions related to taxes, duties, depreciation, financing and sales are reported



190                                 ANNEX 16
Page 2 of 19
in the feasibility studies. (iii) All local costs have been expressed to US doallars. (iv) All calculations
have been carried out in constant November 1993 US dollars.
PLoT INvEmENT iN SEED ENTERPRLsEs
4.    Production of the hybrid corn and sun; ver seeds are expected to comwrence soon after the
establishment of the plants, which would take about one year for construction. The engineering design,
production costs and other operating details were prepared by Giproselkhozzemo, wihirh has several years
of experience in the seed industry. These were reviewed by the World Bank mission during the appraisal.
5.    The rr-in sources of revenue in the case of seed enterprises will be from the sale of hybrid corn
and sunflower seeds to state fanns, collective farms and individual private farmers. The seed prices
assumed for the financial analysis would cover raw seed procurement price, other production costs,
processing and storage costs, quality control costs, market promotion and marketing costs, costs of re-
processing, revalidation of carry over stocks, management overheads, debt servicing, depreciation, sales
and delivery costs, product development costs, such as varietal testing and taxes.
6.    The assurance of timely and reliable delivery of these seeds to the users, especially to those who
are located in close proximity to the seed plants, would ensure a steady market. Moreover, given their
lower production costs, the seeds produced by the project financed corn and sunflower plants would
compete with imported seeds. In the light of comparative advantage and continued demand for high
quality hybrid corn and sunflower seeds, the enterprises are expected to remain finncially viable and be
credit worthy for the life of the loan.
Piwr JNVEsTmENTs IN FRUIT AND VEGETABLE BAsES
7.    The main focus of these sub-projects will be to demonstrate how fruit and vegetable bases could
begin to function as cash and carry and mini-wholesale markets and distribution centers, and gradually
reduce their past function under the centrally planned system as the city government's long-term storage
and distributors of fruits and vegetables.
8.    The mini-wholesale markets will operate under well-defined guidelines providing for cost
recovery, and allow producers/traders and retailers to operate in a competitive environment. Existing
buildings and facilities at the bases are physically worn out, and even if they were to continue their
existing functions, massive investments would be needed to demolish and replace these, which is not
feasible in the short-to medium-term. As the market economy deepens, the bases would restructure their
overall operations focussing more on short-term storage and distribution in a wide range of commodities,
exploring at the same time, how the valuable urban land currently held by them could be more prof.tably
used. The proposed sub-projects will demonstrate this conversion aspect of the industry while broadening
the product range available to urban consumers; improving product quality; reducing post-harvest and
transportation losses; and improving producer incentives by allowing them to participate directly in the
marketing chain.



191                                  AN-NEX 16
Page 3 of 19
Pnr INvEsFmENTS IN CoLw STORAGE FACILITIES
9.    The proposed cold storage facilities for vegetables and potato in Moscow Oblast will assist
participating farms to substantially reduce transportation and storage losses; restructure existing
production practices to increase productivity, product quality and its ability to be stored; and producer
incentives.
10.   The project will provide incremental working capital to introduce new varieties suitable for long-
term storage, improved crop Iusbandry and post-harvest losses and demonstrate economically efficient
strategies for vegetable production, storage and marketing in the outskirts of major consumption centers.
The benefits will include improved prices to producers, based on storage of larger quantities of products
for longer periods compared to the situation without this investment, and will help reduce substantial post-
harvest losses. The financial analysis captures these benefits.
11.   The project will provide incremental working capital to participating enterprises to irtroduce
improved production and storage technologies, which the farms are expected to use to bring about
production efficiencies to reduce the per unit production cost, improve product quality; and increase
marketing margins. These improvements will potentially improve the financial rates of return by about
5-10 percent. These benefits are not currently reckoned in the financial analysis though the costs have
been included. The financial rates of return are particularly sensitive to product prices and marketing
margins and, therefore, project will require, as a condition of sub-loan approval, that enterprises
undertake to introduce mouern production and storage technologies; pay attention to bring about
efficiencies in vegetable and fruit production; and ensure optimal storage capacity utilization using energy
efficient practices. The project will provide necessary technical assistance to enterprises.
SummARY OF PRELIMNARY RESULTS
12.   The preliminary results of economic and financial analysis for seed and marketing enterprises are
summarized in the following tables.
(i) Comparative analysis of hybrid corn seed production costs (Annex Table 16.1)
(ii) Derivation of economic prices for seeds (Annex Table 16.2 and Annex Table 16.3)
(iii) Economic analysis for seed enterprises (Annex Table 16.4 and Annex Table 16.5)
(iv) Sensitivity analysis for economic rate of return (Annex Table 16.6)
(v) Financial analysis for seed enterprises (Annex Table 16.7 and Annex Table 16.8)
(vi) Estimated crop budgets (Annex Table 16.9 to 16.13)
(vii) Financial and economic analysis of marketing enterprises (Annex Table 16.14 to 16.16)

RUSSIAN FEDERATiON
AGRICULTURAL REFORM IMPLEMENTATION SUPPORT (ARIS) PROJECT
ANALYSIS OF REPRESENTATVE PARTICIATING ENTERPRISES
Moscow CffY FRuff AND VEGET rABLE BASES
1.    The two fruit and vegetable bases - the Sverdlovskaya and Perovskaya - which are likely to
r_ceive project assistance are part of the Moscow City's massive procurement and distribution system
consisting of 25 such bases. Under the ongoing privatization program, most of these bases have been
privatized. The Perovskaya has completed the privatization process while privatization of Sverdlovskoye
is und.frway.
Marketing Reforms
2.    Until 1991, tue Moscow City's fruit and vegetable bases worked under directions issued by the
City Government's Fruit and Vegetable Committee. In particular, the Committee directed all the bases,
as to how much quantities of the five main (often referred as political) commodities - potatoes, cabbage,
onions, carrots and red beets - must be received, stored, and distributed, and the prices at which these
commodities must be bought and sold. Consistrmt with the then prevailing practices of the command
economy, the City Government subsidized losses incurred by the bases.
3.    With the liberalization of fruit and vegetable marketing in 1992, the City Government did reduce
the magnitude of its interventions in the vegetable market. The bases' mandatory storage of the five
commodities during the past three years has decreased by about 40-50%, as also the subsidies provided
by the City Government. As for other commodities, the bases now enjoy almost total autonomy in
transacting business. The Moscow City Government's new policy is to further reduce the bases'
contrctual obligations concerning the five main commodities and related subsidies which should normally
be absorbed by the entire commercial food chain and the consumer. This process is however constrained
by the lack of adequate and efficient marketing infrastructure and the banking system that will provide
worldng capital and investment funds.
Markedng Operations.
4.    Currently, both producers and bases incur substantial losses as the products are shipped,
immediately after the harvest, without cleaning, sorting and grading. The storage facilities at the farin
level are inadequate and outdated. The emerging marketing strategy for perishables for major
consumption centers such as Moscow City focuses on shifting long-term storage to production centers.
In future, the bases will therefore increasingly undertake short-term storage; devote larger capacities to
high-value products; perform the role of a distribution center; and offer better quality products to
consumers at competitive prices.



209                                  ANNEX 17
Page 2 of 10
5.    Most bases have already begun to reduce long-term  storage and diversify the range of
commodities handled, focussing of storage of high value products, leasing of short-and long-term storage
capacity to commercial enterprises, improving quality and presentation of goods, offering full-service
storage and marketing facilities, including stock management, inventory control, grading, packaging,
training of workers and construction of market facilities. Leasing operations and trading, outside the five
main vegetables, are the main sources of the bases' income.
Financial Condition.
6.    While the bases are going through a rapid transition in terms of ownership, management and
functions, they also face serious financial stringency, exacerbated by high rates of inflation which rapidly
reduce the real value of balance sheet items, including equity, receivables, payables and the bank loans.
The collection rates for receivables, in respect of products sold on credit to retail stores, are particularly
low. As a result, the bases often default on repayment of bank loans and other creditors. To reverse this
trend, the suppliers are . -ginning to demand advance payments from the bases, while the bases
increasingly want to resort to wholesale and cash and carry marketing rather that providing products to
retailers on credit.
7.    The size, characteristics, and age of the buildings of the bases are such that they cannot
competitively meet the demands of modern urban storage. The bases are heavily built and are not
particularly well laid out for easy movement of goods. The buildings and equipment have been used for
many years - while they are structurally sound, they are badly run-down.
8.    It is for this reason, the proposed project would not renovate old buildings which must be
demolished but instead introduce new marketing concepts such as wholesale and cash-and-carry
marketing, by developing infrastructure on lands owned by the bases and ensure that the cost is amortized
over a period of 7-10 years.
9.    In the prevailing inflationary environment, the financial condition of the two bases, Sverdlovskaya
and Perovskaya, is difficult to assess accurately given that the value of current assets and liabilities is fast
depreciating in real tenns. The bases, however, have large fixed assets (especially land and buildings)
at prime locations, which will continue to provide sizeable incomes, especially through leasing of cold
storage capacities to commercial users and trading in high value commodities.
10.    A summary of the balance sheets and income and expense account of the two bases is presented
in Annex Table 17.1. All these bases handle a substantial volume of business but their profits on trading
in the five main vegetables are relatively low; one base in fact had a negative margin in the first three
quarters of 1993 though the base is hopeful of reversing this situation in 1994 through more efficient
purchase, sales strategy and stock management. The bulk of the profit comes from non-operational
income the sustainability of which has not been proven. Clearly, the bases must focus on making the
operations in five main commodities more efficient and diversify to include high-value commodities. The
pilot projects are expected to assist the bases to achieve this objective.

11.   Since in the current inflationary environment, the income and expense accounts and the balance
sheets are not very reliable to determine the bases' eligibility for project loans, the bases will be required
to maintain separate accounts for project-assisted investments and a separate bank account to ensure that
receipts and payments are kept distinct from the bases' normal operations. The bases will ensure that the
income from project-assisted investments will not be used for cross-subsidizing the bases' non-project
operations. Sub-loan terms and conditions will include these aspects. The bases participating in the
project will mortgage land, project-assisted buildings and fixed assets, accompanied by a general charge
on all assets of the bases. The current assets for these bases are as follows: the Sverdlovskaya: land,13
ha, storage 36,200 tons (of which cold storage 12,200 tons); and Perovskaya: land 8.2 ha, storage:



211                                ANNEX 17
Page 4 of 10
63,300 tons (of which cold storage: 58,000 tons). Project sub-loans will be subject to agreed terms and
conditions. The two bases will have to raise down payment (at least up to 25 percent of the sub-project
cost) which they intend to do by asldng the prospective users of wholesale and cash and carry markets
to contribute and from renting of cold storage facilities to commercial users. These arrangements must
be firmed up before a sub-loan is approved. Approval of individual sub-loans will be subject to the
preparation of individual feasibility studies and the bases' agreeing to the above arrangements.
Moscow OBLAsT VEGETABLE SToRAGE AND ASSEMBLY FACLTEs
12.   The Moscow Oblast produces about 695,000 tons of vegetables (7% of Russia's total production)
and 1.2 million tons of potato (3% of Russia's total production). About 55,000 ha of land is under potato
cultivation and 16,500 ha under vegetables with 9000 ha under cabbage. In 1992, about 434,000 tons
of vegetables and 1.4 million tons of potato were shipped out of Moscow Oblast, with Moscow City
receiving about 34,004) tons of vegetables and 80, 000 tons of potato. While the Moscow Oblast accounts
for only about 23 % of the total quantities of potato sold through the fruit and vegetables bases in Moscow
City, the vegetables account for as high as 66% with cabbage accounting for 98%. The Moscow Oblast
thus plays an important role in supplying potatoes and vegetables to Moscow City.
13.   In Moscow Oblast, large scale producers (former state and collective farms) account for about
61% of the total area under vegetable and 36% of the total area under potatoes. However, during the
past three years, the area cultivated by large producers for potato and vegetables is declining in response
to competition offered by increasing numbers of private farms and household plots which produce mainly
potatoes and vegetables. This trend is forcing large producers to become more competitive in both
production and marketing of potato and vegetables given that they are now privatized (joint stock
companies) and have more economic freedom and incentives following the liberalization of prices, and
marketing reforms.
14.   The prevailing practice of moving large quantities of perishable produce through the winter into
Moscow City is uneconomical and is not likely to sustain as new storage capacities are built at the farm
level. Larger producers and the private farms increasingly want to hold products at the farm for securing
higher off-season prices and retailers directly, instead of through monopolistic fruit and vegetable bases
in Moscow City. This is forcing the bases to become more competitive and diversify.
15.   Existing storage capacities in Moscow Oblast are sizeable but inefficient involving considerable
losses. They do not reflect the real technical possibilities to accept and store produce during the entire
fall-spring period. The storage capacities at the five farms - Podmoskovnoye, Sergievskoye, Ozera,
Bunyatino and Kulikovsky - which are likely to participate in the project have only 5 to 40% of the
actual storage requirements. The feasibility studies of two typical farms - Bunyatino and Ozera-
indicated the following profile. of these farms and selected financial indicators are reported in Annex
Table 17.2.

Bunyatio Farm
16.   The total area of Bunyatino farm is about 1,300 ha, of which 800 ha land is used for potato and
vegetable production. The average annual vegetable production is about 12000 tons but the production
potential can be increased to 25,000 tons. Main vegetables produced are cabbage (5000 tons) and table
beet (1200 tons). There is a primitive storage facility for seed potato for 500 tons and a vegotable cold
storage with a capacity of 2000 tons. The farm vas privatized in 1992 with an authorized capital of
Rubles 14 million. The total number of staff working on the farm is about 475. The pensioners depending
on the farm are 111. The farm can undertake all kinds of farn production, processing and marketing
activities. It is managed by a director, assisted by three deputy directors, and technical experts in
construction, engineering, agronomy, livestock and social affairs. The farm eamed a net income of over
Rubles 209 million in 1992 (according to Russian accounting practices).
Ozera Farm
17.   The Ozera farm is organized on a basis similar to Bunyatino farm. The total area of Ozera farm
is about 3181 ha of which 445 ha land is used for potato and vegetable production. The average annual
vegetable production is about 19000 tons comprising of 12,500 tons of cabbage,.2400 tons of carrots,
900 tons of red beets, and 300 tons of other green vegetables. The major constraint to improving farm
productivity is the lack of storage capacity on the farm. Currently, there is a primitive stock of 1,000 tons
and two vegetable stores with a capacity of 400 tons each constructed in late 1950s. The farm is a close
type of joint stock company founded under the 1990 law on enterprises and entrepreneurship. The total
number of members working on the farm is about 640. The farm can undertake all kinds of farm
production, processing and marketing activities. It is managed by a director, assisted by three directors,



213                                 ANNEX 17
Page 6 of 10
and technical experts in construction, engineering, agronomy, livestock and social affairs. The farm
earned a net income of over Rubles 154 million in 1992 (according to Russian accounting practices).
18.   The sub-loans for pilot projects will be subject to similar terms and conditions. The mortgage
of land and other fixed assets and the farms' commitment to efficiently manage the proposed cold storage
facilities and carry out productivity improvement plans will be the basis for sub-loan approval. The farms
participating in the project will contribute to sub-project costs by way of labor and materials for the civil
work; raising contributions from the prospective users of cold storage facilities; equity contributions by
members; and possibly local government contributions. The project-assisted cold storage facilities will
be accessible to private farms.
KUBANGIBRID (BARI) SEEDS COMPANY
19.   Kubangibrid seed company, which is expected to receive a project sub-loan for corn seed
production facility, is located in Gulkevichi Rayon of Krasnodar Kray. Its main activity is seed
production. It provides technical guidance on corn production to the farms in Krasnodar Kray, Rostov
and Stavropol Oblasts. I. also specializes in the production of corn parent lines for hybrid seeds
production.
20.   The company was privatized and registered as joint stock company in 1992. Private individuals
(23) hold 69.7% of the shares while 23% are contributed by group of three joint stock companies. The
balance of 7.3% of the share is held by an experimental station of RAAS. This seed company bought
an old Roskhleboprodukt (RKP) processing facility in 1992. It has renovated this facility with Russian
equipments to process about 1500 tons seed per year.
Ownership and Assets
21.   The company owns 13 ha of land and office buildings at Tikhoretzk. The Company leases 190
ha at village Kazanskaya for parent seed multiplication. Further, the company acquired, on 35 year lease,
a plot of 6 ha at village Ladozhskaya of Ustlobinskaya Rayon, which will be the site for establishing the
proposed seed processing facility. The company is constructing a seed store of 25, 000 tons capacity
at this site. It owns four harvesters, 5 trucks, 18 small transport vehicles, 2 cars, 1 truck crane, 5
tractors, and 3 seed-cleaning and grading equipments. The current (October, 1993) assets of the company
are valued at Rubles 523 millions. The proposed project site has all the utilities such as power, gas,
water supplies, drainage and sewerage. About 16,000 tons of raw hybrid corn seeds are produced within
a radius of 30 km of the proposed location of the unit.
Company Management
22.   The shareholder's general body is a principal decision making body and elects three members of
the Board of Directors, one each to represent the three groups of share holders. The Board functions as
an executive body of the company. It holds office for two years. The general body appoints the
Managing Director of the company. Board delegates part of its powers to the Managing Director as an
executive head of the company. Board of Directors convene Board meeting as and when necessary, but
at least once a month. The company publishes a well-documented statement of the results of the
performance, disclosing the interests of the company, which is passed by the general body meeting, for
circulation to public.



214                                 ANNEX 17
Page 7 of 10
Administrative Structure and Management
23.   The Managing Director, Deputy General Directors, Seed Processing Director, and Chief
Economist have considerable managerial experience. The Managing Director has twenty years of
experience in seed production management. The Director (Sales) has fourteen years of experience. Seed
Production Director (Chief Agronomist) has been a manager of seed production farm for 10 years.
Senior Book Keeper is a gradw.e with ten years' experience in book keeping and accounts management.
The seed production unit of the company has qualified agronomists, and plant breeders. They are trained
in seed technology and seed production management skills.
Traing Program
24.   The company does not have on its roll marketing experts with good qualification in marketing
management. The company wants to organize regular training programs to upgrade the technical and
managerial skills at all levels. The company realizes the need for the training in special cost accounting,
computerized data management and general management in production and processing. The managers
at higher level shall be exposed to the management skldls abroad. Special training is planned to
strengthen the marketing section of the company.
Sources of Parent Seeds
25.   Corn hybrid seed parent lines are obtained from the following Research Institutes:
o Yugo-Vostok Research Institute, Srednevolzhskoe NPO, Saratov Region;
e Elita Povolzhia Research Institute, Bezenchuk, Saniara Region;
o Kinelskaya Variety Testing Station, Samara Region;
o VIR Experimental Station in Kuban, Botanical, Gulkevich Rayon, Krasnodar
26.   The company gives 1.0 to 2.5% of the profit to the research institutes for the technical service
and parent seeds provided to the Company over and above the parent seed costs. During 1992, this
profit share provided to the Institute was Ruble 13 million.

28.    Because of its good quality, farmers prefer to buy the seed produced by the company even
without the govermnent subsidy. Corn is mainly grown (90%) for the green fodder (silage). The
percentage of investment toward seeds, for the commercial corn grain or silage production, constitutes
hardly six to eight percent. The price of F1 seed however, is higher i.e. 14 to 15 times the normal
grains. The price of parent seeds for Fl seeds production is four to five times (depending upon the
variety and the maturity period) that of Ft hybrid seeds.
29.   The pre-basic and basic seeds of the corn hybrid are produced at present by the Russian Crop
Research Institutes of RAAS, their affiliated Regional Research Farms in the experimental stations. First
generation (Fl) seeds are produced in kolkhozes, sovkhozes and private farmers. Production target for
the first stage of the project is 5,000 tons of processed seeds annually. Area under production is more
than 3000 ha. An area of about 2500 to 3000 ha is essential for the dependable production of 5000 tons
of processed hybrid corn seeds. Company is proposing to expand its activity to produce 10,000 tons of
processed seed in the second stage.
Quality Control
30.   Seed quality testing will be carried out by the trained staff. Manager-Seeds Production supervises
the area of 2.5 to 3.0 thousand ha. Company sends the seed samples to the state-run seed inspection
system for testing The seed inspectorate issues certificate cn seed vigor, purity, germination and other
a characteristics of seed processed at the facility. The fee paid for the services rendered by state-run
inspections is according to the norm fixed by the seed inspectorate from time to time. The conpany
however conducts field inspection regularly. It inspects the field and guides the growers on:
e     selection of land, land preparation and isolation of plots;
Q     receipt of parent seeds, planting methods, protection through training;
°      sowing operation and weed control;
°     provide package of cultivation practices;



216                                ANNEX 17
Page 9 of 10
°     field purity maintenance and technical supervision for off plant identification  and
removal;
O     supervision of parent line and determination of biological crop maturity and yield; and
o     harvesting and finalizing delivery schedule to processing center.
31.   License is needed to market seeds outside the oblast. The license is issued by oblast/kray
Department of Agriculture. The Government provides varietal testing and seed testing for the quality
control support.
Packaging and Labeling
32.   The company's seeds are proposed to be packed in four-layered paper bags to ward off moisture
and to improve the quality of seeds during storage and the presentation of the compaiiy's logo and
packaging style of its own identity. Each bag will contain 25 kg of seed and will be labelled w;ih. the
following information printed on:
*      Company's Trade Mark
°      Name of the hybrid seed variety
0      Status of germination percentage and the generation
o      Year of harvest
o      Name of the seed treatment chemical used
0      Warning about the human toxicity and nature of poison
*      Lot number of the seed grower
o      Shift number and date Processing
*      Fraction specification and grade.
Seed Marketing Plan
33.   The seed demand in these oblasts and krays is of the order of 170,000 tons. The company is
planning to produce 50% of its total seed production with shorter duration varieties of FA 160 - 300 and
the balance 50% with longer maturity varieties of FAO 400 -600. The FAO 160 - 300 will be to meet
the demand of the Central Russia and Siberia market and the FAO 400 - 600 is to meet the market
demand of North Caucasus region. The company will produce these varieties of seeds on contract basis.
34.   Hybrid seeds of different maturity groups are proposed to be produced, processed, packed, stored
and marketed. Since the market system is not well developed and the supply is less than the demand
there is no problem with retail sales at present. Large collective and state farms are the main clients of
the company. The distribution of the seeds at present is regulated by the oblast government; however
the company uses the government channel for distribution of its product. The company uses the
government departmnent to disseminate the information about the company seeds to the clients. However
the company has direct contact with major large collective famns and etnters into contract for seed supply.
As the production increases, the company is planning to set up its own independent market network of
retailers working directly with the company.



217                                 ANNEX 17
Page 10 of 10
Projected Market Share
35.   The company organizes corn grain production and corn cultivation in the following area:
°      40,000 to 45,000 ha in Krasnodar Kray;
0      20,000 to 25,000 ha in Rostov Oblast; and
O      4,000 to 5,000 ha in Stavropol Kray
36.   About 150,000 tons of com grain is produced in Krasnodar alone. When the Kubangibrid facility
is established it will produce 5,000 tons of seed in Ladhovskaya and it will also continue producing 5,000
tons in Tikhoretzk. Thus in all it will be producing 10,000 tons of seeds.
Capital Investment
37.   The proposed seed processing facility requires modern processing and calibration and seed
treating equipments which has to be imported from abroad. The company entrusted the work of
preparing feasibility studies to Giproselkhoszerno (GSZ). The institute has qualified Civil, Mechanical
and Design Engineers with substantial experience in the area.
38.   It is also essential for the Company to have good quality combines to quickly harvest the crops
on time to maintain high quality standard of hybrid seeds. It is also necessary to have de-tasseling
equipments and vehicles for quickly transporting the fresh ears immediately after harvest to the processing
units. Provisions for all the modern equipments and machineries are made in the project. The proposed
processing unit design is contemplated to have good dust control system to protect from any possible fire
accidents and to maintain high level of hygiene condition in the seed unit for the safety of workers. These
dust control systems are to be installed in the shelling, cleaning and grading sub-units.
39.   Foreign exchange will be used to finance the imported seed processing equipment, whereas the
finance charges, the civil works, the working capital, and some of the local equipment will be in ruble
expenses. Seed processing equipment is exempt of import duties as per: (i) the law of Russia's
Federation "About customs tariff" clause 35 item (K); and (ii) the Order of State Customs Committee
of Russia's Federation No: 228 from June 11, 1993.
